The Red Page
Frederick McCubbin
Frederick McCubbin, doyen of Australian
artists, is now in his sixties. He was born
in West Melbourne in 1855. His father, a
baker, took him from school at 14, and
placed him in a solicitor’s office. He stayed
there six months; and his disgusted father,
who had, as he thought, given him a good
start in life, decided that the lad should do
some real work. He placed him as a driver
of one of his bread carts. However, these
things mattered little, since McCubbin had
determined to become an artist. But he had
not the least idea how to set about achiev-
ing his ambition. One day he found his
way to a night school at which drawing
was taught, though only in its application
to some trade-work. He had merely to copy
designs, etc. Then, by an accidental sight
of a fine copy of Titian’s “Flora,” a new
world of color dawned upon the hoy's
amazed vision. His father, dissatisfied at
Frederick’s lack of genius in driving the
baker’s cart, had a happy baker's thought.
If the boy would muddle with paint, why
not let him be a coach painter? Without
being consulted, the boy thereupou found
himself apprenticed to a firm of coach
painters. It is possible that somewhere in
Melbourne now there is a cart upon which
there could still be seen the faded remains
of some masterpiece of design and color
from the hand of Frederick McCubbin, the
only old master in the coach-painting genre.
Then a school-class for drawing was
started at the National Gallery, and the
coach-painter eagerly joined it. But he was
soon disillusioned. The lad wanted to draw
and to paint, and nobody seemed to be able
to teach him : the students were left entirely
to themselves. The drawing-class was sepa-
rated from the painting-class. But McCub-
bin bought a box of paints and set out
sketching on Saturday afternoons. By
1875 he was able to set up on his own
account doing piece-work for builders. By
working three days a week he managed to
support himself: the other days were free
for his art. With great anticipations he
joined the painting-class at the Gallery.
But herp he found, too. that the only thing
that was not taught was the way to paint.
The young ladies who composed the majo-
rity of the class were set to work copying
some picture in the Gallery, and when they
had finished that they started on another
one. Their copies were subjected to a
Vague, mostly complimentary, criticism from
the master; but there was no attempt made
to teach them to paint some simple natural
object. Probably that was the last thing
these damsels wanted. McCubbin was in
despair. It is hard for the modern art stu-
dent to imagine the plight of a youth who
wanted to learn to paint and couldn’t find
anybody to teach him. Luckily some animal
studies that he had made from life he sub-
mitted to Julian Ashton, then editing The
Australian Journal. Ashton bought some
of his black and white and encouraged him,
as he has encouraged so many of our Aus-
tralian artists, to persevere. Julian Ashton
is the prince of Australian art: he kissed
the sleeping beauty of the continent, and
she woke.
In 1576 his father died, and back the son
had to go to the baking trade to save the
business from collapse. However, the artist
was also a good baker, and under his man-
agement the business became so successful
that soon he was able to spare himself a
day or two a week for his real work. And
he found that with the advent of Mr.
Folingsbee to the Gallery the art school had
been reorganised. At last McCubbin had
found someone to show him the way he
would go. But even Folingsbee could take
him but a little distance along his unexplored
path. The teacher would not admit that
landscape should or could be painted in the
open air! But already his students had
broken away from that terrible tradition:
and Tom Roberts returned from England
with his new gospel of “relative values.”
With various other art students who subse-
quently made a name for themselves.
McCubbin set forth to paint Australia
under the Australian sky. instead of shut-
ting the continent up within a dusty studio.
And, finally, in 1884. the self-taught McCub-
bin was appointed drawing-master at the
Gallery. The rest is a long life devoted to
the painting of Australia, a long life of
achievement and slowly broadening reputa-
tion.
A concrete acknowledgment of this fact
now appears in The Art of Frederick
}fcCubbin. containing 45 illustrations in
color and black and white, published by
Lothian, Melbourne. The edition is limited
to 1000 copies, and the price, to non-sub-
scribers, is £3 3s. The publisher is to be
congratulated on the appearance of this fine
tribute to Australian art, since the book has
been printed and bound in Australia. The
cover is crude and garish, but the repro-
ductions, both in color and in black and
white, are brilliant and admirably inter-
pretative. All of the letterpress could have
been spared except the interesting bio-
graphy. The essay by James MacDonald,
and the anonymous introduction are merely
large words in nice fat type.
McCubbin is an artist Australian-born,
who survived it. All the other Australian-
born artists had to get out or get under.
McCubbin developed his art within the
Commonwealth: it was late in life that he
was able to visit the art centres of the
world: and that tour made little difference
te his outlook. McCubbin’s achievement is
a fine but not a world-shaking one. He is a
sincere painter of a limited number of
aspects of Australia. In his early woTk
every picture tells a story, and in his later
work he cannot altogether shake off the
narrative element ingrained in him by his
early environment and his lack of the right
artistic leading. It is not Australia that
McCubbin paints, but “bits" of Australia —
sincerely painted “bits” of beauty, distin-
Edward Lee Masters, the American “Belle-
rive,” author of "Spoon River Anthology.”
guished by a high technical skill, a mastery
of values and a delightful sense of color and
atmosphere. He has set down pleasantly
and permanently certain charming localities
in Victoria; he shows ns an Australia that
is full of verdure and old picturesque
fences and old picturesque huts, and happy
backyards with, fowls and children happily
engaged in their separate and important
pursuits. That is one aspect of Australia,
and it is McCubbin’s lasting achievement
that he has out it down for all time thus;
hut his scope is a severely limited one: he
leaves all the other aspects of Australia—
in fact, we may say, all the other Aus-
tralias. equally paintable—out of his can-
vas. Thus the book, itself merely a selec-
tion from his work, has an air of monotony.
It is a beautiful monotony, though. The
portraits—-for even so obvious a landscapist
as McCubbin has to become a portrait-
painter in this commercial Australia of ours
—are obviously not his first-love. They are
skilful, satisfying, probably excellent* por-
traits. and hard. To all these minor criti-
cisms, a mere glance at this lordly volume
will provide exceptions. But to have
produced some six of this array of pictures
is more than sufficient achievement for any
Australian artist’s sixty years of work.
And to this achievement must be added that
other already mentioned: McCubbin was
born an artist in Australia, and survived it.
Herbert Warren, who professes poetry at
Oxford, forwards a full report of his lecture
on Maoriland poetry—“a large and rich
crop. The best collection was Veto Zealand
Verse, by W. F. Alexander and A. E. Currie,
published by Messrs. Scott, in 1906. The
first poem, entitled ‘New Zealand.’ formed
an excellent introduetion. It was the work
of the Hon. Mr. William Pember Reeves, who,
in his Long White Cloud, had also written
an excellent history of the island. It pre-
sented a fair ideal. Other characteristic
and suggestive poems of his were ‘The
Colonist's Garden’ and the very touching
'Passing of the Forest.’ The contrast
between Australia and New Zealand was
well given in the verses entitled ‘Written in
Australia.’ by A. 11. Adams.”
In his sketch of M.L. poetry, he naturally
began with Domett. “A beautiful and
touching poem, suggesting the contrast
between the old and the new. was ‘Onawe,’
by Dora Wilcox.... There were other M.L.
poets whose works were worthv of note,
such as F. W. Broome. 11. Church, W. Law-
son. Mary Colbome Yeel, and Anne Glennie
Wilson.” He concluded with Ernest Currie’s
poem. “Laudabunt Alii.”
With reference to this Page's criticism of
V arren's lecture on Australian poetry, the
Professor writes that underneath the almost
grotesque slang of The Sentimental Bloke,
he recognises a good deal of genius; “but I
can hardly imagine that it will live indefi-
nitely. as it is so much bound up with the
fashion of the time. It will become in 30
years like an old Punch, historic but a back
number.” Professor Warren has also been
much struck with the work of the late
George Essex Evans, who seems to him to
have considerable depth, “and certainlv
wrote some very fine patriotic poems.”
While admitting that Douglas Sladen is not
in the true sense an Australian poet, the
Professor asks us to recognise that the
canse of Australian and M.L. poetry in
England owes a great deal to him —a fact
this page has abundantly admitted. The
Professor states that in his Australian lec-
ture he made considerable use of the work
of Arthur H. Adams. He specially men-
tions “The Australian”; and in his M.L. lec-
ture he read the whole of “Written in Aus-
tralia” to his audience. He is also im-
pressed favorably by Dorothea Mackellar:
A. T. Strong he thinks “a good artist” and
J. Sandes “a very spirited and stirring
versifier.” Finally. Professor Warren is
anxious to keep up to date in our poetic out-
put. The advice already given in this page,
for Australasian bards to forward all their
stuff, if necessary as freight, to Sir Herbert
Warren. Magdalen College, Oxford, is there-
fore sound.
"Bellerice,” the Australian Edward Lee
Masters, author of "Answers to Corres-
pondents" and other Works.
The Choice of Fiction
The hardened fiction-reader stood in the
subscription library thoughtfully choosing a
new novel. For one so hardened she looked
surprisingly flapperish. Being hardened, she
never read the book-reviews: they merely
told her what the critics thought of books,
and what did the critics know of the literary
yearnings of her soul? Besides, you had to
wade through their reviews before you found
out whether you would like the' book. Might
as well read the book. She had an easier
way. N'ot that she had invented it herself.
It was immemorial: since there were best-
sellers women have chosen novels this way.
Taking up each new novel in turn, she
deftly opened it at the first page, delicately
sipped the first paragraph, fluttered swiftly
to the last page, sucked the honey from that,
and knew. Every quotation telis a story—■
the whole story.
Out of the big batch of novels which the
nice young man assured her were all new 7,
she picked one. Zenia: Spy in Togoland. by
Mrs. Charlotte Cameron (Laurie).
Page 1. “Prepare to take first ship to Togoland;
will telephone for appointment this evening.” The
thin telegram slip fluttered from her hand: she
picked it up and read, several times, the blurred
carbon message, her wonderful eyes opening wide
with excitement.
Ha! Togoland? First ship! Her wonder-
ful eyes! Sounds exciting! (She did not
notice how neatly the author had given her
the key to her emotion by the deliberate in-
sertion of that word “excitement.’’)
Page 192. Within an hour Sir Norman and Lady
Trevallion sailed for Swakopmund: he to resume his
official duties until the dust of war had settled ; she
to live her life anew, a life sweetened by a strong
man's love.
Hm! Satisfying. So she wasn't a spy
after all. Sir Norman....a strong man’s
love. Fancy getting both, a strong man’s
love and a baronetcy! Yes, but these spy
stories?. ...
Peter of Potopah, by Lee Holt (Hodder
and Stoughton).
Page 1. The great range of the Rockies stood
coldly grey in the dusk of the coming night. Scarred
warriors, creatures of fierce moods, and gods of old,
they were like immovable spectres in the midst of
a transitory, fugitive world.
IIuli! American. Looks dull and grey.
(The author shouldn’t have used that
word.) All about mountains, too. Turn
over.
Page 311. A month later Peter was pardoned....
Five years later Peter married Betty. Their
farm prospered, and Betty’s dairy became renowned
for miles as a pattern of what a dairy should be.
They have three children....
That all? Three children and a dairy.
Next!
Youth Unconquerable, by Percy Ross
(Heinemann, per George Robertson. Svd-
ney).
Page 1. “And so, I suppose, I am penniless?”
Cherry had been sitting in her room in the second
oldest College for Women in Oxford, like an ordi-
nary girl before this moment; now she rose to meet
a tragedy.
Oxford.... ? Yes. it’s exciting when it’s
about men; but a Women’s College! Pretty
deadly, I should thiuk. All the same, she
isn’t an ordinary girl. Of coarse, she has
a hard struggle, and then she meets him.
Let’s see.
Page 354. Lavender laid her head with the
strangest sigh of comfort upon that enterprising
breast, surprised to feel so at home there; a Dew
and never dreamt content and pride invading her
hitherto selfless spirit. The moon shone and the
stars glittered, and the wind rose and the night
whispered as Menteith, etc. _
That’s funny. Wasn’t she Cherry to be-
gin with, and now she’s Lavender! (She
dipped and dipped in the hopfe of discovering
when and how Cherry turned T

Mrs. Balfame, by Gertrude Atherton
(Murray).
Page 1. Mrs. Balfame had made up her mind to
commit murder.
Page 373. Mrs. Balfame was alone with the crush-
ing burden of her soul.
This looks perfectly sweet. Murder! But
Mrs. Balfame? Why Mrs.? Sounds like
one of the neighbors. A married woman,
probably dowdy. Why not call her by her
Christian name? Rather tired of murder-
stories. And apparently nothing happens.
All she gets is a crushed soul. Xo-o-o?
She made her choice. She picked up—
which? Remember, she is the ordinary
reader of ordinary fiction, unmarried, with
a distinctly suburban home. She either
types in a dull office or helps mother in a
dull home, I forget which. She has had a
polite boy or two, but no strong man’s love,
no domineering personality had hitherto
loomed upon her restricted horizon. Both
were as far out of her reach as a baronet
for a husband or a wedding by a bishop.
Even a prosperous dairy farm, with or with-
out three children, was beyond her vision.
She had never had the emotional satisfac-
tion of suddenly learning that she was pen-
niless ; and the only murdering she had done
had been at the pictures. Fate had not
even allowed her the tragic luxury of a
crushed soul. She was not in love, but quite
ready to be in love, ready even to feel at
home on an enterprising breast. But there
seemed to be a strange lack of enterprising
breasts in her suburb.
Which literary salve or tonic did she
choose, for her urgent feminine emotional
need? It is not necessary to state; the
answer is too easy.

A Frenchwoman’s Notes on the TTar.jby
Claire de Pratz (Constable) has little en-
lightenment to give the reader. She chatters
on pleasantly, without displaying any re-
markable insight, and employing a style not-
ably deficient in vigorous or graphic phrase.
Yet she does give one interesting sugges-
tion why France was.so unready for tfar.
It was because the Republic feared the com-
ing of war. All Republican politicians had
perceived to what extent a possible war
would be dangerous to the Republic as a
form of government. They argned that if
France were to be vanquished, the nation
would consider the Republic as the respon-
sible cause of defeat, and would speedily rise
to overthrow it And if. on the other hand.
France were to be victorious the danger
might be still greater. They feared that a
victorious General might become a military
dictator. Hence, even till now. the press has
carefully abstained from encouraging the
popularity of any particular General.
Special Tribute to Mr. Edmond, late of
Bulletin Office.
Retired from the office
For ease and rest,
An editor and critic
Who was one of the best.
His weekly slafings
Tr ere but a lesson on art.
As The Bulletin chief he
Was true to his part.
Slatings I suffered
But would not despair.
Rough times had taught me
To grin and bear.
I resumed my task
Till I rounded the bend.
And in Mr. Edmond I found
-1 just critic and friend.

12, 16 and 20 Gauge
Light, Hammerless Repeating
Shotguns
Sportsmen differ as to whether a 12, 16 or 20 Gauge is
the best shotgun for field shooting, but the knowing
ones agree that the Winchester Model 1912 lightweight,
hammerless repeater is the one best bet in the shotgun
line. This Winchester being made in 12, 16 and 20
gauges permits an advocate of the big, medium or small
bore to indulge his fancy and be sure of getting a gun
that is true to its gauge in balance, weight and length.
All three gauges of this model are made of specially
selected materials. Nickel steel, which is twice as
' strong as ordinary gun steel, is used throughout for the
metal parts in this Winchester. This means a light-
weight gun without sacrificing safety or strength.
This gun has a cross-bolt trigger lock, a smooth,
quick and easy action, and a simple Take-Down
system. It loads and unloads easily and its shoot-
ing qualities are not excelled by the highest priced
double guns. There are many other pleasing and
exclusive features of this Winchester which appeal to
sportsmen that could be enumerated, but the gun itself
is the best evidence of its ♦ quality and desirability.

A Natural Remedy
Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs/' and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house.

Australia Riddled with
Deadly Kidney Disease
ALMOST EVERYBODY COMPLAINING.
Kidney disease is most certainly on the in-
crease. Thousands and thousands of people
in the towns and villages of this country are
in terrible danger of deadly kidney disease.
This insidious disease creeps on, gradu-
ally getting a firmer and firmer grip, until
when it is too late the poor sufferer discovers
he has Bright’s Disease. Have you kidney
trouble? Do you know how to recognise
whether you are suffering from this or not?
Here are some of the symptoms of kidney
disease : Aches and pains in various parts of
the body, nasty taste in the mouth first thing
in the morning, constipation, cloudy and bad-
colored urine, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, puffiness under the eyes—
these are all symptoms of kidney trouble.
And then people who suffer from backache,
rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Grave.
There is a majesty in carven stone
M ith solemn emblem and emblazoned scroll;
But, in the dull reverberations of the roll
Of slow-swung bells, in lavish blossoms
thrown, -
I find but empty forms. I know a grave
With no pale effigy or wreathed urn,
But one white cross, that passers-by may
learn
The name of him laid there. That he was
brave
Needs no brass capitals in serried rows;
’Tis in the one short legend at his head —
“He fell in action.” And the wild thyme
blows
Where Nature makes a garden for her
dead.
.EGYPT.
GEOFFREY CUMINE.
Knowledge.
Was it you, or the rushing stream, or the
grass rain-splashed,
Or the voice of a bird in the hedge to its
drowsing mate,
The grey of the twilight sky where the
first star flashed,
Or the drone of the freighted bee flying
home so late?
Or the clover blooms in the sparkle of dew-
light sweet.
Or the moon like a silver rose on a field of
stars ? —-
Through the high black trees her white
light fell to my feet,
Making a ladder of brightness with crystal
bars.
And I thought: “My heart is glad for the
evening star,
And the silk-soft world, and the song of
birds in the land”....
With the sob of the waves between, in this
city afar,
I know that the gladness lay in the touch of
your hand.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
The Sun Mote.
Like a sun-mote lost in a darkened house,
you flitted from room to room,
Shivered away from the gnawing mouse,
shuddered from corner gloom,
Halted awhile where the winds carouse
round the stunted yellow broom.
Like a sunbeam piercing a broken tomb.
retreating in sudden fear... .
Had I built a fire in the draughty room, I
might have trapped you, dear —
Scattered the reign of the sullen gloom and
bade you a welcome here.
Had I built a fire of my misty books, of
parchment to feed the flame,
I could have lured you with golden looks and
wheedled you as you came;
But the yawning grates were as black as
rooks, and Joy a forgotten name!
You glanced your light o’er the empty grate,
you wept o’er the icy tiles,
Then turned you round to the open gate
where the broad warm roadway smiles,
And I felt the need of you all too late—and
the call of the sunlit miles!
QUEEN SX.AND.
M. FORREST.
Wandereis.
We wanderers on careless roads,
By reckless fancies led 4 ,.
To bear on devious ways our loads,
And miss the goals ahead
That others, careful of their ways,
By purpose set attain,
Garner a harvest from our days
That they may never gain.
We know the griefs of smiling paths
That into deserts bend ;
The cold and bitter aftermaths
Of hours that we misspend;
Yet those white thoughts, we cherish best
Are culled in wasted hours ;
The bracken of an idle jest
May hide the-fairest flowers.
We ponder not with thoughtful eyes
Our choice of ways to test,
The road that careful souls despise
Proves for our joys the best.
We are content with happy sound
To, take the gain and loss;
Life breathes a thought-air bland and kind
Where tangled footways cross.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Pastel.
A mouth like a rose
That is nourished on wine;
Two lips that disclose
An immaculate line
Of the winsomest teeth ;
Then, just underneath,
A chin —oh, a chin
That is tempting as sin
To a timorous saint.
But if once I begin
Maladroitly to paint
All that poets might sing
Of your beauty, dear thing,
I shall never get through,
And that wouldn’t suit you,
Or I judge you amiss.
Is that pout half a kiss?. ...
I don’t mind if I do!
But your sweetness, my life,
Has a swift poison hid in ’t.. . .
Will I make you my wife?
Would you mind if I didn't?
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, JUNE 15, 1916.
WHICH WAY, LABOR?
Tiieke is no room for difference of opinion
about these things :
1. The cost of living has gone up and the por-
ch sing power of wages has gone down.
2. There has been decreased production and an
enormous increase in importations.
3. The national debt is swelling like a balloon
and the drain of interest is correspondingly increased.
4. Strikes are without parallel in the country’s
history, and are probably more numerous in pro-
portion to population than anywhere else on earth.
5. Taxation is rapidly moving upwards and it is
going to keep on climbing.
What the politicians intend to do about it
all they have not the remotest idea. They
are living from hand-to-mouth, wasting
public cash and groping around for schemes
and devices that may distract the attention
of the community from the more vital
issues. And that is what the matters just
enumerated happen to be: _ they cut right
across the grain of Australian politics, and
can no more be avoided or their consequences
disregarded than the war can be eliminated
from the consideration of contemporary
events. To some extent the ineptitude lately
displayed by the numerous Parliaments in
Australia is the climax of many years of
demoralisation —the reflex of an evil system
of government by which public opinion is dis-
torted and the nation’s own statute books
made instruments of political reaction. To
some further extent the calamities enumer-
ated above are the results of popular igno-
rance; and it is just about time the citizens
of Australia commenced to consider seriously
where thev are getting to, what they want
to achieve, and how they are going to do it.
Also, it is time for the various political
parties which give birth to politicians, to
make up their minds what they are standing
for. the methods they desire to be identified
with, and the name and address of what they
call their objectives. The habit of wildly
cursing the price of meat and strikes and
the Kaiser is merely futile. It settles
nothing. If it did—well, Hughes would
long ago have settled everything.
To frantically yell “Damn!” and fling a
vote into a ballot box is about as effective
as to make the same remark and toss the
domestic cat out of the window. Loud howls
about the cost of living and the prevalence
of strikes are of no assistance to the com-
munity unless accompanied by clear speci-
fications of what reform these enraged P a rtij
sans have to offer. And there are about <Go
good, sound reasons why the Australian
people should be sourly critical on this point
and examine their noliticians and political
“programmes” and “platforms” with a
microscope. This paper has got some very
definite ideas about Australian industry,
but it is not mad enough to suppose that indus-
try can be other than a jest if there is a strike
every five minutes. Also The Bulletin
is not without faith that the cost of living
can be maintained within reasonable bounds
by public authority acting honestly and with
understanding. But it is just as firmly con-
vinced that only additional confusion and
heavier burdens on the Australian family
will follow in the trail of seven vote-hunting
Ministries pretending to grapple with their
problem by, on the one hand, piffling with
price-fixing ordinances in which the differ-
ence is split between 2d, and 21d., and, on
the other hand, piling up the cost of govern-
ment, wringing larger and larger sums from
the common herd by taxation, and squander-
ing millions on wasteful undertakings. How
much further this madness will go is not
hard to determine. It cannot be far. Some-
thing is going to break. The citizen who
contemplates the figures relating to imports
and exports, to loans and interest, to taxa-
tion, strikes, the cost of commodities and
public expenditure, and who fails then to
detect a creaking sound, must be hard of
hearing.
» * * •
There was a time when the people,
alarmed at the prospects, turned from the
muddlers of the Old R6gime and looked for
salvation from organised Labor. To some
extent their trust was justified; to some
extent grossly betrayed. But whatever the
past may have given, to-day nobody knows
what to expect from the movement, because
Labor is talking with two voices. From one
set there comes this definition of the Labor
movement: —
Labor stands for social justice and seeks to achieve
it by constitutional and economic reforms, securing
to the worker the full reward of his labor, and by
enlightened legislation and prudent administration
moving towards the goal of Socialism.
From another group comes this: —
Reforms of existing conditions are no good to us.
What Labor stands for is the sweeping away of the
wages system, the establishment of industry upon a
basis of fraternal co-operation, and the complete
destruction of Capitalism.
If the Australian citizen, with a rapidly
diminishing earning power, involved either
directly or indirectly in strikes that are as
numerous as atrocities in the war zone,
with a growing debt on his shoulders and
before him the certainty of there being more
of it —if the citizen is to understand that
Labor’s curative treatment will consist of
upending wages altogether, and getting
everyone to join hands and go on a fresh
tack, where is he to suppose that that will
land him? With one ear he hears Labor
telling him that its objective is along the
lines of historical prudence, constructive
statesmanship and reform. With the other
he hears that this is no good—that there
is a short cut to something he doesn’t under-
stand, by climbing over the ruins of the
things he has been struggling to improve.
And with his eyes he sees Labor Ministries
continuing to create more embarrassment
for the people in the fond hope that they
will be rewarded with votes and three cheers
for doing it. Taking matters by and large,
it looks a fair thing to suggest that the time
is ripe for Labor to explain whether it is
steering north or south or both ways together.
* * - * *
A political party which with one breath
proclaims Cohen to be the linch-pin of its
policy and the lOU the people’s sword and
buckler against oppression, and with the
next asserts that Capital is a monster which
must be destroyed ; a party which' is vari-
ously described as (1) aiming at evolu-
tionary progress and (2) demanding prompt
revolution; a party at once of order and
disorder, of practical aims and impractic-
able visions—surely such a body is in a
bad way. And a country chained to the
party Joss, where uproarious conflicts
between Ins and Outs obscure the clash
of principles, is in a bad way, too. Aus-
tralia cannot afford to be in this position.
Nor can its trades unionists afford to
see the Labor movement strangled by loan-
mongers and parodied by Labor Ministries,
or reduced to the condition of a pipe
installed for the purpose of assisting the
escape of hot air. To them the profoundest
significance attaches to such questions as
the cost of living, the encouragement of
production, the prevention of waste, the
protection of the wage-earners from the
exactions of boodlers and monopolists and
incidentally from parasites of their own
class. These are the fundamental things,
far transcending such considerations as
whether, regardless of anything else, a cer-
tain coterie of individuals shall be privi-
leged to climb on the shoulders of their
fellows into a well-paid job.
THINKING BY A CREEK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
j trained here from Adelaide through 200
mdes 0 f w heat land, and not many bad acres
1* it all-Hamley Bridge, Balaclava, Brink-
worth, Gladstone, Laura, Booleroo Centre,
and, at last, Wilmington, in the fertile plain
between the ranges of Flinders and the
Horseshoe. And then a couple of hours of
, . . ~ , , , , , ,
afternoon driving through wheat-land and
avenues of haystacks, the horses scrambling
UP clay-slate hills in the Flinders Range, and
down into a cup of slate and sandstone and
feathery-topped gums in Spring Creek—a
mere slice in the black soil of an old flat, rich
as Stilton cheese. A last look at the plain and
the Flinders and the Horseshoe before falling
into the cup; grey gums in the watercourses;
bald hills each with a solitary she-oak on
guard half-way to the sky-line; yellow hills of
the near Flinders, with the green of gums in
the channels; the blue of the distant Flin-
ders, and the purple and red of the Horse-
shoe. Something very human in the shapes
of the sprawl in" foothills—breast and navel,
shoulder and hip, ankle and toe. The deli-
cate light of Central Australia in the late
afternoon: Wilmington now only as rows of
plantation, but, closely seen, beautiful in its
amphitheatre of hills, j'ellow, purple and red,
and the morning-glory vines to splash the
evening with violet. The light and the great
spaces that belonged to my youth ; the white
gums in the streets of Wilmington; and
miles or so away, and starboard of the sun-
set, Port Augusta and Pichi llicln 1 ass. At
foot, the long, wide saucer of the plain, straw-
yellow, and brown in stubble and tallow.
South, the sorrowful acacia and the swamp-
gums drooping like them. W estward, the
cup aforesaid, and a mine at the bottom of it
stranded like a tea-leaf. And then a long,
sliding, slithering progress to the bottom ot
the cup.
I left it all as the sun dipped with
an appearance of haste, and went further
down the cup to the camp on an open
platform of flat stone on a level with
the graceful treetops of the gums m
the creek bed. With the sunset the last
cockatoo shrieked his mingled menace and
apprehension, and the last bird found silence.
Rabbits came out of the burrows in the creek
bed, and began to feed as we began shooting
them. Two fell, but the others came back
again and ate, uninterested in the probability
of death leaping from the camp—greedy as
privilege, and like monarchy in that
they survive the death of the best.
Criminal fools introduced them to Aus-
tralia, and then other criminal fools
introduced new forms of death to destroy, the
original curse. The poison-cart has wiped
out kangaroo, wallaby and Australian birds
and left the raboit flourishing. The doctrine
of the survival of the fittest may be true when
animals are left to fight for survival with
only natural weapons: but let the sneaks of
civilisation or the lightweight scientists enter
the game, and the fittest survives least,
because strength is unsuspicious. With a
grin or with treachery a clown may kill a
SIIAKSPEARE.
Partly because of that poison cart and the
absence of the fleshy thud of wallaby hopping
out to feed, the night closed in to silence;
silence with a starlight that leaves things half
visible and without the mystery and decep-
tion of moonrays. In that light a man may
still see. Yet not if he have the dull eyes
that cannot interpret and remember, but re-
ceives only the fleeting image of things as a
pool receives a passing cloud. The air
divinely soft; the clean trees against the
purity of the sky-line; a night with all the
beauty and the melancholy of youth. I had
wondered for months what it was I wanted,
and here it is—with the ear not yet broken-
in to it, and being broken in with discom-
fort, as a man new to Venice lies awake
listening vainly for the noises of traffic.
Peace moving through quietness and cleanli-
ness to absolute silence.
I did not want even friends —the best of
the friends I’ve summered and wintered with
—desiring so much to be alone that I would,
if I could, have left my body and gone deeper
into the silence, glad not to hear even the
sound of my own pulses. I remember one
such quiet night at Heemskirk, spent riding
across the featherbed of the button grass;
the Cape Sorell light 35 miles away, occult-
ing, momently blazing at my shoulder, and
going back to darkness. The sunset had
ranged the hills of granite for an hour and
glistened to the sand dunes, the sun, flat as
a pancake, dropping into the sea, where came
Tasman in the Zeehan and the Heemskirk
to give a name to two hills. Two hundred
and fifty years thereafter the people of the
coast changed Mt. Heemskirk to Mt. Agnew
in honor of a doctor of Hobart, who lost some
money in a tin-mine that didn’t have enough
tin to go round. How English !
Myrtle forest and button grass were passed
before dark ; later that night I made camp
in silence so deep that imagination might
hear the leeches of the swamp telling tales
of blood letting. That’s the real life for a
man: hard work, and honest weariness, and
a dive in clear water; a night of stars and a
kindly air to blow upon the brow that has
earned contentment with sweat. And silence!
Yet, at Heemskirk, in all that hell of diffi-
culties of development and transport, there
were batteries lying about tiie hills, all the
mountains of granite and button grass pegged
for leases, and “mines” taken up in the surf.
Man is a strange animal. Heaven is quiet-
ness, and Hell is noise and bad smells; yet
has he invented government without ability,
privilege for fools, and war which nobody
escapes except the makers of it.
Here in this creek in the Flinders Range
is a sanctuary of which the like should be
made compulsory on one night of the year;
every man forced to go alone, to think him-
self out, and decide which part of himself
he should kick hardest. The shopkeeper, and
the exaggerated and ridiculously dignified ex-
ample of him, the wholesaler, who flaps the
flag and bleats empire to-day in the sure
and certain hope that he will buy of Ger-
many a day after the war, if Germany will
be the cheaper; the mouth-fighters and self-
advertisers who generate limelight from the
bones of decent men; and all the noisy
frauds who think that Patriotism consists in
loudly and publicly hating Germany rather
than in loving Australia so much as to cheer-
fully bear discomfort for her sake; the
Judge whose cruelty lies in the love of hurt-
ing the men he sentences; the barrister who
for money will defend the criminal he knows
to be guilty, and then accept a brief to prose-
cute in a conspiracy to defeat the law —here
to this creek should they come. Also, all
parsons, kings and aldermen, and all such as
defeat the truth, split hairs and deceive them-
selves. But whether they could be silent long
enough to bring themselves to a level con-
sideration of themselves is quite another
matter.
So, like a grain in the bottom of this cup
in the hills, I breathe the starry silence and
am saturated with it. Eastward there is
wheat land, limestone, mallee, Wowserville-
on-the-Yarra, Riverina and the Pacific;
westward, the two Gulfs, long plains and
salt lakes, the Golden Mile and the Indian
Ocean ; northward, millions of acres of wheat
land and pasture, saltbush, spinifex. the lost
rivers, Mitchell and Flinders grass, and a
territory four times the size of France—
France, that liberator of the world in thought
and action, whose fall would put back
the clock of the world further than if all the
other combatants fell dead to-night; south-
ward, more wheat lands, and limestone ami
the terraced country from Murray Bridge to
Kingston and Robe, where if petroleum •
not struck directly I shall iTShSU
annoyed. (So money-getting won’t stay £
of the programme. Retro me Sathanas,)
Further south, Tasmania and all the fr o £
devils that sail the winds between
Horn and Whale Head. Upe
What is the sum of all this greatness thi«
lonely spaciousness? Its future is big and
in such times events do not tarry their f>
term in the womb. For Australia the future
is about to be delivered, and no child can 1 .
named until it is born. Fools, mostly im
ported, tell us we have no history because
we have no tradition—tradition, the fact of
the liar and the holy writ of the charlatan’
Had we this thing called tradition we would
be torn to pieces as Europe is to-day. The
oldest country in the world, we are the
youngest. Fate does not reserve such a
jewel as this for nothing greater than acci-
dent or caprice. Fortune did not, without
aim. protect this land by 4000 leagues of sea
until the older -world could learn that state-
craft is as hollow a mockery as priest-
craft, and that in good government there is
no mystery. And good government and the
peace of the world will come only with uni-
versal republicanism.
These hills are gracious and silent because
the privileged class is not here to partition
the land and the chances among itself. Given
Australia settlement a thousand years ago
and to-day some such privileged class as that
governing England, which can neither work
nor think, would be ordering the thinkers
and the workers to do their fighting. Aus-
tralia. just because it has no traditions and
is modern and hard-headed, has done better
in the war in proportion than any other
part of the loose confederation miscalled an
empire; big-framed, big-souled men gone to
help the helpless and revive the dead. The
great trouble with monarchies is that they
are fat-headed with tradition and never
modernise; barbarians wdio wall not be of
to-day. who cling to superstitions and live in
the dirty past that privilege built to make
easier its grinding of the faces of the poor.
There is a silence at Gallipoli. Only the
voices of the fools who caused that silence
bray in safety.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
THE CURSE OF THE “SECRET”
DISEASE.
“T. Pallidum” discusses? a matter of just
about as much national importance as the
war: —
The English Royal Commission on Venereal
Diseases has issued a plainly-written report which
should be of profound interest to Australia. Inci-
dentally it points out, as a result of exhaustive
inquiry, that 50 per cent, of sterile women are
rendered so by gonorrhoea, that 24 per cent, of the
children in London blind schools are blind by reason
of maternal gonorrhoea and another 30 per cent, by
reason of inherited syphilis. It refers to the pre-
disposition to cancer of the tongue which is one of
the legacies of syphilis in adults, and to the heavy
mortality from syphilitic diseases of the blood-
vessels. Imbecility and idiocy are largely due to
inherited syphilis. Out of 1001 pregnancies investi-
gated, in 150 syphilitic families, 172 resulted in mis-
carriages or still births. One out of every seven
males and one out of every 33 females admitted to
the London County Council asylums are lunatics by
reason of general paralysis, and general paralysis is
an after-result of syphilis. The total cost of
asylum treatment for syphilis-created lunatics in
England and Wales is over £150,000 a year. In the
one year 1912 the British “home” naval and military
forces, totalling 226,092 men, lost 485,655 days as
a result of venereal disease, or 1330 years of working
time, for which the country paid. Amongst the adult
civil population the loss in wages could not be
calculated, owing to the absence of any means ot
obtaining returns, but it must have been enormous.
The deaths from venereal disease are concealed by
the absurd system which makes a death-certificate a
public document, and deters a doctor from stating
his honest opinion of the primary cause of deat
for fear of offending the victim’s relatives.
* * * *
The Commission addressed inquiries to 285 hospi-
tals supported wholly or in part by public con ll
bution. Only 67 of them were civil enough to
reply. In the great majority of hospitals it "as
found that no adequate records for statistical pur
poses were kept. Treatment of venereal lsease
was not encouraged in general hospitals, an some
of the subscribers objected to the admission o
venereal cases. Some “public” hospitals had ru es
forbidding the admission of the disease.
Health authorities provide practically no 0 cia
facilities for laboratory diagnosis in this direc-
tion ; and the training of medical student*
was found to be insufficient, with t e
suit that medical men do not always re y*'
these diseases with enough seriousness.
Commission is uncompromising in respect to ns
feature. “The medical practitioners of this coun
are to a large extent unfamiliar with the ne''j-
methods of diagnosis and treatment. m 'g rp
be expected, the trade medical journa s
vigorously combating this statement, and o e
wise hurling mud at the Commission for 1 s o
conclusion that “concerted action by a P u
authority is essential” for dealing properly "i
question. The quack and “young man's frie
harpy are cordially recommended for penal
The’evidence of ’a representative of the Natl
Society of Herbalists is left to speak for “ sel ” .
“in our opinion effectually disposes of t e
of herbalists to be regarded as competent to
venereal diseases.” Incidentally they ex P r
definite opinion against the Contagious Diseases - >
and regard them as rather more prone to jj V)
spread of disease than to control it.
“There can be little doubt that a frarrer a
towards these diseases would lead to less 00
ment, often involving recourse to quack rem >
which may retard cure or render it impossi l >
would thus assist in checking one of the gr
evils which can afflict a community. in - -
might with advantage be tattooed on the
those publicists who cannot speak of venere , j.
without calling it a “hidden canker or a
curse” or a “red plague” or by some ot er
synonym. .
* * *
The Commission gets off some useful recom
mendations as to what should be 0 ~a
England, but its report should interes - I ‘-
more particularly in respect to what can an
be done here. Venereal diseases are admit* .
prevalent in Australia, and they will become _f
prevalent as an almost inevitable conseque


military conditions. There is sitting at present in
Melbourne a Federal Committee for Investigating
Causes of Death and Invalidity in the Commonwealth.
One of its selected subjects of inquiry is venereal
disease, and in the preliminary report the committee
admits that “enormous power for evil should be
attributed to” this and alcoholism. Such reports
as the Committee has issued up to now have been
brief surveys based on the statistics of a couple of
States, accompanied by sundry academic statements
of measures which should be taken by some person
or persons or authority not clearly indicated. A
really useful piece of work would be for the Com-
mittee to undertake an inquiry into venereal disease
in Australia on at least as detailed and thorough
lines as have been followed by the Royal Commission
in England. Sterility of women, disabling of chil-
dren, and impairment of adult efficiency are rela-
tively of greater national importance in Australia
than they are in England, and the opportunity exists
to probe the whole question thoroughly. It cannot
be done by a few hours of discussion round a table,
or a two-page report of statistical summaries and
academic suggestions. The true position and im-
portance of venereal diseases in Australia requires
to be known, and there is no means of ascertaining
it with reasonable accuracy other than that of close
and detailed investigation into all phases of the
subject by a small body of competent inquirers
possessing the requisite knowledge and legal powers,
The investigators are already available, and if the
Committee’s powers do not extend to the calling
of witnesses and the prosecution of detailed inquiry
throughout Australia, they can be readily enough
widened.
“NO SURPRISES.”
According to Winston Churchill the
great feature of the recent sea battle was
that the Germans had no surprises concealed
about their ships. Their naval construction
had been on the old lines; their naval gunnery
was similar and inferior to that of the British.
For this reason their enterprise was an
entirely mad one. The only way in which
a fleet inferior in weight and numbers can
hope to beat a superior one is by surprise.
When, in the days of Nelson, small British
fleets met bigger ones of France and Spain,
the surprise lay mostly in the Nelson touch
which compelled ships trained on a theory
of long-distance fighting to engage at pistol
range, or even closer. True, in the latex-
battles, like Trafalgar, this had largely
ceased to be a surprise; but by that time
the prestige of the British sea-wizard had
so grown that enemy gunners felt like walk-
ing overboard at the mere sight of his flag.
The Germans’ land surprises during the
present war account for the fact that they
are still holding Belgium and an uncomfort-
able corner of France on the one side and
Poland on the other; but at sea they had
nothing new to show, and were whipped
back to the ditch at Kiel.
* * * *
If we could get this idea of surprise values
firmly into our minds, the future of Austra-
lian defence would look more hopeful. The
only surprise we have had to offer as our
contribution to this war has been the fighting
quality of our men; but far more than that
may be needed for the successful holding of
a thinly-peopled continent. It may be true that
no great danger is imminent, though if half
a dozen battle cruisers had slipped past
Beatty in the fog their guns might have
been battering us before the winter was
over; but it is equally true that a win-
ning surprise takes a long time to develop to
the limit of its possibilities. British gunners
take 12 years to train. If an Australian now
produced an idea of supreme value for the
defence of this country, it may be reasonably
calculated that if we went earnestly ahead,
losing no time and making the utmost of the
advantage it gave us, we should be ready to
put it to the test somewhere about 1928. _ And
the optimist who assures us that nothing on
earth can rise to plunge Australia into a
state of war by 1928, ought to be firmly
but gently put in a place where his rosy-
tinted ravings don’t matter. . Two years
befoi’e the outbreak of hostilities well-
informed people were assuring us that there
never could be war with Germany; two
weeks before the guns started to bellow a
majority of the ill-informed were still of the
same opinion. Had they been less trustful
this horror might have been averted. At
any rate, however calm the skies look at the
moment, there is no safety for a country save
in serious preparation and in making full
use of all its best brains.
* * * *
The surprise possible to Australia must lie
as much in method as in matter.
could be plainer than that the methods ol
defence now employed by British troops in
France and Flanders would be utterly useless
for the defence of a great continent by small
armies. Britain has been compelled to alter
her own methods greatly to suit the present
needs; but, as Kitchener tried to make us
see, the British army methods, even enlarged,
and sub edited as they have been, could no
serve our purpose. As far as we can Judge,
Britain has 1.500,000 men on a front of 90
miles; Australia in her day of trial may
have 300,000 men on a front of a thousand
miles. In other words, while Britain base
her operations on the employment of about
16,000 men to the mile, we may
make our defence with only 300 men to t
mile. Between the two there is as wide a
difference as between the plans of Welling-
ton at Waterloo and De TV et at the close <
the South African turmoil. Our surprise of
method, if we are to possess the artic e,
must be of such a nature that one man can
do the work of several hundreds through
mobility and resource. Now that the Oom
monwealth authorities have set the machine
of home defence and citizen training working
again, let us therefore above all things h
the Kitchener warning and remember •
our military establishment has more to learn
from Botha and Smuts than from Joffre
and llaig, not because they greater
generals, but because they understand the
continent-wide game as it must be p • •
Australia. In any case, the P a * -
stands that war is not an occupation like
bricklaying or rock-chopping; and if in the
day when an enemy lands he reports n
surprises,” we can consider ourselves down
and out in the first round.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Tall Man Who Did Things,
People had been writing about Kitchener
for 20 years, and most of what they wrote
was cheap fudge. But it was the stuff the
public wanted in an uncritical, somnolent
age, and if the result was a caricature-
well, what odds, so long as the public in-
sisted upon having such a figure on the pre-
mises? And then both Kitchener and the
British public paid the penalty: the man
because in the popular mind he had been
elevated to the position of a military
necromancer; the public because of a fatal
blindness to its obligations and perils. In
France a Kitchener would have looked a
national danger. In Britain, where the mili-
tary influence is wholly different, he seemed
the embodiment of what Britain most needed.
And in a measure he really was, for the man
had one great, commanding quality—he was
a magnificent straining post, and in the hour
when the fences had to be put up in a hurry,
w’hen agitated politicians were running about
wringing their hands and the nation awoke
with a start to the first dim realisation of its
nakedness, he played his part with dignity,
courage and strength. He talked but little.
H'e stuck to his job. And when he died he
had finished it. His place as a soldier
matters little. His great merit was that he
was an organiser, and had the temperament
and personality for organisation. It may be
found, when the air clears, that he made
many mistakes, some of them serious. It
would be a surprising thing if he did not.
The improvisation of armies of millions is
an undertaking in which mistakes can
reasonably be expected. Besides, he
had to deal with muddle of unex-
ampled magnitude—to grapple with a
crisis to which British statesmanship
had closed its eyes right up to the last, and
which, being not a man of large vision, he
had probably not foreseen himself. If he
did foresee it he, at any rate, failed signally
to prepare Britain to face it. But in the
welter that followed he stood four square;
not an inspiring figure, but certainly a
strong one.

The Long Arm of Responsibility.
The destruction of the warship that
carried Kitchener lays the Asquith
Government open to the same criticism that
boiled a while after the sinking of the Lusi-
tania and then grew cold. There can be
no reasonable doubt that the Hampshire fell
a victim to the German secret service, even
as the Lusitania did, the difference between
the two cases being that many people in
sundry places knew of the Lusitania
scheme a week before it was carried to a
successful issue, whereas the secret of the
forthcoming attempt on the Hampshire was
confined to the inner circle of Hun intrigue.
An order gazetted by the British Home
Secretary as soon as the tragedy became
known was practically an admission by the
Government of its belated suspicions. It
ordered that persons wishing to proceed to
the Orkneys must obtain the permission of
the military authority at Kirkwall, the
capital of the islands. A number of ships
had previously been torpedoed in those
waters, yet the Government had failed to
take this simple precaution against the
danger of German spies carrying informa-
tion to and fro. The same want of vigilance
as regards the coast of disaffected Ireland
had made the Lusitania tragedy a com-
paratively simple job. The enemy alien and
doubtful alien danger has been handled with
kid gloves ever since the war began. Even
tragic circumstances like the Lusitania
horror and the most disastrous of the Aep-
pelin raids on London compelled the Govern-
ment merely to gusts of mild action —so
mild that it was not until a fortnight ago
that the authorities ordered the dismissal
of all German captains from the British
mercantile marine. As for the question
whether up-to-date German submarines are
being adequately combated, that is a ques-
tion for seamen only. What everybody
knows is that German designs against cargo
and passenger ships are accomplished in
most cases by means of secret-service infor-
mation. It isn’t by chance that a Hun
submarine gets the opportunity to torpedo
a Lusitania or a big cargo vessel whose
departure from port has been signalled, and
whose whereabouts at a given time can be
calculated. The submarine peril is in a
large measure a secret-service peril, and
were there no German spies at any British
or “neutral” port the Huns would rarely’
effect an accidental coup. Therefore the
bell-toppered brokers of London Stock
Exchange were justified shiiekmg
“Spies!” when the news of Kitchener’s
destruction came to hand. _ Against Ibis
widespread treachery there is no sort or
safeguard save widespread suspicion and
stern precautionary measures.

Which is to go—Labor or the P.L.L.?
The executive of the N. S. Wales P.L.L. has
hurled a sudden and evil-looking bomb into
the midst of the recently-whipped and
severely-chastened Parliamentarians who
are supposed to do its bidding : it intimates
its refusal to endorse any Labor candidate
who declares his belief that military service
is a public duty to be done by all. If the
attitude is persisted in, the next move must
be made by N. S. Wales, which must turn
down the P.L.L. executive as a poor
narrow-visioned, undemocratic thing. Most
of the members of the State’s Legislature,
whatever tlieir other faults, are sound on
thi« principle of universal service; and it
an irresponsible, unrepresentative minority
insists on being a law and conscience to
them, there is nothing more certain than
that the most upright of the members will
be driven out of the party, which will then
almost certainly suffer an overwhelming
defeat at the polls. If, on the other hand,
the politicians take a firm stand, brush the
vaporings of the P.L.L. executive on one
side, laugh at its refusal to endorse their
candidature and appeal to the voting public
against such preposterous tyranny, the
P l L. will either retrace its foolish steps
or be snuffed out as completely as an
unpleasant foreign match on a windy day.
In a word, the P.L.L., if it insists on its
mad and foaming career of destruction, will
raise the issue whether Labor as a party
shall cease or whether the machine which
professes to control Labor shall be kicked
aside. If Holman and his Parlia-
mentary following will deal firmly with
the alleged crisis which has arisen, it need
not occasion them an hour’s loss of repose.
If they don’t they will certainly have the
pick of Australia to deal with when the war
is over, even if they manage to escape the
wrath of the heavily-drained constituencies
now.
The Muddled Business of War “Honors.”
The position with regard.to medals, ribbons,
badges and war decorations in general is
wholly unsatisfactory. In Australia a
Returned Soldiers’ Association has . been
formed, and, off-shooting from it a Rejected
Volunteers’ Association. One may. also see
youths wearing colored ribbons inscribed
“Anzacs, 1915.” They may have taken part
in the operations on the Peninsula; it is
possible, alternatively, that they are impost-
ors, who never saw Gallipoli, but ornament
themselves with the object of acquiring free
refreshment and cheap glory. And now
the Defence Department is issuing four dif-
ferent badges—to home service men, to re-
jected volunteers, to munition workers and
to returned soldiers. At any of the numer-
ous Fronts one may meet, thousands of men
who have risked their lives almost daily,
and w T ho are wearing nothing to show it. for
the sound reason amongst others that if
they attempted to do so they would be
severely punished. As against this it is
common to see soldiers who have not heard
a shell burst in the past two years wearing
recently-conferred ribbons of all the mili-
tary orders except the V.C. It is a ludicrous
fact that it is far easier to win a “comba-
tive” medal such as the D.S.O. or the Mili-
tary Cross in a base office than in a front-
line trench. For facing unimaginable dan-
gers and hardships on Gallipoli, month after
month, certain Australian officers were
given C.M.G.’s —the'decoration that has been
regarded hitherto as a trivial gaud to be
flung to the civil servant who has done
fairly well in an inferior job. Dozens of
men on base work or “cushy jobs” have
earned D.S.O.’s in the present war. A few
weeks ago, in Egypt, a well-known English
aviator flew from the Canal to the Turkish
railhead, bombed the ammunition depot
there, was brought so close to the ground by
the subsequent explosion that the enemy
ceased firing at him, and got back after a
flight of nearly 250 miles, with his machine
looking like a colander. He was rewarded
with the mere Military Cross, which is of
much less consequence than the D.S.O. And
he was surprised to get even that much. The
genuine fighting man feels that way because
he sees so many acts of wild heroism going
unnoticed. Eet it be granted that eveiy
man who fights bravely under appalling con-
ditions cannot be given one of the great
orders; and let it be further granted that
certain soldiers may fairly earn these orders
by the performance of cvivilian work. There
still remains the fact that to the man who
takes the big risk, something more is due
than so many shillings per diem and the men-
tion of his name in tbe newspapers when
he stops the inevitable projectile. The Brit-
ish Government announced recently that
“the time had not arrived” for the issue of
war medals. It has. Life is short in the
best of circumstances. In the battle-lme u
is a matter of weeks, not to say days. It
there is any merit at all in war-medals, it
is reasonable, obviously, to give them while
war is in progress, as is already done in
the case of war-orders. Under the present
arrangement Australia is threatened with a
garish rash of mostly home-staying citizens
displaying masses of shining ironmongery
on their peaceful bosoms, while the chests
of our expeditionaries seem fated indefi-
nitely to bear nothing more conspicuous
than identification discs.
Things that Must Go with Protection.
“0.K.”: Australia wants something more
than an improved Protective tariff to cure
the people of their weakness for foreign
goods. It wants its manufacturers and
workers to be worthy of Protection in the
last degree, which is the prohibition of all
foreign rivals. To be thus worthy they must
at least produce the equal of the foreign
article, after getting a fair chance to do so.
My complaint is that in some lines the some-
what-protected manufacturers have been con-
tent for 25 years to supply a large public
demand without seriously endeavoring to
improve the quality of their product. Lake,
for instance, the popular lager beer. 1 uder
Protection an imitation of light German
lager was brewed in Australia in Mel-
bourne, firstly—and was drunk with enthu-
siasm by people who appreciated its mildness
in comparison with ordinary “colonial beer.
Has it improved in the desired quality ot mild-
ness? ! —on the contrary, it is more like
“colonial” beer and less like German lager
to-day than it was a quarter of a century
ago. Meanwhile the imported article grew
lighter and less alcoholic than of yore.
Beck’s lager, now vetoed for enemy
reasons, appealed to what may be called the
restaurant public. Doctors recommended i-
for convalescents who craved for beer. There
was a growing demand for a local substitute
for Beck’s ; but the local brewers didn’t
attempt to supply it. Consequently other
brands of imported lager, from neutral
countries, have been rushed upon the market.
Local manufacturers are to blame. The
brewers were not game to invest a small
portion of their profits in experimental brew-
ing of the lighter beer which has hitherto
been imported. Plucky- experimenting is
essential to progress in production. Another
handicap to local industry is the foolish
obstinacy of enthusiastic Protectionists in
denying simple facts concerning young and
crude manufactures. Take, for instance, the
local lucifer matches. There was a howl of
indignation from Protectionists two or three
years ago when a Melbourne match factory
closed down owing to what was called the
consumers’ disloyal preference for cheaper
imported matches. As a matter of fact, the
locally-made article was then very defective.
The head of the absurdly-thiek match was
apt to break off when struck on the box, or
the unseasoned bodv broke in two. The pub-
lic knew from experience that the goods were
no good, whilst angry politicians swore that
the matches, being Australian, were naturally
the best in the world. Their talk was a
waste of hot air. The public continued to
buy imported matches, and the local Bryant
and May factory continued to improve on its
first output. To-day the B. and M. match is
preferable to what is imported from the low-
wage factories of Sweden and Japan. I speak
honestly as a consumer, not as a staunch
Protectionist who would tell any darned lie
to serve the cause. The importation of
foreign matches should be stopped by a
thumping tariff now that the local product is
quite “up to Dick.” At present it is handi-
capped by its former ill-reputation (which
the grocery stores endeavor to keep burning)
rather than by the fact that it costs id.
more for a dozen boxes than its imported
rivals. Nobody tells the public that the old.
genuine objections to the B. and M. match
have disappeared. Certainly the grocer
doesn’t. Probably he makes an extra farth-
ing a gross profit on the foreign stuff; hence
his negatively stinking-fish policy. And the
public is indisposed to assume that the local
manufacture of matches has improved. Why?
Because the ardent apostles of Protection
have always tried to bluff them into the
belief that anything and everything made in
Australia could not be improved upon. Pro-
tectionists have protested too much. They
have helped to aggravate the very prejudices
they protested against.

Nibbling at the Parks.
The tremendous desire which people in pub-
lic positions evince for cutting little bits off
the public parks in our towns and cities must
have some sort of psychological basis. An
ordinary citizen ne'-er has the least desire to
grab a park, unless in rare instances as a
plain personal steal; but the official mind
somehow recoils from open spaces, and if. it
doesn’t actually want to cover them with
bricks, it is constantly evolt ing schemes by
which they can be whittled away and turned
into something other than they are. The
recent demands in and about Sydney for
pieces of existins? parks to convert into school
playgrounds look most innocent; but they
are not entirely the mild, pellucid things they
seem. As the parks stand they are every-
body’s playgrounds, the extent and scope of
the play being regulated merely by the
authority of a stray gardener here and there
who cultivates a plot or two of flowers. To
fence off portions for the exclusive use of
certain groups is to limit the hunting ground
of the people in general. Especially has the
child in general a right to consideration
before the child in particular; and, more-
over, once alienated for whatever purpose,
land near cities or within their bounds lias
an unhappy knack of calling for bricks and
mortar. The Australian child is a person
of supreme importance—the more so on
account of the heavy war wastage. He
deserves the fullest consideration he can get;
and there is no building in all Australia that
can compare in public value with the ground
on which the child plays and breathes fresh
Australian air under a good Australian sun.
If special school playgrounds are needed arid
no open land can be obtained, there should be
no difficulty at all about tearing down a few
terraces of ill-ventilated, unhealthy houses
to give room. To snip pieces off the park*
is merely robbing Tom to confer some imag-
inary benefit on Thomas. If land is really
wanted for the children in connection with
the schools, let them have it at any cost ex-
cept at the expense of the children them-
selves. These people who want to cut up
parks on any excuse need watching. It is
one of the cardinal public sins and one of
the best tests of a public person’s attitude
of mind towards his fellow citizens. The
man who would filch away a park would sell
his country for about tuppence and say he
was doing it for patriotic reasons.

handing out promotions has never been very
popular, but of late the system has become
worse, to the probable prejudice of recruiting.
At the start, commissions were mostly given
to those belonging to “good families,” quite
irrespective of ability and experience. And
when Btlljim wrote home of muddles and
mistakes at Egypt and Gallipoli the enthusi-
asm about taking risks under such eminent
leaders wasn’t boundless. The other day in
Westralia there was a fine chance to secure
junior officers who had seen service. Of the
Youth Militant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In all the wars the lords of evil wage
Youth fights for Age.
Youth is the sword and striking arm of
Power.
In the dark hour
With a clean heart for guerdon, unafraid
It bears the brunt of wrongs it never made,
Paying the blood-debt left to it for dower;
And though the dragon’s teeth in spears arise
It faces death with laughter in its eyes.
And safe at home behind its bastioned gates
Age sits and waits,
Knowing full well the wings that hide the
sun
Are evils done;
The world that swarms with myriad hates
to-day
It fashioned falsely from the moistened clay,
And Youth alone can see redemption won.
What shall requite it now its blood is cold.
A hive of honey, or a hoard of gold i
We are the youth that girds its armor on,
Childhood has gone!
For all the evil seeds the past has sown
We must atone ;
Shedding our dreams of ease and comfort, we
Fare forth undaunted on a perilous sea
To carve with steel the new world’s corner-
stone.
But though false hearts may prove the con-
flict vain
We fight that men shall never fight again.
VICTORIA.
VANCE PALMER.

Three Points of
Goodyear Superiority.
SOME MOTORISTS buying their
first "Anti-skid-’ tyres seem to think
■that a tyre with any kind of projec-
tion on the tread will answer.
If the projection is at all rounded it will
sbp—what the motorist must have in a real
anti-skid tyre is a sharp edge projection to
grip slippery pavements and muddy roads.
There is a scientific reason for using the
shape, patented, and now used exclusively on
the Goodyear “All-Weather Treads,” whose
blocks of contact are lined up exactly to
front and rear, giving the long, sharp, angular
edge, faced to the direction of skidding.
Here is the first great advantage possessed by
Goodyears alone in the world of tyredom.
COMPARE THE WEARING area of
Goodyear All-Weather Treads not
witli ANY OTHER, but with ALL
OTHER patterns of anti-skids.
Tou will he impressed with the fact that
“Goodyears” have by far the greatest amount
of wearing, surface, without detracting in the
least from their hull-dog grip on everv road.
The blocks on Goodyears keep their shape
under the hardest of usage for thousands of
miles—they never all wear off. Here is
another point of vantage in the permanency
of the anti-skid action of Goodyear All-
Weather Tread Tyres.
THE CONSTRUCTION of a tyre
may be ever so perfect in design, but
if it falls down in quality of com-
ponent parts it is no better—in fact,
not as good as its competitors.
Here is where Goodyear Tyres score highest
in the test of actual usage. The material
throughout is of the hie-hest quality obtain-
able.
The quality of Goodyear Tyres has, above
all, been responsible for the volume of sales
that have necessitated a factory alone employ-
ing over 9000 men to-day, where in 1901 it
employed but 225. Nothing but quality,
value for monev and satisfied users could
have attained this result.

The Sudden Arrival of a Bear.
There was a loud, growling noise in front
of the Austrians at Galicia last week, and
when the smoke of a furious bombardment
cleared away they discovered that the Bear
had rubbed the snow out of his eyes and was
leaping forward with a bayonet. Where-
upon they started to go astern, and there
was great slaughter, with captures of Aus-
trian soldiery and the issue at Petrograd of
communiques announcing victory. The
yarn as it stood a couple of days ago was
that the Bear had bitten three great lumps
out of the Austrian line running from Cer-
nowitz northward to the Pripet marshes and
was busily engaged pushing himself through
the holes. If it is all true, then the Aus-
trian is in a bad way; and as the Russian
appears to have several mountains of ammu-
nition handy, hope that he is asserting an
emphatic mastery and tangling the Huns up
in a serious complication arrives at a
moment when the commodity is in great
demand. While all this was going on the
German continued to batter away at Verdun
(where, in spite of his reported gigantic
losses and frequent annihilation, he seems to
be gradually getting into the French posi-
tion!, and a stray line or two in the cable
messages made reference to a recent hot time
in Flanders, where, according to the London
Times, the enemy “has been made pay
heavily for his successes,” and “the bloodiest
battle fought since Loos” has taken place.
The meaning of these phrases is concealed
behind a vast silence. The presence of our
own lads in the western trenches would
seem to ignora ut parents and sweethearts and
wives to be a reason why Australia should
hear something of what is happening there-
abouts; but, glory be! Hughes paid them a
visit the other day and made some speeches
and had afternoon tea with someone, and
we had several thousand blithering words
about that! These frequent hunks of infor-
mation about Hughes valiantly working his
jaws, strolling by rivers, eating and gener-
ally toiling like a beaver, are a fearsome
illustration of the horrors of modern
warfare. The Australian citizeness who
grabs her morning paper to find out
if her husband is fighting and can
only find that Hughes is talking about
someone’s imperishable glory, ought to
be ashamed of herself if she cannot recog-
nise how important it is for military reasons
to keep the newspapers, flooded with stuff
that doesn’t matter. Meanwhile, if there is
much about Hughes and nothing about the
Anzacs, and still less that is of any value
concerning Salonika and other points, there
is at least the definite news from Italy that
in consequence of the recent defeats in the
Trentino the Salandra Government has
been passed out. It was-about time. Italy’s
military activity had for a long while been
the subject of queer comment in neutral
countries.
There have been four hotly-contested by-
elections in the Ma State since the war
and other things started, and the Holman
Government has lost the lot —Willoughby,
l’arramatta, Drummoyne and Bingara. Biu-
gara had been invaded by the Ministry en
masse to resist the re-election of McDonald,
who sat in the Assembly as a Labor man.
but in the midst of the disturbance between
Holman and the P.L.L. a few weeks ago
came to the conclusion that the time was
ripe to sit as something else. So he re-
signed "as a protest against interference
with members of the Legislature by Labor
organisations,” and, opposed by McClelland
(Labor), ran as an Independent. The
vote was counted on Saturday, and Holman
heard the banshee wailing long before the
job was finished. McDonald* who won at
the general election by 205 votes, was 600
or so ahead this time. Whereupon Holman
put another piece of steak on his eye and
said McClelland was “greatly handicapped.”
lie kas, indeed.
“Greece is pandering to all parties,” says
a Melbourne paper. “Her subservience is
sickening.” Greece, in short, is more oily
than righteous.

If Julius Knight had started his Sydney
1 loyal season with “The Silver King” a week
earlier, he might have claimed a glorious
share in the six o’clock victory that will .put
the Demon Drink in its cage before the
thirsty theatre patron even goes home to
dress. Whether a prudent actor would
care to be associated with a reform that is
going to fill the intervals with gloom is
another matter. The fine old anti-booze ser-
mon with the happy ending is well poured
out by the present co., and a war-sobered
public receives it gratefully as a sort of
moral tonic. Julius himself, in a smaller and
less flamboyant hat than the returning kings
of olden time were wont to wear, lands the*
flapping fish of applause with the old, old
lines, and is as unlike a reciter as possible
when rolling out Denver’s Dream. Likette
Parkes makes a very youthful and appealing
Nellie Denver, whose complexion is not im-
paired by poverty, and Boyd Irwin is a glit-
tersome Spider. Old friend J. B. Atholwood
is admirable as Jaikes, and Harry Sweeney
will be a satisfactory Geoffrey Ware when lie
learns to arrive early and die after the shot
is fired. Emma Temple gets a brief look-in
and a welcome late in the evening; she is the
insinuating old she-yokel who wants to marry
Atholwood. "The Lion and the Mouse” is
billed for revival on Saturday.
Derek Hudson gets his big chance again in
“Gipsy Love,” which melodious foreign pro-
duct is being run off by the re vis d
Royal Comics at Her Majesty’s, Sid-
ney. The poor, thin “book” is beauti-
fully wrapped up in Leliar’s music,
and the Gipsy with the hair over his
eyes is so gallant and takes his lovemaking
so seriously that the feeble flapping and gag-
ging of the comedians is hardly noticed.
Leslie Holland is Dragotin, the burlesque
Roumanian noble; Florence Young bobs up
again as a mature Lady Babhy, though old
admirers insist on continuing to he gallantly
surprised by her youthfulness: Minnie Love
is kittenish as .Tolan ; and Ethel Cadman is
a low-powered Ilona. Addie LenUard looks
and struggles well as a proud, non-working
gipsy girl, and John Ralston’s melodious inn-
keeping is like a drink of heavy port. The
unctuous Phil Smith repeats his miniature
Roumanian Simple Simon; and altogether
the writer would feel more cheerful about the
Allies’ chances in the Balkans if people like
that inhabited Bulgaria or some other hos-
tile State.

Coming late in Allan Wilkie’s Shak-
fpearian season at Sydney Adelphi.
“Othello,” produced on Saturday, finishes up
to-morrow (Friday) night. Which is a
pity; it deserves a longer hearing. In this
tragedy the supports show to better advan-
tage than in most other efforts, partly, no
doubt, because of the wider experience of
most of them in melodrama. The lago
of Walter Hunt was a notable in-
stance; lie was an excellent villain.
The vivid recollection of the “Othello”
of Oscar Asche naturally overshadows
Allan Wilkie’s Moor; yet it is a
creditable characterisation, a little over-
strained in places, but always at least hang-
ing together. The Cassio of Robert
Faulkiner was most effective after the appli-
cation of a dozen pewters or so, though he
wearied somewhat of being tipsy when the
Royal Commission began to take evidence.
Frediswyde Hunter-Watts was a pleasing
Desdemona in figure and speech. With the
advantage that she was not called Upon
to wail at any length. The Roderigo of
Walter Champney was a comedy touch that
strayed out of bounds. Elwyn Harvey
(Bianca) and Valentine Sydney (Mrs.
Tago) earned certificates in Class II.; and
John Cosgrove (portly Governor of Cyprus)
and Fred Kelioe (Brabantio > did their little
well. On Saturday “Romeo and Juliet” will
be staged ; then, after four nights of that, a
two nights’ resurrection of “Hamlet” will
close the season.

The Royal Commission appointed to
investigate thfe matter is still discovering
sensational evidence of the disgraceful, but
amusing, advertising methods by which the
American Soap Trust pushes its goods upon
the credulous public. At the Sydney Cri-
terion, where the Commission is continu-
ously sitting, the attendance of the public
is large, and the character of the evidence is
frequently productive of “laughter in the
Court,” which the ushers are quite unable
to suppress.
“Peg o’ My Heart” is-still making money
for the Taits at Sydney Palace; but dog
Michael looks as insolvent as ever.
Rose Berry, a girl burlesque artist with
some new tricks, came to Sydney Tivoli last
week-end. Ley wood and Novell, a mixed
team in the same line of business, also
arrived ; and some feet higher up Loose and
Sterling showed off their muscles on
the Roman rings. After that group came
the same old Tivoli Pollies, with the official
ad.-minstrel singing the praises of everybody
from Vera Pearce to the scenesliifter in four
keys at once.
The only difference between the arrival of
Kultur in Belgium and its appearance at
W irths’ Hippodrome in Sydney is that the
real article was preceded by artillery fire at
Liege while the Wirth Brothers provide
music and song and exhibitions by clever
people on bicycles and a slack-wire 'to start
with. But this doesn’t prevent Kultur being
presented as the unspeakable animal it really
is, and when the Hun gets swept away iii
20.000 gallons of water the only possible
objection to be raised is that the whole
Pacific Ocean should be staged and dropped
with three cheers where it would do most
damage.
