Three nights this week Miss Hilda Free-
man lectures at the Playhouse about The
Blood Lust in Germany. Saturday, 24th,
the Repertory Co. will make a commercial
start in “Quinney’s,” on which occasion
Melba will mark her goodwill to the strag-
gling cause by appearing in the fourth act
of Verdi’s “Otello.”
The war is about to do something for
music-lovers in Australia, as a set-off to
all the musical schemes it has blighted. The
Gonsalez Opera Co., which Marlow has
been game to take up, was not originally
formed for tonring purposes, as the Quinlan
Co. was. Lorenzo Gonsalez and his brother
had been running grand opera in Italy for
years before the war broke out. When
that disaster happened the firm found
a difficulty in getting theatres, so
Lorenzo took liis crowd to India; and
it was a Calcutta connoisseur who
advised - George Marlow that here was
a first-class organisation, judged by the
Quinlan standard. Gonsalez was then in
Sydney, having come along to see
whether Australia could be worked, and
Marlow resolved to risk it. There are a
dozen principal vocalists and a chorus of
40-odd in the co. A couple of Gonsalez
signors share the conducting of the orches-
tra : this has a backbone of 10 members
from Italy, and another 20 or so players are
being engaged in Melbourne —a more trouble-
some job, it seems, than the augmenting ot
the chorus. Lorenzo Gonsalez is delighted
with the Melbourne recruits for the chorus.
But. then, no foreign musical man was ever
otherwise than pleased with the local sing-
ing talent. The extra choristers have cap-
ital voices, plus the quick intelligence
necessary to carry them through a rapid-
change repertoire. Twenty operas or more
are in the bag—all of them old and tuneful
works, hut in some cases (such as “Favor-
ita,” “Un Ballo,” “Huguenots”) not fami-
liar to the younger generation. The
season will begin with “Trovatore’
“Traviata” and the “Gavalleria” and “Fag-
liacei” double to follow. For the season
Marlow has fixed a maximum of 6s. and a
minimum of 2s. on the prices, and he ex-
pects the co. to be hailed as mighty hue
value for the money.

“So Long, Letty,” will shake its manifold
legs at Dull Care for another week or two,
and then “The Girl on the Film” will be
revived at Melbourne Majesty’s.
“On Trial,” at Melbourne Royal, will con-
tinue to unfold its old familiar sort of tale
in an unfamiliar way seven times a week.
Little Dorothy Seacombe and her exceedingly
hasty Daddy (Frank Harvey) seem to be
the principal objects of sympathy in this
distressful trial for murder. Madge Fabian
gets very little consideration, especially
from the women in the audience. Her
attempts to dispel her husband’s sus-
picions are too obviously doomed to
failure. file’s the least artful libber that
ever was in domestic life. Her awful
inability to juggle with a guilty secret is a
reflection on the sex, and the verdict of the
female jury is that her carelessness in losing
her virtue and her velvet handbag was only
exceeded by her folly in losing her head.
At Melbourne King’s “Just a Girl” is
still paying a huge sum for a footing in the
English peerage. According to the late W.
S. Gilbert, dukes used to be about two a
penny; but the fact that Esmeralda is
charged £1,000,000 for an impecunious speci-
men with accumulated mortgages seems to
imply that some monopolist has cornered
the market. England ought to look into it.
But this Girl can’t wait: she has to leave
to-morrow night. Saturday’s entertainment
will be a new comedy-drama combining the
main incidents in Steele Rudd’s “Poor Par-
son” and “Duncan McClure.’”
Next to Cardo and Noll, the sugary song-
sters, the best turn at Melbourne Tivoli just
now is the three Kundies —one large man
and two slim boys —on the tight wire. Burt
Shepherd is an expert in the unlovely art
of stockwhipry, and Jack Bornhold’s novel
manner of dealing with a burlesque melo-
drama on a film screen would be very enter-
taining if his voice and diction were not as
hard and monotonous as a garden roller.
Horace Goldin’s new illusions are less
honored in the observance than in the advt.,
one of them being the venerable “vivisec-
tion” trick which the clown and pantaloon
used to perpetrate upon a dummy harlequin
in the happy days ere The Bulletin was
born.
At Melbourne Bijou Wish Wynne con-
tinues to kodak the mannerisms of English
female types without broadening her impres-
sions by caricature. Her picture of the
whimpering eldest girl in a Cockney family
is probably the cleverest example of her
photography. In the second part of the pro-
gramme the mechanical pleasantries of “The
Dizzy Limit” have replaced the absurdities
of “Something Doing.” The new revue is
brightly dressed as usual, but so far as the
entertainment is concerned —well, The
Bulletin would rather go fishing. Daisy
Jerome is a lavislily-froeked intrusion in the
show. Her trouble is that she intrudes too
long. She ought to be cautioned for loiter-
ing. Incidentally the red-headed lady has
started a Jerome song competition, and the
management has promised to fine itself six
guineas for encouraging the disturbance.
Apparently the cash will he handed over
in three prizes to the misguided females who
are found to be most gifted in imitating
Daisy.
"ON TRIAL," AT MELBOURNE ROYAL.
A very bored juryman tries to influence
Hob Greig, the piylieaded foreman.
“The Celebrated Scandal,” showing the
clever Betty Nansen in the principal role,
is stirring the feelings of the populace at
Hoyt’s Melbourne Olympia this week. It is
a dramatic story of a married lady’s platonic
friendship for a discreet young bachelor, hut
blood and tears have to flow in considerable
quantities before Mrs. Rumor is persuaded
that her suspicions are unfounded. The
other Olympia feature is “The Siren’s Song,
wherein a swindler’s soul is tended in pious
circumstances, with results that redound to
the glory of the Church. "The Song” is also
running at Hoyt's Bourke-street theatre,
where the biograph is telling all it knows
about a musician's tragedy called “The
Silent Voice.”
Adelaide theatres are largely coming round
to two new ideas: (1) day shows, (2) a
sliding scale of prices for day, night, and
holiday or Saturday. The Majestic, now the
city’s only variety-house, plays twice a day,
with the magnetic Madge Maitland, Adel-
aide’s own Hilda Felstead (greatly im-
proved by professional experience), and the
sharpshooting Corrigan and Vivian in the

James Brinsmead, an ancient figure among
the advertising chemists of Melbourne, and
best known to the public as the author of
an application for rendering Little Willie
pest-proof, has mortared his last pill-mixture.
He was a bent, shrunken shadow of a man
when the end came ; but he kept turning out
his fancy-labelled penn’orths at a handsome
profit until near the last.
“Jenkissimus”: One great daily, with a
knight at its helm, managed to read into a
cable the other morning that both Kitchener
and Jellicoe were lost. A special edition
was rushed out with great speed. It bore
a four-column scare-head, containing the one
word “Kitchener,” followed by a thing like
a landslide. Close inspection showed that
the words “and Jellicoe” had been chiselled
out of the plate in a hurry. A day or two
later the same great daily posted outside its
office in, let us say, Column-street, the news
that a raft bearing 12 survivors of the Hamp-
shire had been washed ashore. But to this
day the sub-editor of that paper is wondering
why some unknown seeker after the truth
which a great daily is demanded of conscience
to speak, impugn it whoso list, should ring
up and ask in all innocence and without
malice —oh. quite without malice —“Was
Sir John Jellicoe on the raft?”
When in 1889 the Calliope steamed out
of Apia Harbor, Samoa, in the face of a
hell-fire hurricane while a dozen war vessels
of other nations in the harbor were being
pounded to wreckage, she carried eight
middies, four of her own and four belonging
to the Orlando, flagship on the Australian
station. Of these eight one commanded the
Monmouth when she was sunk in the Coro-
nel fight; one was in charge of the Ilogue
when she was submarined in the North Sea ;
one had the Amphion, mined, and the
Undaunted, also sunk, in the North Sea ; a
fourth was Admiral Ilood, who went down
in the Invincible off Jutland; a fifth com-
manded the Chatham, which blew up the
Koningsberg; and the sixth was in charge
of the Sydney when she destroyed the
Emden.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Lou Tenant”: ITow long has our Andy
Fisher been in holy orders? Attended a
cinema show here (a small fishing village
somewhere in France) and one of the few
titles of pictures shown in English on the
screen was “The Reverend Andrew Fisher
inspecting Anzac Eroes in London. The
joyful yelp of the Australians present made
the Tommies and natives in the audience
wonder what the joke was.
“Mummer”: Shakspeare was wrong. The
part's the thing!
“Kodak” : The Front Legs of the Donkey
does chicken-farming when the panto, sea-
son ends. He says anyone with a fair
income from some steady job like Front- or
Hind-Legging can keep it going for a while.
The only people who fall in are the greedy
wretches who require more than three hours’
sleep per night and want to make money
out of the fowls. “Art for art’s sake” is
his motto. He admits that it is hardly
playing the game for a Poverty Pointer to
amuse himself brewing ammunition for the
unfriendly gods; liis only defence is that no
egg ever left his studio unless it had legs
and. was unthrowable.
“C. Ockney”: A somewhat remarkable
pro. at present Fuller-circuiting in Aus-
tralia has come in in carpet-slippers. Her
name is Wish Wynne and her specialty in
vaudeville is to give “character studies of
English home life.” The remarkable part
about the lady is that she has not proclaimed
that not so many months ago she was
playing the lead at one of the best of Lon-
don theatres. The o’er-modest Miss Wynne
was in fact the artist selected by Granville
Barker to create the heroine Janet Cannot
in Arnold Bennett’s “Great Adventure”—
the part played by Violet Paget when the
comedy was done at the Little, Sydney—•
and her success during the long, long run
of the piece was only equalled by that of
Henry Ainley, for years one of England’s
best-equipped mummers.
“Redfern Carpenter”: I once was mad
for the boards. I’m glad I was restrained
from setting foot on them. All credit to
dad. His arguments—two of them—were
weighty and knocked the nonsense out of
me. As I say, I’m glad now, though I was
sore then. Recent revelations regarding
the working conditions of the adulated actor
convince me that I’ve got the better job.
It’s steady and reliable, and up to now there
has been no 25 per cent, reduction of my
screw “on account of the war,” and when
I work I get paid. I learn by a paragraph
in the papers that, the mummers having
lately got the managers to recognise their
union, tremendous concessions have been
made by the bosses, and that new terms of
contract have been approved by both sides.
Details of the new agreement, which are
proudly given to the public, show that the
pros, at whatever salary give six nights
and two matinees (that is to say, eight
performances) as their week’s work. I
learn, further, that the performer has to
give three weeks’ rehearsal free before
commencing his engagement. Also a year’s
contract does not really mean a year, but
40 or at the most 42 weeks. And these
terms comprise what the secretary of the
union enthusiastically proclaims “the best
arrangement iie lias ever seen in any pari,
of the world as between managers and
actors.” What the conditions could have
been before this superlative agreement came
into operation can only be guessed at, 1
used to wonder what “Poverty Point
meant. I know now.
A recent London success is an English
opera, “The Bosun’s Mate,' by a woman
music-maker, Dr. Ethel Smyth. It is of
special interest in these parts because
Maorilander Kosina Buckman is the prjma
donna, and has been getting torrents of
applause for her work.
All the actresses belonging to Australia s
earlier dramatic history are being turned up
for war-fund performances in London —
Genevieve Ward. Olga Xetliersole, Bern-
hardt, etc. The number the war kills is
modified to a large extent by the number it
is bringing to life.
W. S. Percy is progressing in “Joyland."
at London Hippodrome. He started the
“interrupter in the audience” to llarry Tate,
then became valet in one of the sketches, and
now is concerned in several of the big turns
of the show. Arthur Wontner, who played
heavies for Julius Knight some years ago,
has gone into musical work, and is playing
the Prince in “A Happy Day,” at Daly's.
“ILB.”: “For the Term of His Natural
Life” Co., Ltd., is forming in Melbourne
with a capital of £4OOO, in £lO shares. The
idea is to make a film of Marcus Clarke’s
novel, and that has been done before. The
directors include one of the author’s sons
and S’renry Weedon —the latter probably
added to give literary tone. The co. secures
the Australasian and I .K. lights for 10
years, and estimated profits are £20,000 —
method of estimation not stated. The
vendors get 130 fully-paid shares and £7OO
in cash, which is quite enough consideration
and probably leaves insufficient for working
capital. The* high costs of efficiency in
movie production in the U.S.A. should be
sufficient hint to the Australian that it’s a
money-eating business, and that a little
capital is the same old mistake of sending
the boy to do the man's job.
“II.O.” : The limit in the way of a muddle-
headed jury figures in “On Trial.” The
charge is murder. Mrs. Strickland, in her
hubby’s absence from home, spends the day
at the seaside house of one of his friends.
When Strickland learns of this he kills the
gentleman. At the trial the wife tells a
harrowing story, which her husband hears
for the first time, of how, 13 years previ-
ously, when an in-no-cent girl of 17, she had
been seduced by the murdered man. Eleven
of the jury favor the husband’s acquittal:
the twelfth is doubtful, but is eventually
won over, because, as one intelligent juror
points out, the affair with the wife com-
bined with the wrong done her' 13 years
earlier surely constitute provocation to
justify the crime. So the dullards solemnly
acquit the hero, unanimously overlooking the
fact that the first hint of his wife’s youthful
“misfortune” was received by the husband at
the trial itself.
“Moana”:' They are starting to censor
kissing on the screen in parts of Uncle
Sham’s country. Ohio has decided that no
flicker kiss must occupy more than 10 feet
of film—-which seems a reasonable, allow-
ance. It doesn’t matter whether it is a
husband being reconciled to his wife or a
mother kissing her son. Anything over 10
is Out.
The captains of both the Queen Mary and
the Invincible married Sydney girls. Cap-
tain Prowse, of the Mary, was married to a
daughter of Dr. A. J. Brady ; and Captain
Cay, who went down in the Invincible, had
for wife a daughter of the late William
Laidley, M.L.C. But Mrs. Cay died four
years ago.
The burning of the ferry steamers Daphne
and Lobelia removes two more of the old
single-ended type which for so long menaced
traffic in Port Jackson. In 1900 the Harbor
Trust issued a ukase against the pattern,
and the present-day double-ender became
universal, although, with the anxious regard
for vested interests common to Govern-
ment Departments, the Trust permitted the
old outcasts to ply within certain limits.
The Daphne, a comfortless, awkward old
craft, was used on the Lane Cove River for
the best part of half a century.
The well-meaning cranks’ attempt to cur-
tail the hours for the sale of liquor on the
eastern goldfields of Westralia proved a dis-
mal failure. Everywhere, excepting a couple
of small, broken-hearted centres, the refer-
endum vote was almost unanimous for 11 p.m.
Which of course goes to prove (1) that the
goldfields are not patriotic; (2) that their
soldiers don’t want to be saved from the
Depths of Der-rink (average depth of der-
rink per day per man, one egg-cup) ; (3)
that as a result of its sinful waywardness
and waste its Little Children have to go
without boots. (Loud tears.)
THE PUBLICAN'S LAMENT
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The publican at Wollambell
Said sadly: “.Vow and then
1 have been hard, 1 grieve to tell,
On simple actor men.
I've seized their traps, and cast them forth,
But blacker luck befell:
They come no more from west or north
To play the plays we liked so well
At Beeglehole and Wollambell.
Instead we get those picture plays.
They come in scanty force —
Two mis’rable teetotal jays,
One gorforsakon horse:

DYSPEPTICS SHOULD AVOID DRUGS
AND MEDICINES.
try' a little magnesia instead.
Some people instinctively shut their eyes
to danger, and it may be that instinct, or
custom, or habit causes dyspeptics to take
drugs, patent foods and medicines, artificial
digestants, etc. But closing the eyes does
not banish the danger, and it is certain
that neither drugs nor medicines possess the
power to destroy the acid in the stomach,
which is the underlying cause of most forms
of indigestion and dyspepsia. They may
give temporary relief, but ever-increasing
quantities must be taken, and all the time
the acid remains as dangerous as ever. Phy-
sicians know this, and that is why the
usual advice now given to sufferers from
digestive and stomach trouble is, “Just get
some iisurated magnesia from your chemist
and take half a teaspoonful in a little water
immediately after every meal. This will
instantly neutralise the acid and stop food
fermentation, thus enabling you to enjoy
hearty meals without experiencing the least
pain or unpleasantness afterwards.” —Adyt.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
“iEsculapius”: Nine months ago two
overworked Australians were preparing for
a bathe in the blue waters of the Aegean.
“Beachy Bill” objected, and the boys
jumped for cover. One of them was too
late. The dead man’s chum lay on the
operating-table while his arm was being
explored for an embedded piece of shrapnel.
To the astonishment of the surgeon the
forceps withdrew not a splinter of iron but
a ring of gold. It was the emblem of
fidelity from a girl in Australia to the Anzac
who was blown into particles.
* * * *
Paris Nesbit’s son It.G., now acting as
a police magistrate in Adelaide, has made
a simple and sensible reform, just by say-
ing so. A man was put into the dock,
charged with being mentally defective, and
Nesbit objected to his being there with
criminals. All the police could say was
that it was a practice which had been in
force for years, and that the press did not
report these cases; to which the P.M.
replied that that showed the press thought
as he did. All such matters are to be heard
in chambers in future.
THE WIDOW’S VOTE.
“So you think I am too old for you?”
“A hit —hut I don’t mind giving you my
second preference.”
Parson Howell Price, of Waterloo
(N.S.W.), who has four sons arguing the
point for Australia with Bill the Blood-
soaked, did a bit of battling on his own
account the other day. An old church in
Sussex-street, Sydney, was sold some years
ago. as big warehouses and stores had
taken the place of dwellings in the locality,
and congregations had dwindled till they
were hardly visible. The purchase money,
under the act of Synod authorising the
sale, was to be applied to building other
churches where required in or near the city,
and meanwhile the interest was to 8° to
“evangelistic work” in slum areas. lhe
Svnod was now called together to sanction
a "scheme whereby the money should be used
instead of enlarging the Chapter House at
the Cathedral and building “new and com-
modious offices” for the secretary of the
Soeietv for Providing Bath Buns to
Indigent Stockbrokers. After the Arch-
gaiters in the chair had outlined the plan
and given it his blessing, Price got up and
hurled this bomb :
I can take you to places where our submerged
tenth exist under conditions which are a disgrace to
our Church and the Commonwealth. I can show
you tenements in which people live where moialit
is not possible, and yet we are proposing to apply
this fund, set aside for evangelistic work, PY
purposes. Some time ago I wrote to the Archbish p
for help, but I am sorry I got no reply. The people
I am speaking about look with suspicion on the
Church, and say, “The Church authorities do not
care for us, so why should we care for them.
What they sav about our Church dignitaries would
make your blood run cold. These things are reacting
against the usefulness and extension of the Ohuicn
to which we belong.
Then a squad of faithful curates headed
by an archdeacon, fell on Price and bleated
him out of his trench; and. the bynod
obediently authorised the dignitaries to use
the spondulix in making the gulf between
the Church and the poor a bit wider.

“General McMuddle” supplies a report of
the engagement:—
An angry crowd filled the narrow lane, glared at
the towering skyscraper and jostled towards the
steps. A stream of people was struggling to get
clear of the doors. Inside, a couple of lifts were
leisurely drifting up and down in answer to rings
from 11 floors, and sometimes one reached the
bottom. When it arrived 15 people leaped in and a
hundred cursed loudly and tackled the stairs. They
were nearly all bound to the same place, somewhere
near the roof —a narrow rathole where two harassed
individuals were writing in books on a counter.
After prolonged jostle and getting his feet trodden on
and absorbing the aroma of beer and onions, the citi-
zen participating in this struggle managed in an
hour or so to get what he wanted and then escape
in a procession of violent language down seven
flights of stairs, each with a sharp twist in it. It
was a great day. Oh, no! It wasn’t at the out-
patients’ department at one of the hospitals. It
was merely the place where the Sydney citizen had
to go one day recently to induce the Commonwealth
to accept his contribution to the revenue by way of
income tax. Why the Commonwealth Treasury
should have shown such absurd lack of imagination
and not concealed the receiving authority in the
Jenolan Caves is an inscrutable mystery to the
officers of the Intelligence Department.

“Karoola” explains and makes matters no
better: —
McCallum, M.H.R., of Maoriland, made no mis-
statement when he said (B. l/6/’l6) that Austra-
tralian privates are not supposed to use first-class
railway carriages in Egypt. According to an Order
issued in February last by the 0.C., Cairo District,
“privates must travel third-class on the trains; ser-
geants, sergeant-majors and warrant-officers may ride
second.” Also military pickets have for many
months guarded the approaches to Shepheard’s and
the Continental Hotel, to intercept interloping
rankers. A cheery disregard is, however, paid to
the law. As in the case of unreasonably offensive
civil statutes, a sort of -compromise has been arrived
at. Any non-com. or private can get into the big
hotels by representing himself to be an officer’s ser-
vant, or by walking in with an officer. Provided
he doesn’t get uproariously tight no one will molest
him; though, by a sort of unwritten law, neither
privates nor non-coms, seem to invade the grill-
rooms where the ultra-fashionables congregate. As
regards the trains, rarely does a white soldier dream
of travelling third, which is the class affected by
the iocal insanitary' natives. Many go second, from
motives of economy. No officers, whether British or
Australian, object to the numerous Commonwealth
and Maoriland privates who habitually go first; and
the Egyptian railway employees know enough
to follow suit. It was once rumored that a couple
of Australian non-coms, were barred from the dining
compartment of the splendiferous white train which
runs between Cairo and Luxor; but the yarn has
never been properly substantiated. Anyhow, it
would only have represented the exceptional insult
which goes to prove the deference which decently-
behaved members of the A.I.F. are mostly' treated
when abroad.
The “defcrerce,” presumably, which includes
them with niggers in an official order! No.
the matter cannot be glozed over. The order
is an insult which Australia will not tolerate.
“Australian Mother” on the same subject:
As a mother who has given her only child to the
Great Cause, may I add a few facts in relation to
R. McCallum’s statement in the Maoriland House
of Reps., with regard to the order that Australian
soldiers may ride only in third-class railway car-
riages in Egypt ? I received last mail a letter
from my son, dated April 19, in which he tells me
that they are only allowed to ride third-class—with
the niggers. A mate of his had the temerity to
venture into a second-class carriage, no third being
attached. He was pulled out by military police, in
spite of an officer speaking on his behalf. As they
pay' their fares they think this not only a humilia-
tion but a rank injustice. We have long known
that pickets are posted at Shepheard’s and the
Continental, to prevent the common herd from
entering those sacred portals. As an Australian, and
a soldier’s mother, I should like to know at whose
instigation this vile insult is offered to us and our
sons. Many have made big sacrifices, given up
good positions and prospects at duty’s call—to be
considered only good enough to ride with niggers!
It is an offence to do anything to prejudice recruit-
ing. That being so, will the Minister for Defence
instantly call on the responsible persons to cancel
all these infamous orders?

LAUGHTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The laughter of the Anzacs in the face of death
has impressed the world. —Daily paper.
AA’e laughed so well when merry sunbeams dancing
Through the high tregp made light and shade below ;
From bough to bough the flirting wrens were
glancing
And all the world had seed of dream to sow.
AVe laughed so well in summer’s comradeship
AYhen the sun kissed the lily’s fragrant lip.
AA’inter with silver look and aged wrinkle
Blows down the chilly way his bitter breath;
The large green rain, on bush and bough asprinkle,
AA’eeps softly where the year lies damp in death.
Vet we laugh well and mock the bleaker skies
Because the long days made us summer-wise.
AA'e laughed so well when life to dancing measure
Went tripping down the yellow ways of peace,
His blown hair lifted by the winds of pleasure,
His white feet in the joy-cloud’s sunlit fleece.
And now because of that high laughter born
Of the white truth we laugh black war to scorn.
x.s.w.
GEORGE STREET.

“The Old Soldier” writes: —
“Second Star” (B. 8/6/T6) is quite right, and so
was Winston Churchill, about the folly of classing
officers’ soldier-servants as fighting ifien. l have my
doubts whether officers of the A.I.F. ought to be
allowed soldier-servants at all. The practice is
suited only for a long-service army like the British,
in which it grew up. There the rule was that no
man was to be employed as a servant, or in any
other capacity that exempted him from drill, till he
had been “dismissed recruits’ drill.” In fact, he
had to be doing duty long enough to satisfy his cap-
tain and the adjutant that he was an efficient
soldier. He had to go through the annual training
and course of musketry, and (in the artillery) the
annual gunnery course. That’s a little less accom-
modating than letting men off their recruits’ drill
because officers are too dignified to clean their own
boots. There are not nearly sufficient trained soldiers
to act as servants, and, anyhow, as most of the
officers have never been accustomed to the attend-
ance of a manservant, they would not miss him
if he were not available. Australia cannot afford to
waste fighting hands, but if nursemaids are needed
they can very well be found among the rejected for
physical unfitness.

“Chug” wants to start a Bloquage:—
What’s the use of C.M.G. to an Australian? Re-
wards of this kind were invented in a different sort
of country to please people with an altogether dif-
ferent outlook. Take the case of Hughes. lie
appears to be prancing about the British Isles, shout-
ing at the top of his voice in a fashion altogether
uu-Australian. Nobody in the Commonwealth, or,
at any rate, nobody with an Australian handshake,
would offer him prizes for that. His speeches
wouldn’t win a certificate of “Very nearly up” in
The Bulletin’s Correspondence Column; yet
freedoms of cities and doctors’ degrees enough to
fill sugarbags are being flung, to him. That's all
quite right. He's earning British honors in the
British fashion by neglecting his job and making a
noise. But we want Australian honors of our own.
What’s wrong with an Order of the Dinkum Bloque
to start with ? When somebody has done an out-
standing thing which the whole nation delights to
recognise, let him be made an 0.D.8. There
needn’t be any special swank attached to it. No
medal need be struck nor ribbon pinned on to his
tunic; just the plain name given and nothing
more. Then the simple words spoken in the
street, “Here comes the Dinkum Bloque,” would be
greater applause than the shout that greets emperor
or king. Smaller services could be acknowledged
by the bestowal of names of lesser dignity. In
Australia the male population generally addresses
itself as Bloke, Cove, . Joker or Cow, with a few
other titles of natural nobility which are not yet

remark that Melbourne was being “music-
ally starved.” Other murmurs of discon-
tent followed. Finally
Professor Bill Laver
could stand it no longer
and issued a challenge.
If the starving musical
population would come
forward with their
purses open, and if Melba
would contribute from
her voice-box, William
was prepared to provide
a series of concerts at
£2OO a time —say £lOOO
worth altogether, to be
paid for by the subscrip-
tions of the music-hun-
gry. “The tide of our
great musical famine,”
he said nastily, “may be
stemmed by a little more
practical enthusiasm and
less idle talk. The con-
certs may be had by pay-
ing for them.” The atti-
tude of the professional
instrumentalist has of
course had much to do
with the famine in Mel-
bourne. For some time
past the beggar has in-
sisted on being paid for
his services, and paid
well —nearly as well as a
navvy. This has disor-
ganised matters, since so
many of Melbourne’s
musical cacklers are the
kind which regards a
soulful of delight as dear at two bob.
Laver gets it in one. The stuff is in the
store, and all that the ravening music-lover
has to do is to walk in and produce the cash.

“L.E.”: Germany is the home of the
elongated word. When I was with the
troops in New Guinea I was appointed
Chinese, German and French interpreter at
the British headquarters. One day a Ger-
man prisoner was brought in and I pro-
ceeded to interrogate him. I asked him his
occupation and he replied, “Bilderverkett-
ungsmechanismusarbeiter.” This bothered
me for a time. So I translated the word
bit by bit and I got the following:
“Pictures - chaining - together - mechanism-
worker.” Then it was simple enough. He
was a biograph operator. But till then it
merely sounded like two dachshunds going
through the mincers in a sausage factory.
He was driver on a goods train and rum
was his tipple. One night a strange fire-
man was on the job and Jim was dozing
off the Jamaica. As the train was rumb-
ling towards a station the fireman was at
his wits’ end to ascertain the number of
blasts to give on approaching the points.
After many urgings to his mate to have
some sense Jim muttered, “Give ’em one
long ’un and let ’em cut it up themselves.”
* * * *
The distress signals of a sea captain:—
The man-huntress is abroad. Our ships are in-
fested with her whenever we carry callow officer-
boys. Her practice is to book a deep-sea trip,
usually to Colombo, bringing her trap in the shape of
a brace of maidens, sometimes three. I know one
determined dame who undertook the responsibility
of scoring-off five misses between Port Melbourne
and Ceylon. The object of the dowager is to get
her protegees definitely “off” before sighting Asia,
and so practised and persistent is she that she
usually bags her prey. The young military officer is
very often a good fellow, but as a rule he is fresher
than wet paint where woman is involved, and all
the promiscuity of ocean voyaging helps the old
party on his trail.. The result is that marriages
are breaking out at Colombo like measles, and any-
thing in the shape of a parson can provide for his
decrepitude. If Johnnie comes marching home,
dear Sissie has a husband, anyhow. If he fails
to reappear, Sissie has a pension. From the bridge
it looks a callous and cold-blooded business, and I
feel like posting a notice on my ship, “Beware of
Man Traps!”
* * * *
Tuesday was the centenary of Sydney
Botanical Gardens. They were, in a way,
begun in Governor Phillip’s time, but did
not get a formal birth certificate until June
13, 1816, when Governor Macquarie “dedi-
cated” the chair hewn from a rock, and gave
it his wife’s name. There is a bit of still
earlier history hanging to the Gardens. One
of the so-called Scotch Martyrs, Joseph
Gerrard, occupied part of the land, and,
dying in a hut there, was given a grave
on the spot—somewhere alongside the creek,
near where the conservatories are to-day.
The actual spot is unmarked.
“For a time,” said the cable, “the sun
was in the eyes of our gunners, but by
skilful manoeuvring our ships got into posi-
tion and a deadly fire was directed at the
enemy.”
“Teller”: Yes, as Tiie Bulletin has
remarked once or twice, compound interest
is a powerful thing. Bill and Jim, both in
khaki, breasted the counter the other day,
and one—the financial member of the party
—asked, “Does this bank give compound
interest?” On being told that it did, he
placed 3d. on the mahogany and said, “Bill
and me want to know how long it will take
this traybit to double itself, so as we can
hath have a drink.” It took only two
minutes.

All the little niceties of sea-warfare are
gone, and battle is now plain murder from
stem to stern. In Nelson’s day a warship in
a big fight didn’t see the smaller craft
unless attacked by them; and as a frigate
had no chance against the first broadside of
a seventy-four, the little vessels were gentle-
men, too, and refused to fight anything that
wasn’t their own size. Now it is all differ-
ent. The destroyers run in and try to stab
the big ships; the big ships walk the
destroyers down and trample on them; and
CIRCUMSTANTIAL EVIDENCE.
“Well, I only hit him to if a sock!"
the creeping submarines go for both with
their torpedo fangs bared. There are no
stately surrenders with admirals in all their
stars coming on board to yield their swords.
Ship, admiral and cook are down before a
boat can be launched ; and their companions
splurge on into the light without pausing for
either tear or cheer.
For one brief, crowded hour the Ilun
Acquired a place within the sun,
But dearly for the boon he paid—
Bull’s gunners put him in the shade.

H.O.
A BALLAD OF UTTER BOREDOM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The shops are shut and the day's half done,
Arid there’s nowhere to go to and nothing to see;
My only neighbors are folk to shun,
And there’s no milk left for a cup of tea ;
The house is dull as a house can be —-
Dull as a dozen of pious drapers—
And, uttermost depth of the day’s ennui,
I’ve nothing- to read but the daily papers.
Through streets that drowse in the ardent sun
An empty tram bumps down to the quay,
And a dog too languid to bark or run
Yawns as he lazily hunts a flea;
Some people come straggling, two or three
With indolent footsteps, idle gapers,
And the crackling sheets slip off my knee- —
I’ve nothing to read but the daily papers.
Callers to cheer me there's never a one,
Though I’d joyfully welcome the cnttiest rhe;
The earth is utterly void of fun,
The moon of delight is in apcgee,
And how depressing is fate's decree—
To sit bemuddled with boredom’s vapors
And find, when I’d fain set fancy free,
I’ve nothing to read but the daily papers!
Envoi.
Friends, pray list to my piteous plea.
Come as you please, play any capers;
I yawn alone and —alas, poor me !
I’ve nothing to read but the daily papers.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
It. is an accepted theory that the Chief
Justice even of the Speck should be a
knight, and Herbert Nicholls, once a rather
fiery democrat, is the latest victim, after
escaping for two years. A son of 11. R.
Nicholls, for about 30 years editor of the
Mercury, lie earned shoe-leather for years
in tin 1 Post Office, and then went in for
law and later for politics. He was leader
o? the Opposition from J 906 to 1908, when
the Evans Government recognised his merit
(and incidentally got rid of a troublesome
opponent) by making him a Judge. Two
years ago he became, at 46, the youngest
Chief Justice in Australia.
Melba, who was a friend of Kitchener’s for
years, is one of the
few women who
ever saw tears in
the warrior’s eyes.
When “K.” was in
Australia Melba
was at a dinner
given by the
Chelmsfords. After
the meal she was
asked by several of
tin l men to sing,
hut did not feel
inclined to, until
Kitchener put it to
her: “Madame, I
have been an exile
for eight years.
Won’t you sing me
just one verse
of ‘Home, Sweet
I lome’ V” She did.
of course. “And if
you could have
seen the tears in
his eyes as he
kissed my hand,
unable to speak,”
says Melba, “you
would not have
said that lie was
hard and cold.”
G.J.R.
MURRAY, Chief
Justice and Lieut.-
Governor, S.A.
One Mother of the Gracchi at Colac
(Vic.) has made Cornelia look like a
Peace Society spinster. This is Mrs. Alex-
ander Fraser Ross, whose five sons are an
ensample unto the flock. Tascar Alan is
captain and adjutant of the 7th Field (Brit-
ish) Royal Engineers, in Flanders; Lieu-
tenant Keith got his platoon after Gal-
lipoli ; Corporal Alwyn, of the First Aus-
tralian Light Horse, also helped to keep
the Turk at bay; Sergeant Frederick Sal-
toun is somewhere near the Red Sea; and
Corporal Ewen Alexander is with the
Engineers.
DEFINING THE SPECIES.
“What do they mean by calling the Prussian
officer a ‘blonde beast'?"
“Why, that he's a fair cow, o’ course.”
W. S. Buzacott, who died in Sydney the
other day, was secretary to the Free, Secu-
lar and Compulsory Education League, out
of whose agitation came the present
N.S.W. Public Instruction Act. The chair-
man of the League was James Greenwood,
who before he became editor of the S. M.
Herald preached in the Baptist Church at
the rear of St. Andrew’s Cathedral. Parkes
introduced the measure and afterwards
claimed all the credit for the system; but
Buzacott always affirmed that the hairy old
opportunist obstructed the work of the
League until he found that public opinion
had jumped its way. In his spare time the
useful Buzacott had for over 50 years been
a ship-chandler, and for most of that time
the mainspring of the Shipwreck Relief
Society.
Basil Watson, of Melbourne, who stepped
off the Atlienic at Hobart the other day,
has crowded a lot into the last three of his
22 years. He had had a year or so with
Hawker, the Australian birdman, so he got
a job in the air branch of the British Army.
After serving his time in the workshops he
was put on to test new machines, and got
along all right till something went wrong
with the works of a brand-new 150-h.p.
biplane. They pulled him out of the wreck
with a split head and concussion of the
brain. Still he reckons he had rare luck
not to have been roasted. Watson’s idea
is that the British are not so slow in the
air as they might he. They have more aero-
planes than the Germans, and their latest
500-h.p. machines will do 120 miles per
hour on the level and can climb 3000 feet
in 44 minutes, carrying two men and a
quickfiring gun. Tin 1 Fokker, he says, is
overestimated; it isn’t as fast as the now
British machines, and can only carry enough
petrol for a short run, whereas the British
have a range of nearly 700 miles. His idea
of finishing the war is to send 1000 aero-
planes to bomb Ivrupp’s, and so queer the
Huns’ munition supply. But of course that
has been tried: didn’t six aeroplanes drop
bombs on Essen?
Maoriland retains vivid memories of Mrs.
Harry Rainey Parker, Kitchener’s eldest
sister. The Parkers owned Elephant Hill
station, in S. Canterbury, and Mrs. Parker
mostly ran the run. Her second daughter,
Ellen, made rural life her hobby, married a
farmer, and helps to cultivate the turnips.
The mother and one suffragette-inclined
sister are doing war work now in England.
A son is in Egypt on a soldiering staff job
v hich he got before the strife began.
On the retirement of C. J. Dashwood,
superannuated from the position of Crown
Solicitor of South Aus., his work was divided
between Dr. Richards
(Government’s legal ad-
viser) and Shierlaw
(Fublic Prosecutor). A
new man, A. J. Hannan,
will replace Richards as
Parliamentary draftsman,
and advise politicians
who get tangled in their
amendments, or want to
strike out clause 178,
without considering the
effect on clause 83, sub-
section 2 (B). By the
way, the brisk veteran
Dashwood, on being
gently pushed out of
office on the score of
years, celebrated the
event by getting married.
George P. Doolette,
who gets an English
knighthood, was a tailor
in King William-street,
Adelaide, when, in 1898,
Thomas Elder financed
an expedition to look for
pastoral land in the un-
known parts where S.A.
and Westralia meet. It
reported gold at Cool-
gardie, on which Doolette,
George Brookman and
others put up £l5 each
in a syndicate of ten, five
more shares being given
to Pearce and W. G.
Brookman, who went to
prospect. They gave up
Coolgardie for Hannan’s,
found Flynn and Tucker
on the Central Boulder,
and pegged out the adja-
cent Ivanhoe and Great
Boulder—about 400 acres
in all of the Golden Mile.
The syndicate, being
short of cash in 1594,
sent Doolette to London
to get some. Westralia
was simply unknown
there, and he afterwards admitted having
almost given up hope; but at last he got the
Great Boulder off —and then came the boom.
The common conception of Kitchener has
always been wrong. He merely disliked
fuss, and being a poor speaker shrank from
oratory. Otherwise he was a plain person
on two legs. Out in this part of the
world a few years ago he gave people who
came in contact with him the impression
of being rather tired, of having simple
tastes and a disposition to say damn. Ilis
qualities w r ere masculine. The mentality
was not striking. The face was that of a
man who thought slowly of one thing at a
time. There are various yarns to account
for his never having married, and in all of
them the inventors appear to have overlooked
the possible fact that he didn’t want to. Any-
how, the title goes into the family of his
elder brother, whose eldest son is in the
Navy. Perhaps the cash will go there too.
There ought to be a fair wad. It won’t be
soon forgotten that he sat in a gallery while
the Commons discussed how much they
ought to give him for his job in S’Africa.
Hay Frederick Donaldson, who went down
with Kitchener in the Hampshire, was born
in Sydney in July, 1856, in the old Craigend
House, which still stands on the highest
point of Darlinghurst, though its one-time
grounds are now covered by back streets and
terraces of hash-houses. His father wrns
Stuart Alexander Donaldson, who was the
first Premier of the Ma State after the grant-
ing of Responsible Government. Hay Fred-
erick’s elder brother, S.A., also born in Syd-
ney, became a parson, and died last year as
master of Magdalene College, Cambridge. A
younger brother, born after the family had
moved to the Cold Country, is Archbishop of
Brisbane. In spite of the handicap of being
schooled at Eton, all three won distinctions
at Cambridge. Hay Frederick, a topnotch
mechanical engineer, had charge of some big-
jobs in India, and was one of the boss
engineers in the building of the Manchester
ship canal. Eventually he became superin-
tendent of ordnance factories at Woolwich
Arsenal (Eng.), and latterly had been tech-
nical adviser to Lloyd George as Minister
for Munitions. A half-brother is L. S. Don-
aldson, who for many years fined drunks and
read the language passed up on paper at
Sydney’s old Water Police Court.

The icy fang of the winter’s wind put an
end last week to Melbourne’s most ancient
mining expert. Henry Itosales, born in
Spain, came to Ballarat in the golden ! 50’s
as a mining engineer, and made his for
tune. For many years he managed the
famous Uallialla, which paid a quarter of
a million in divs. without'making a call
Rosales was an elderly, estimable gent when
the Walhalla Co. was wound up, but lie
lasted a further 80 years after that, and
was 96 when he died.
Kitchener was 66 when the Germans "ot
him. He had done all his best work before
he was 50. The rest consisted mostly of
staying on top and breaking anyone who
wanted to argue the point. Among them
was Curzon, who was Viceroy of India when
Kitchener went there as Commander-in-
Chief. Curzon got the worst of it, and the
enmity between the two men grew in bitter-
ness as the years went by. Kitchener’s
strength was mainly of character. He had
as a young man an infinite capacity for
taking pains and in a nigger environment his
personality found appropriate stimulation.
He was just a forceful man, who in Egypt
and Zanzibar and Palestine had acquired the
habit of unquestioning command. In some
parts of Europe the same type of army offi-
cer is quite l common. Instead of ordering
black brother about he orders the local
inhabitant.

THE DATS,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cold faces still and white,
Eyes open wide —-
Wares fobbing loud to-night
Tell how they ride.-
Tossed with the floating weed,
Rising and falling —•
Deaf ears that cannot heed
Victory calling.
Flame from the rending gun,
Thunders of doom !
Swift the red lightnii gs run
Through the grey gloom !
Swift our steel chargers leap,
Uriii defying,
Driving their lances deep,
• Rearing and dying!
Deluge of shot and shell!
This must decide.
Death holds the balance well,
Weighing their pride.
Broken their squadrons reel—
Spur the doom faster!
l it! illy the flying keel
Speeds from disaster.
Ryes I hat arc open wide,
Still faces white —-
Coldly the wares they ride
Sob in the night.
Tossed with, the floating spur,
Rising and falling —
These shall not hear afar
Our triumph calling.
X. H, \V
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

The WILD CAL
COLUMN
Fihelly, who is administering the Justice
Department in Queensland, told a deputa-
tion the other day that “it was hard lines
that any policy-holder who during the
war was unable to pay his premiums should
have what he had paid forfeited. He was
quite sure other policy-holders did not want
bonuses or profits out of the misfortunes of
their fellows.” But what policy-holder is
having his premiums forfeited through non-
payment? Long ago the principle of sur-
render values was adopted in Australia, and
now under the operation of that rule no
policy is voided so long as it is worth a
penny. The principle is quite simple. Every
policy has a certain cash value. That is to
say, if, having undertaken'to pay in till
death or for 25 years, you want to withdraw
in 10 years, the surrender value at that
period is computed and you get it. No
. doubt a great many people are disappointed
at the amount of that value. They expect
to get all they have paid in with interest.
That of course is impossible. They forget
that, though they have lived 10 years, a lot
of other policy-holders haven’t done so, and
that those people’s wives or children have
had to be paid the full amount of the
policies, although the holders may have
contributed only two or three pre-
miums. The true surrender value can
be and usually is computed with scien-
tific exactness; in other words, the
actuary can tell the true value of
the policy on any given date. And with
practically no exceptions policies are not
forfeited until that value is exhausted. Thus
the office makes no profit out of them. It
is quite a mistaken idea that it pays an
office to forfeit a policy; the policy is not
forfeited while it is worth anything to any-
body. If policies are kept alive after they
are of no value, it can only be at the expense
of other policy-holders; it would cost just
as much to keep a worked-out policy alive
as it would for the office to issue new free
policies to its friends. There would be a
horrible row if anybody suggested that that
should be done; yet in principle and in
result the two actions would not differ in
the least.

There’s a lot of difference between the first
three lines and the last three. Apparently
when things went wrong with the seasons in
1903-4 the London got alarmed, and, having
still a good deal of dear money to provide for,
decided to sock away every available penny
in case of trouble. Things turned out better
than the very-English directors feared they
might, and by 1912 their confidence had been
so thoroughly restored that they disclosed
£135,000, which they had saved up for con-
tingencies, and which they didn’t think was
any longer required. All this explains some
at least of the great difference between the
20 or 30 thousand admitted profits of 10
years ago and the 70 or 80 thousand of the
present.
There have been other contributing causes,
though. Ilun the eye over this comparison
of 10 years’ movements: —
1905. 1914. 1915.
Notes in circulation £106,144 £7,155 £6,248
Bills, etc 667,663 683,358 711,268
Old deposits . . 1,258,090
New deposits . . 2,935,600 5,151,572 5,497,148
Liquid assets .. 1,644,597- 2,209,663 2,346,054
Advances .. . . 3,522,297 4,378,277 4,626,631
Premises .. .. 383,187 274,465 289,555
The old deposits which have entirely disap-
peared were "the remnant of the land-boom
jimjams. One or two Australian banks
prided themselves on the bargain they drove
with the depositors whose money they locked
up when they went bung in ’93. To the
credit of this bank (maybe this was because
it was very English, too) it didn’t try to
drive a hard bargain with people to whom it
was morally bound to pay 20s. in the £ so
long as it had a penny itself, and it actually
undertook more than it could perform. Even
after modifying the terms the holders of
these old deposits got 4| p.c. To replace
this money the bank raised some new capi-
tal ; also it has added £2,500,000 to its new
deposits in 10 years, and has thus been
able to increase its advances by well over a
million. These things all helped profits.
There are pref. shares and ordinaries; but
the former are now preferential only as to
capital; they don’t any longer come in ahead
of the others when there’s a limited amount
of profit to be poured in the trough. The
future so far as shareholders are concerned
will mostly depend on the management —of
this bank and of Australia. The field is
here all right. Apart from the Common-
wealth Bank no new bank has been born in
Australia for nearly 30 years; and there has
been a very large development of trade
during that period. And as the war will cer-
tainly give a fillip to manufacture, the
development of possible jobs for the banker
should be still more rapid in the future. But
what we shall really do depends on (11 pub-
lic confidence in the stability of tariff pro-
tection and (2) whether the Australian
workman will take his coat off. As for the
London’s creditors, there are two things to
give them confidence; there is enough wait-
ing in the till and in liquid assets to pay
them 38 p.c. of all that the bank owes them ;
and it has £4,916,186 in non-liquid assets
wherewith to pay the £3,868,608 of debt
which it couldn’t pay at once out of its
liquid assets.

This is a little concern which combines impu-
dence with money-lending. Twelve months
ago The Bulletin drew attention to the
dangerous rule under which the reserves may
not exceed a preposterously inadequate 1 p.c.
of the total amount out on mortgage; and it
was added that, not only is the greater part
of the money borrowed —sonfe of it on no
better terms than money at call—but that
even capital is withdrawable “seemingly at
a moment’s notice.” The directors reply
that capital isn’t withdrawable at a moment's
notice, because “if funds run short maturing
shares have to be paid out in rotation by
ballot.” But, they add. it has so far never
been necessary “to resort to the ballot to
ascertain who should receive their money
first.” Suppose there had to be a ballot
“to ascertain who should receive tlieir money
first”—that is, among the shareholders —
what sort of a riot would there be among the
creditors? To show on what short terms this
money is borrowed it is only necessary to"
say that on April 20, 1915, the money owing
on “debentures” was £121,231, and that
during the past year no less than £126,557
of “debentures” money matured and was
paid out. Thus obviously the greater part of
it is not debenture money at all in the
ordinary sense of the term; it is money left
on deposit for very short terms. Now on
April 18, 1916, the co. had: —
Share capital ..£173,768
“Debentures’’ . . 120,413
Other liabilities .. 4,337
Total i: “..£298,518
On past experience it seems safe to say that
practically the whole of this “debenture”
money will be called for within a few months
—say two years at the outside —and during
last year no less than £35,970 of share capi-
tal was also called for and paid out. Yet,
notwithstanding that the co. is liable to be
called upon to provide this very large sum of
money, no less than £297,l94—practically
the whole of it —is out in advances on mort-
gage and shares. The balance at the bank—
that is, the entire liquid’assets—amounts to
under And it is a co. financing in
this manner which prohibits itself having a
reserve of more than 1 p.c. of the money
risked in advances ! In these circumstances
The Bulletin again draws the attention of
the Government of Maoriland to the shock-
ing state of a law which permits a co. to
call itself a bank and take deposits, and at
the same time retain a rule preventing the
accumulation of a reserve of more than 1 p.c.
of the risks. What the co. thinks it is going
to do if there should be a financial crisis, and
it should be necessary to realise securities
under adverse conditions, Heaven only
knows.

to fall back on when in doubt; as to the
latter —well, a co. like this deserves to lose
its money if it chases around after doubtful
securities for the sake of an extra pound or
two of interest.

The co. was formed to take over and dis-
pose of the wreck of various Melbourne con-
cerns that foundered in the storm of the
early ’nineties —the City of Melbourne
Bank, the Federal Bank of Australia, the
English and Australian Mortgage Bank and
(in 1910) the Mercantile Bank. The share
capital is now £300,000 and there are liabili-
ties totalling £75,453. On the other side
are “investments” taken over £299.209,
purchasers’ balances £79,<831, and new in-
vestments £00.013; the balance is mostly
cash. The old investments include a Fiji
sugar estate and a sheep station ; and though
they give variable returns the outlook for
such holdings is generally good. Last year
the net return from these two properties
was £8035 less than in the previous year.
The first table shows £18,721 recently writ-
ten off. This is really an amount by which
profit in previous years was improperly
swollen : it represents income tax for which
the co. was held to be liable plus costs.

The fund to provide for current risks is, of
course, not a true reserve. It consists,
indeed, of unearned premiums. The contin-
gency fund is apparently a general reserve.
Anything left over, after paying shareholders
30 p.c. on their little £l9lO of capital, is cut
up among policyholders. For the past year
the premiums at tariff rates amounted to
£11,024. Off that policy-holders get £3441 plus
£1352, or £4593, equal to just under 42 p.c.,
or Bs. 4d. in the £. It was worth forming a
co-operative co. for, especially as sharehold-
ers also get 10 p.c. on their money, put aside
for contingencies (apart from unexpired
risks) another Is. Set. in the £, and give the
Chamber of Manufactures a substantial aid
which would otherwise have to come out of
the members’ pockets. Actual claims last
year were 20.8 p.c. of the premiums, and exes,
(exclusive of were 12.2 p.c. By re-
turning the balance to the manufacturers’
pockets this co. has done something of solid
value for Australian industry, and is in a
small way a national benefactor. The funds
which are rapidly accumulating are held very
liqiud either in bank deposits or in war
loan.

The foreigner gave this co. a bad time for
a couple of years; and drought, which
affected the salt harvest, joined with increas-
ing costs to bring the net profit for 4 914-5
down to about 2 p.c. on the capital. During
the past year the foreigner has had his
troubles, and, though costs have further in-
creased. “selling prices are being adjusted to
meet the situation.”" The fact of the matter
is everything has been moving up except our
tariff. 1\ ages have been climbing, and
though cost of production has thus been
advanced, the altered situation has not been
met by higher duties. The only thing that
has saved the Australian industry from being
squeezed worse even than it has been is that
probably as the result of more plentiful
gold wages and other costs have advanced
in other countries, too. But whether the
higher wages paid in other countries will be
maintained after the war is very doubtful;
and that makes it all the more necessary to
give this and other Australian industries
more protection. The old yarn was that
it was dangerous to make the duty a farthing
more than was absolutely necessary, because
the fat manufacturer would simply put it in
his pocket. The fat manufacturer has a
fat chance of doing it in these days. That
this salt manufacturer, at any rate, hasn’t
been able to make a fat thing out of his busi-
ness is shown by this: in 10 years it has
averaged only 7 p.c. on the capital, and the
surplus of assets other than fixed property
and plaint has grown less than £5OO a year.

And there has been no stuffing away of pro-
fits into property and plant. Those items
stand practically where they did 10 years
ago—there is 'less than £2OOO difference
between then and now.

Heaven only knows what is going to happen
to this little co. now. It was formed pri
inarily to do the shareholders’ workmen’s
compensation business, and that is still by
far the most important department; but it
takes also plateglass and fire risks. In the
past year about £lO7O of the total net profit
came from workmen’s compensation; in the
previous year there Mas a loss of over £701)
on all other business. Now the State has
set up an insurance department, and seeks
to establish the sole right to take workmen’s
compensation risks; and if it succeeds it will
be a serious question whether it will be
worth while for this co. to carry on as a
separate concern. If it isn’t, there will be
this consolation: shareholders who put up
only £1873 will find (after current divs. an<l
bonuses have been paid) over £4500 of more
or less liquid assets.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.—Advt.

randum, which sets forth the object of

holding of responsible positions for seven
years shall, it is suggested, supply the
i(quired qualification.

* * * *
Bodangora (N.S.W.), after cleaning up
756 tons for £2841, hands out another six-
penny, which will cost £1250. Dividends
have been dropping in every other month ; it
takes approximately 1500 tons, worth
£4500. to provide a sixpenny. The capital
is £25,000, in 10s. shares, so a regular bi-
monthly sixpence is equal to 30 p.c. Still
it isn’t reasonable to expect regular distribu-
tions, because reserves are not large and
have to be renewed by development, which
isn’t certain in its results. But, giving
all that in, the resurrection of this old
property is another small mercy for which-
N. 8. Wales mining has to thank the
Victorian.

Amalgamated Zinc, which has signed a
contract to take 4000 electrical horse-power
from the Tasmanian Government, and
secured the right to 30,000, with a possible
extension to 50,000, is now looking for a
site for works. It is negotiating with the
State Government for the lease of 50 acres
of land at Derwent Park, a few miles out
of Hobart; and, as there is every reason why
the State should be affable, it is likely to
get what it wants.

Mt. Boppy (N.S.W.) produced £119.508
worth of bullion last year from 79,540 tons
of ore, which spells a shade over 30s. fier
ton. The profit was £26,937, against
£35.416 in 1914. Boppy cannot afford to
handle oi'e of much lower grade, because
costs averaged £1 2s. 54d. per ton last
year, and the prospect of making them
just now is very slight. The dividend was
Is. per share, in addition to 10 p.c. on prefs.
Some of Westralia’s big gold-mining cos.
are in for a rough time. Since the start of the
war, prices of material have crept up until
now, under this head alone, it costs about
2s. 6d. more to treat a ton of ore than it
did at the beginning of 1914. To that has
to be added the introduction of. in some
cases, unnecessary regulations which meant
large capital expenditure; and increased pay
in various directions and the Scaddan
profit tax on money spent in develop-
ment haven’t improved matters. Several
cos. hitherto making ends meet will certainly
have to shut down.
* * * *
Westralia’s export of minerals other than
gold was maintained last year despite war
difficulties. Copper shipments totalled
£91.169, or £52.995 more than in 1914.
Pilbarra and Phillips River were the chief
producers. Tin exports at 429 tons, worth
£5742, showed an increase of 66 tons com-
pared with the previous year’s figures. Pro-
duction is now confined to Greenbushes and
Pilbarra. Collie is still the only working
coal-field in the State. Its six mines pro-
duced 286,666 tons, or 32.544 tons less than
in 1914, the decline being due to water
troubles in one of the mines and a falling
off in the bunkering trade. Silver obtained
as a by-product was worth £24 295, and
lead and silver-lead £39,032.
* * * *
Westralia’s Mines Department report for
1915 shows that tin 1 value of the mineral
output was £5.478.149, or £55,841 less than
for 1914. Gold accounted for £5.140,228, or
nearly 94 p.c. of the total. The total
mineral production is novf £130,383,124, of
which gold represents £125.258.154. The
average value of the ore treated rose from
37.815. a ton in 1914 to 38.415. in 1915, and
in Ivalgoorlie (which for years has pro-
duced more than half of the total yield)
from 34.205. Ao 85.405. Working costs were
slightly lower, but credit cannot be taken
for that, on account of the fires and floods
of the previous year. The number of men
engaged in all classes of mining totalled
12,253, a decrease of 921. The Call was
mainly responsible for this drop, and the
loss of good miners has naturally had an
effect on production and costs.
Evidence in the Edna May Deeps’ case
disclosed the varying methods adopted in
the West of estimating gold values from
diamond-drill cores. One is to split the
core down the centre and divide one-half
into short lengths. Each length is crushed
and assayed independently; and then a
sample of the whole lot is put through,
h rom these results the average value of the
core is calculated. The other half is put
aside in case of argument. This method is
m general use on the Rewick-Moreing and
some of the big mines at Ivalgoorlie. Another
way is to assay alternate pieces of core. A
third, that adopted by Stuckey at the Deeps,
is to chip off portion of every bit of core
and put the balance back in the box. The
most accurate method is to crush the whole
of the core and assay a sample; the disad-
vantage is that no solid portion of the core
is left for check sampling.

I’or the last two years (Holden Tiidge

worked out,” and the management is now
scratching for ore. Tonnage has fallen con-
siderably, but with reduced-• exes, a small
profit is still being made. The co. has a
cash balance of over £ll,OOO, and as the
Board has been unable to get a suitable
show in which to invest it the chairman
hints at passing a portion of the money
to shareholders by way of return of cap-
ital. Some time ago Bs. per share was
distributed, and if a further return of
capital is made all chances of the co. pur-
chasing another property will be gone.
From what the chairman said one would
believe that Westralia had absolutely noth-
ing to offer in gold-mining ventures. Yet
since the Golden Ridge announced that it
was a buyer of new or old mines, Mel-
bourne cos. have secured the -Edna May,
Edna May Central. Pinnacles, Riverina
South, Leonora Blocks and a host of out-
back leases, while a Ivalgoorlie co. got the
New Boddington.

Extract from a Gwalia Central (W.A.)
report, which seems to indicate that this
boosting co. is at it again:—
Have struck magnificent gold at the end of No. 4
shaft drive. Looks like the beginning of a line
shoot of ore going several ounces per ton. As it is
in virgin country the stopes over this level should
give a good account of themselves.
If they do it will be more than the co. has
ever been able to do before, despite hifalutin’
reports and dazzling developments.
*. -X- * *
Mount Cutlibert’s smelting account for the
past half-year is not over-cheerful. Blister
copper worth £48,551 was won, but £81,740
went in smelting charges, railage, refining
and realisation, leaving about £17,000 for
mining costs, etc. And as mining costs, etc.,
including the outlay on 1860 ft. of develop-
ment and driving, interest bill and office
exes., ran to over £23,000. there was a loss of
£0377. Cash in hand has simmered down
to £529, and this mite, with £14,506 of
copper on hand and in transit, is what the
co. has in liquid assets against liabilities of
£20,922. Of course, the big prop in the
chow is the ore reserves, which Powell,
senior, figures at 158.500 tons, containing
12,800 tons copper. The Cuthbert mine is
the plum of the claims with its 140,000 tons
of 7 p.c. ore developed, leaving Ivalkadoon to
provide the sweetening with 10,000 tons of
124 p.c. stuff. What put the hands back
financially was the block on the railways when
the drought broke, and the discovery, made
after smelting had begun, that the type of
furnace suited for the Mount Elliott ore
wasn’t the right fit for the Cuthbert product.
The co. should have its own smelter at work
about August, so it will have to bridge the
gap between the end of its smelting con-
tract with Mount Elliott and the time when
its own plant can be blown in. With a new
smelter to pay for, and stocks wanted,
finance must still be a problem. But Percy
Pigott and the Powers don’t seem to worry
half as much over this nigger on the fence
as some of their faithful followers do.
* * * *
Charters Towers feels no better. For the
first five months of this year it paid only
£6484 in dividends and made calls amount-
ing to £5OOO. Bonnie Dundee is the latest
disappointment. Speculators had the idea
that the big make of stone in No. 4 near
the shaft would continue. Unfortunately a
fault was met in the sink, and the western
end of No. 4 pinched. Clarks and the
Papuan are not finding rich bottom levels
either.
A smart Melbourne crowd, led by A. J.
Cleveland, is out to test geologist Montgom-
ery’s opinion about the Ben Lomond and
Great Republic tin formations in North Tas-
mania. Up to 1892 the Great Republic
crushed 1500 tons for 181 J tons of oxide.
Judged on a percentage basis this stands up
to the collar, but something more is needed to
shove along a co. witli a register of 150,000
shares like the Ben Lomond-Republic of to-
day. The start is hopeful: a new shoot was
run into at 425 ft. So far as developed it
measures lift, by 30ft. by 90ft., so it has
contents of, roughly, 3000 tons. If the shoot
pans out 15 p.c. dirt, as loyalists allege,
sufficient profits will be forthcoming to test
the large parallel formation on the same
lease and give the Ben Lomond a decent
trial. The co. has bought the plant put up
at the Penzance, North Dundas. Next month
should see the acid put on the prospectus
literature.
* * *■ *
The Lyell Blocks Board bravely decided
to poke a drill down after the shoot of ore
that is supposed to have been squared off by
the Lyell Co. on its boundary at about
1200 ft. As things have gone, it looks like
hari-kari, for the ore, instead of coming out
bornite, is speckled Conglomerate. Meantime,
punching north in the 1300 ft. drive, the co.
is in stuff as tough as jasper, and with no
ore shoeing to take the sting out of the bore
results at the other end of the mine. The
next move, no doubt, will be to bore over-
head to see if the shoot has been skied. And
if it is not found there enthusiasts won’t be
daunted, for then a big fault will have
heaved the shoot in some other direction.
* * * *
The Ml. Lyell Co., which generally finds
it can do the thing better itself, has gone
into the hash-house business now. In order
to provide increased accommodation for the
employees in its mines, it has laid - out a
couple of thousand on a 30-roomed pub at
Gormanston (Tas.), and is going to turn it
into a boarding-house.
Mining is looking up in the Sueck from the
shareholders’ point of view. Last month
there were five dividends and four calls, but
the dividends totalled Os., and the calls only
Bd. The dividends were: Mount Lyell, 25.;
North Mount Farrell, 25.; Magnet, Is. Id.;
Briseis, Gd.; and Itoyal George, Gd.

The Bruni Island Petroleum Co. which is
looking for oil, in defiance of Dr. Wade, on
a specklet of the Speck, has started its
boring. The plant can go to about 3000 ft.
The rough-up between I funster, of the
Tasman and Crown Lyell Board, and John
Nicholson, of the still flotation process, was
a willing go. In the end John was pushed
away because, although holding 205,000
shares against the chairman’s 32,000, he
hadn’t taken the precaution to see that a
majority of the shareholders would vote
his ticket. Dunster therefore remains in
possession. But though he may win a round
he can’t win the fight.
* * *■ *
That Bendigo is feeling the pressure of
higher wages and lower call-power is mani-
fested by the smaller number of mines work-
ing. The following shows the gold won in
the first five months of each year since 1911,
and indicates a horrible slump of late: —
1911 . . 71,1520 z. 1914 . . 84,2350 z.
1912 .. 69,3450 z. 1915 .. 48,0820 z.
1913 . . 65,7700 z. 1916 . . 34,5680 z.
The jump in 1914 was due to the New Chum
Goldfields, which raked up a rich patch
after long years of call-paying. At present
Catherine United is the gamble of the dis-
trict, and if a few plum-pudding specimens
came in to sweeten up the big formation the
lively crowd behind the mine would at once
set things humming. A lot is made of a
3oz. return by some tributors at the
Clarence, but the crushing was a drop in
the waste of barren quartz showing in the
mine. To give the field a lift Minister
Livingstone has donated £2OOO to the Tam-
bour-Major portion of the district; and that
is good policy, for only a lucky hit on the
side-lines will win back the investor and the
punter to Bendigo.
Mine-manager Gullock is back in Dayles-
ford after a turn at. Ardlethan, and the
N.S.W. miner hopes that Daylesford will
keep him. lie is again noisily hoeing his old
Nuggetty Ajax patch. Behind him is opti-
mist Vince, who is betting on early divs.
Well, the mill is on the rattle, and will soon
determine whether the vertical make is the
real stuff or only the brummy mixture with
which Daylesford contented itself until the
Ajax struck its decent shoot.
ir * * *
The Wallaroo and Moonta directors have
done the decent thing by their men in re-
funding the 20 p.c. reduction of wages made
during the last quarter of 1914. Copper was
then down to £55 or under; and the em-
ployees “to prevent the closing down of
the mines, promptly and loyally met the
emergency by consenting to forego a portion
of their wages.” Now that prices and mar-
kets are quite otherwise, the co. makes it
up again. The amount is about £14,000.

SIGNS OF BILIOUSNESS.
When your tongue is coated evenly all
over with a whitish-yellow color and your
breath is bad the chances are that you are
bilious. If you also have indigestion, con-
stipation. headache aftd a general ill feeling,
there is scarcely a doubt about it. Your liver
needs attention.
Harsh cathartics which purge violently
and then produce a re-action only aggra-
vate the trouble. Gentle assistance is what
nature needs to establish regular habits of
health, and Pinkettes, the little pink laxa-
tive pills, free from harmful or habit-form-
ing drugs, meet the requirements most admi-
rably.
Send for a free trial sample to the Dr.

ing The Bclletiv Pinkettes are Is. a
bottle at all medicine dealers. —Advt.
