Sydney, June 12, 1916.
Xly Heap. Akini.—
It is good uews that old friend Dick
Nevill, most tactful and astute of Governors’
Helps, has come in for a tidy income. Lord
Dick was too fond of backing a horse to
take heed of the morrow —so the morrow
used to bring its troubles. The surprise is
that the Marquis had so much to dis-
tribute amongst his numerous family. Twice
married, the old gentleman had about 13
sons and daughters. But some of them g<*t
to the Dream Shore first. The beautiful
twins. Rose and Violet, had tragic deaths —
one after an agony of illness; the other of a
gunshot wound.
Hughes Onslow, who went under in the
Jutland sea fight, was a brother of the loud-
voiced Captain Hughes Onslow who briefly
disturbed our Navy Board with his anti-
Australian attitude and now holds the
helm of the Revenge iu the North Sea.
Another brother, Xlajor 11. 0., a noted hunt-
ing man, was killed in Flanders last year.
They claim kinship with the sheep-growing
elan at Camden.
George Rex hung a C.B. round the collar
of Fleet Paymaster Share a few weeks ago.
He is our old friend who liked Australia so
well that he stayed with us for years as
private sec. first to Viking Rawson and then
to G.-G. Nortlicote.
Captain Verney only returned to the staff
of his old chief. Chelmsford, after severe
damages in France had made the doctors
put him on the shelf. A bullet through his
l ight thigh, followed by appendicitis, brought
our old acquaintance to the Border Land.
When the fiat, “Unfit for further service.”
was pronounced, Verney accepted the billet
of Indian Viceroy’s Controller of the House-
hold. He can take things easy. Four mere
A.D.C.’s obey his word and innumerable
Indian vassals salaam outside his door.
At last Lady Chelmsford must be happy.
It took 60.000 men to guard the vice-regal
journey through part of her Arabian Nights’
kingdom a while ago. The old petty
jealousy of the Denmans is now drowned in
a sea of cloth of gold. Joan and Ann
Thesiger, the two oldest girls, are “out”- —
though dancing men even iu India must be
pretty scarce.
In February.* 3911, when the Prowse-Brady
wedding disorganised the traffic near the
Town Hall, it was noticed by the supersti-
tious that the “lucky” orange blossom was
absent from the bride’s finery. Diamonds
galore and a black opal iu the pendant given
by mother (Xlrs. A. J. Brady) were hung
here and there. Local society—there were
nearly 300 wedding guests- -loaded Bertha
Brady on this eventful day with duplicates
of everything, from ropes of jewels to butter-
coolers. Now gallant sailor Prowse, skipper
of the Powerful when all this riot of money
tossed round his bride, has gone to the Deep
for ever. He was in elm ,r ■ of the Queen
Xlary when she sank with a wound in her
heart.
lias our old friend the aesthetic young
Flag-Lieut. Fisher—lately Captain—gone
down in the North Sea fight? He was here
on Admiral Poore’s staff, but hated the
society bear-leader tricks expected from a
Hag-lootenant in these waters, and got back
to sailor jobs. There was a reported en-
gagement to a young daughter of the landra
Greenes, but it melted into thin air. Lady
Poore used to call him the most unselfish
being on the premises.
The woman six-o’cloeker can preface her
Song of Thanksgiving with a generous word
for the Liquor leaders. In a scamper round
the (lolling booths one never saw women
worried or waylaid as they arc when Smith
fights Robinson for the “constitooency.”
Large and prosperous-looking brewery per-
sons merely rushed their lot to the
booths in cars. Leaping out, they beard,
more in sorrow than in anger, the steady
tramp of the six o’clock brigade.
XVitli her eyes glued on the goal—usually
a rabbit warren, roofed with galvanised
iron—woman occasionally took her whole
family to the burrow. . Fortified with bun.
it waited at the entrance, awestricken by a
steely glint in mothers eye which it wasn’t
used to. She was giving a final admonition
as I passed: “Yous drop the baby on that
kerb, an’ I’ll smack ther lot o ’yer when
1 done me vote!”
The last-hour talk about Russia’s mag-
nificent advance on tea had something to do
with clinching the matter —if one can judge
from chatter amongst Our sex. Curiously
enough, the parsons didn’t seize the Bear’s
hug of Austria as a teetotal weapon. In the
iirst place they probably knew that somebody
would ask them awkward questions about
the wine-bibbing French at Verdun; and in
the second place they might have had still
more awkward quotations thrown at them
from that very striking book. “An English-
man with the Russian Army.” He didn t
find it all tea, at any rate.
How undiscriminating is the grim Reaper!
He reaps the “bearded grain,” as the poet
remarks, and “the flowers that grow
between.” One of these latter, reaped last
week in Spain, was Xlrs. Degen, who before
her marriage was Xlary Dailey, youngest
child of the late W. B. Dailey, Iv.C. She
was a beautiful, joyous little woman, exu-
berantly healthy and spirited, who had won
a fair reputation as an artist. On several
occasions she had had pictures hung in the
Salon. When the great war started Xlrs.
Degen was living in Belgium with her hus-
band and eight-year-old son. They fled
before the invaders into Spain, and there,
in Grenada, she succumbed suddenly to
peritonitis.
J. S. Horsfall has passed away from his
flocks and herds at the age of 70. The
length and depth of the Widgiewa wool made
the old man a power in pastoral affairs. A
son married one of Edmund Fosbery s
daughters—the lady with the placid expres-
sion and the chin-strap millinery. A
daughter left Rupert Carrington a widower
years ago. Now her son, fighting in France,
is next to Uncle Carrington’s Barony. The
Lincolnshire extra goes past the nephew.
Joynton Smith, with the Areqdia and the
Carrington and the Hydro at Xledlow and
the Imperial on his hands, still finds himself
unoccupied, so be is taking over the Carl-
ton, the new place in Castlereagh-street
where tea-parties discuss the latest crino-
lines.
The Welfare Exhibition folks toddled up
to Government House the other grey after-
noon. One member of the party, photo-
grapher Friend (of Kerry’s), was heavily
laden with a portentous book of views.
XVhen the clock chimed four the fortress
gate (it’s mostly glass, really) swung on its
hinges. Then the great advance began.
Committee magnates of the Free Kinder-
gartens made a solemn trail round the draw-
ing-room, pounced on the hand of his Ex.,
seized Lady Edeline in the same way, and
sank into seats. John Xlaekay then unloaded
his compliments to the Stricklands for their
interest in the kiddies’ recent show. The
tome was presented to his Ex. at this crisis.
Peter Board retired to a sofa to sit alone
with his new C.XLG. Dr. Philip Jones,
Xlrs. Frank Davenport, general sec. Ellen
Desailly and the ’Xlurkan Elsie Simpson,
of Go-lumbia ’Varsity, were lookers-on.
Soldier Finn handed the company to the
Presence, and a lively party of juvenile
tennis-players came in from the garden when
the cakes were served.
You’ll get with
this the photo, of
XI is s Soubeiran,
enthusiastic joint
lion. sec. of the
French - Australian

This Society, which
says, as we do,

has done extraor-

Why? “I am an
T . orphan of the
I ranco-i russian war,” says Xliss Soubeiran.
"Who then can grieve more sincerely than I
for the sufferings of my countrymen?”
A toddler when the Huns entered Paris,
her father dead, her mother gave her life
to nurse a wounded soldier who was dying
alone, of typhus. “At that time all was con-
fusion : there was no organisation like the
Red Cross, and typhus raged amongst our
wounded. We had fled with our mother to
cheap apartments, for we had lost every-
thing. But in the attics above were those,
like the dying soldier, even poorer than we.
Xl.v mother went upstairs to help, and caught
the virulent plague. They found her dead,
my elder brother and sister, toddlers also,
playing with sabot on the floor. Friends
came. They each took one of us. I was
taken with refugees to Switzerland So
you see reason in my feverish desire to
help my countrymen now that our old
oppressors are again on French soil.”

A scratch team of Anzac footballers, only just out
of hospital, astonished J. Bull by their performance
against Wales and the North of England. Then
at the first faint breath of Spring they started out
with the willow. Bexley Heath C.C. challenged the
Abbey Wood camp. The locals were surrounded by new
flannels, a brass band and admiring friends. The
Australians strolled along in their army boots and
old Gallipoli uniforms. It was Easter Monday—the
day' before Anzac Day—so we could only get a
scratch team (mostly footballers), as all the officers
were on duty. Some of our boys hadn’t handled a
bat since war broke out. But they mopped up
Bexley. And then they remembered they had apolo-
gised for bringing such a weak team! It was like
taking candy from a kid I Scores: 42 for
Bexley—l9B and sundries for us. The locals fin-
ished handsomely, though, buying many, many long
beers for Australia after their surprise had cooled
down.

A millinery note from Egypt:—
The Prince of Wales sets the fashions for our
officers in Egypt. Anyway, for some of them. He
made his first public appearance here when he in-
spected us a few weeks ago. The only things I re-
member about him were his horse and an osprey he
wore in his helmet. It was half Scotch thistle and
half pen-nib wiper. General White, the Adjutant-
General, a grand old man, came in a slouch hat
like I wore on Sunday mornings when I mowed the
lawn. But if General White hadn’t noticed the ab-
surd pen-nib wipe others of less consequence had.
I was waiting on the railway station at Ismailia
(where H.R.H. has his residence) two days later,
and a large, outsize colonel walked on with a tiny
osprey in his helmet. I turned Republican on the
spot.

The other morning the learned Xleredith
Atkinson told the Study Circle (which had
taken its grey matter to Government
House) that present-day woman had a lot
to be thankful for. The damsels of the middle
ages not only spun the yarn, but likewise
ploughed the soil when their legal lords gave
the order. After the chat a lady arose and
inquired in icy tones why men still sold
tapes in drapers’ shops. The orator shook
his head. He didn’t know. And the click
of knitting-needles—the Strickland girls
leading the chorus —fell upon the listening
ea r.

E. Gard Trouton, a prominent Allies’
Day committee mail, came down from the
Blue Hills for the big wind-up corroboree.
His friend, A. L. Blythe, another hefty
worker—his brother, by the way, belongs to
the Gloucesters, who did some trench storm-
ing in France last week —isn’t back from a
Xlaoriland trip yet. Of £143,500 handed
out to our Allies, a big whack, £57.000,
went to the Poles.
Xlrs. W. XVeston. who owns one of the
handsomest mops of titian hair in our Best
Suburbs, has gone off in the Niagara. She
hopes to meet hubby, a surgeon on active
service, somewhere on the other side.
St. James's verger had to dust the has-
socks on Saturday for the wedding of lima
May, whose father used to be a manager
in Anthony Horderns’. The ’groom.
Horace John Cornish, is a son of a school-
inspector. Autumn leaves and white posies
and choir-boys trimmed the event. The
dainty little bride’s white satin shimmer
was covered with terraces of Brussels net
embroidered in silky texture. The cake
was eaten at Sargents’.
A wail comes from Woolwich-on-the-River.
“We hear,” says a despairing housewife, “of
unemployment amongst women. I’ve been
searching registry offices in town for months,
and can’t find anyone sufficiently unem-
ployed to take high wages and an easy billet
under my roof. There are two complaints
• —my suburb is too dull for Gwendoline, and
my family (of five, including me and father)
is too large for Evangeline. I have sworn
to give out all the washing, except the tea-
cups. and I do the cooking, anyhow, not
wishing to kill my defenceless family with
the efforts of light generals. But the num-
ber of my olive branches (3) puts me beyond
the pale. One haughty handmaid who inter-
viewed me said she considered two people
(wife and hubby) a reasonable family.”
What we can do if pushed to it, is illus-
strated by the work of British women in
laboratories under Government direction,
and in herb-growing to supply druggists,
who formerly imported (good old easy-
going Imported) such things from the
enemy. Here, where the chemist is getting
hard-pressed, there’s a great opening for the
same kind of job. It is only waiting for
some one to press the right button.
X'erbrugghen’s string quartet made a
stately progress through Beethoven and
Haydn at last week’s Chamber concert. A
curtain lecture from the Director prepared
the company for its highbrow entertainment.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, June 12, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Playhouse was opened with a flourish
of bare shoulders on Saturday night. It was
socially the biggest first-night we have seen
since the war began. As the proceeds were
to be cashboxed for the French Red Cross
all the Governors came. The G.-G. and his
party were in the pen on the right hand and
Governor Stanley and his lot on the left.
War worries, or the climate, have thinned
down our once-plump G.-G. He looked
slender and military in his evening clothes
and a close haircut gave him some of the
aspect of a sheep in October. Lady Helen,
in black, veiled her white shoulders in a
gauzy scarf of petunia-pink. Little Doris
Blackwood was demure as a mouse in
’possum-colored velvet with a grey snood
round her hair. There was a cluster of
khaki uniforms about her chair at times.
The Stanley dame sat in black velvet jug-
gling a twinkling lorgnette—an old lady's
implement which somehow failed to make
her look severe. Her hair was wreathed in
gold leaves and her young face in smiles.
The newest young Gentlielp was in attend-
ance. He wears very jolly Irish blue eyes
and a Charlie Chaplin moustache. In these
sombre days I think these bright young
tradesmen should wear Methody whiskers,
ttnd so tone themselves down.
Gregan McMahon, in the hour of his
triumph, stuttered nervously over his lines,
and the voice of. the prompter was loud
as the band. Melba sat in a centre box
resplendent in black and diamonds and agog
with excitement. She has made a personal
hobby of the new Playhouse. There were
grey-haired soldiers in attendance, and secre-
tary Tommy Cochran hovered like a beauti-
ful bird in the background. He looked pale
and exhausted but happy—oh, so happy!
I must tell you about the special little
boxes at the back of the dress-circle. There
are a dozen or more in a row, right at the
extreme hack of the circle and on a slightly
higher level. If the rest of the audience feel
curious about the occupants they have to
turn right round and stare shamelessly. Even
then the boxes are latticed with hardwood —
which is still new and scented "with the
forest. Whatever may happen later, on this
first occasion the Toorakians who occupied
them sat up and looked the whole world in
the face through the slats. The Mayoress
gleamed behind very round spectacles and in
white satin, while the Madden dame looked
ghastly pale in paddygreen. Lawyer Mar-
shall Lyle occupied a box and bachelor Dr.
Gould, from Toorak. had a theatre party in
the centre circle. He has just joined the
Reps., and yearns to play Othello. Dr.
O’Hara was faithful to the stalls, and
brought his usual large girl-party.
The theatre is daintily fixed. The light is
filtered on to the white ceiling by alabaster
shades, and there is a clean smell in the air
that tells you the Playhouse’s future is still
before it.
The amateur actresses stuck severely to
black clothes of excellent cut, and the first
stage setting of cerise-tinted walls and cinna-
mon-gold curtains provided a real picture
background. One conspicuous “prop.” was a
giant petunia lampshade stretched to a fas-
cinating shape. Some discerning soul said
it had “beautiful hips”—and really it did
suggest the marvellous curves of a head-
milliner when she is corseted in all her glory
at the firm’s expense. The front of the
.stage was dressed in quantities of potted
palm—the potted palm that George Ade con-
sidered to be the hall-mark of fashionable
amateurism.
And I had almost forgotten! The play
was “Man and Superman.”
The State vice-regals are giving an even-
ing party on Thursday. Nothing frivolous,
y’ know —just a family gathering of the
Red Cross brethren and their sisters.
Lady Helen is to pronounce a blessing
next Saturday upon the wonderful steam-
laundry presented to the Wirth Park Rest
Home, in St. Kilda-road. by the employees of
the Colonial Ammunition Factory. This
extra cost over £lBOO to build, and the
money came from wages. It’s a gift to be
uroud of.
fielding to the pressure of their own off-
spring' the family’s kid-connections, the
Leslie Quarts had a pocket-edition patriotic
carnival in tbeir garden. The little Tudors
and Plantag^ ue t s of Toorak came along in
swarms, and ta' e result was that the small-
change ’taken,' \ Vi Sen totted «P by treasurer
Miss Nvillasv, reached to within a trifle of
£75. The cash has b c - en invested in a piano
for the Returned Solo^ 1 ® and
the cheering of the gratified Billjim has
hardly subsided yet.
Mrs. Robert Harper nowai^aJ s puts most
of her Scotch energy into the -South Yarra
Guild of Service, founded last August for
supplying soldiers with anything the guild
can make, collect, buy or otherwise hands
on, working for no particular patriotic body,
but sending supplies along wherever the)
appear to be most needed. For instance, in
one recent month the womenfolk of the con-
cern gossiped so little that they were able
to hand 1000 garments to the French Led
Cross. Mrs. Harper is the guild s president,
which partly accounts for the fact that Lady
Helen dropped in the other afternoon to
register astonishment at the size ot tne
attendance and the visible results.
Miss Butcliard has been telling the
knitters how to make valuable dyes from
native shrubs. At the Arts and Crafts she
showed bags of wool, natural, bleached and
dyed in many colors, with simply-got stains
made from vine leaves, sugar gum, lemon-
scented gum, logwood and native mosses.
The logwood gives a tender lilac color; the
moss makes a warm red-brown.
The Arts and Crafts .are now going
stronger than ever on home industries. Mrs.
Oswald Syme, who is working hard as sec-
rotary, has primitive looms on view to teach
the fretting moderns the virtue of patience.
One is just two richly-carved sticks which
are meant to be stuck into the ground.
There is a precious Maori kiwi mat on
view. I am told that the tribal soul-chauf-
feur used to bless the first strand, and if
the weaving woman got that same strand
crooked or short, she was sure to have her
ankles caned and her best hat jumped on.
Artist Chris Lorimer shows her fathers
plaidie of the Glencairn tartan, woven by his
sisters from wool grown on the haggis-
strewn hills, in proof of how hand-woven
stuff outlasts the years. Miss Duff lias
house linen finished up from the original
flax by a female ancestor in the south of
France. She shows flax in every stage up
to the bleaching green and the loom. They
want to encourage country people to work
up in their own homes materials grown in
their own backyard.
At the Arts and Crafts rooms in Clyde
House, women are weaving all the
time, and the mere idler can see them for
sixpence. School flappers are flocking along
to get lessons, the spinning wheel being
for the moment more interesting than the
piano. Nervy women might give the hand-
loom a trial. It is a soothing companion. The
shuttles are like little canoes. Slip one, then
t’other, then “click” the beater with the foot
treadle. It is as regular as the three beats
of the heart, and all the time the pattern is
growing and the colors blending. You
couldn’t help growing dreamy and thought-
ful with the easy rhythm. The A. and C.
membership is only half a sovereign, with
limitless benefits from exhibits and a library.
The directors are also out to instruct
workers in the art of how to sell their goods
when they’ve made ’em.
Courage, sister! The Malvern and Gar-
diner Ladies’ Rifle Clubs met and shot
lately at the targets on the renovated Gar-
diner range. The marksgirls have not got
to the length of tying pink chiffon and ribbon
bows on the sights yet, but the sport is the
best yet found for showing off boots and
stockings. Mrs. Hall led off for Malvern,
and Maisie Howitt, after seeing brother
Alaric off to the war, came for some practice
for the same triumphant team. Mrs. Cherry
was the bright shooting star amongst the
defeated Gardiners.
Dr. Pat Leary is showing his cheery face
on the Block again after doing much hard
hospital work at the Front and fighting
rheumatic fever, pneumonia and typhoid on
his own account. Having beaten that
selection, he is barred from further cam-
paigning. Melbourne pals have been dining
the returned with great enthusiasm, and Pat
retaliates with after-dinner stories which
are re-told later at feminine tea parties—the
usual terminus of all good stories.
Some of the boys in a battalion which has
just sailed wei*e musical themselves and had
famous songsters for relations. The result
was that their camp concerts were first-
grade affairs and drew such swarms of sav-
ing civilians from town that the last- few
functions had to be placarded “For Soldiers
Only.”
Mrs. Peter, of Glenloth station, is in town
proud in the possession of knitter’s cramp—
a very honorable complaint closely related
to writer’s cramp. Mrs. Peter’s attack was
brought on by growing a dozen pairs of
socks a week for 17 months.
The first lady major in Australia is
Matron-Major Richardson, an Australian
uurse who is sister to a military officer. She
has had two trips to a place where the war
crackles briskly, and is now inspecting mili-
tary hospitals in the Commonwealth. Her
official upholstery for day wear is black and
gold, with purple touches, and there is
another gorgeous thing for the evening.
A returned warrior chants a Te Deum of
bis own about the work done for the sol-
diers in the London House of the Roths-
childs. where discharged warriors are pre-
sented with new outfits. The gorgeous recep-
tion rooms are filled with tables on which
are dozens of everything, and the battle-
worn soldier marches round with an attend-
ant and makes his own selection. One of
the Rothschilds, Mrs. Soiyan, is in charge,
with a staff of pretty English girls.
Eileen Williams, solicitor Morrice Wil-
liams’s only lambkin, has left for port o
London under sealed orders —something to
do with serving the Empire in a different
frock each day. A few years ago the pink-
cheeked damsel commenced a Paderewsking
career at the University Con.; but the in-
spiration faded early, and latterly most of
Eileen’s working-day had been spent at the
Glaciarium or on the Block.
Mrs. A skin Foster is convoying a trio of
daughters to London via ’Murka. She says
she wants to see son Ormond, who has
decided to do his bit -with the English instead
of falling in with the Australians. I always
suspect when I hear of this nowadays that
the young man has doubts about being able
to keep up with tlie Anzacs.
RODOLFO GONBALEZ, director of the
Italian Grand Opera Co. which Marlow has
engaged.
Adelaide’s official communique
Maorilanders have swarmed in the streets for the
best part of the week, and about 800 of them were
guests of the Cheer-Up Society at lunch on Tuesday.
Incidentally, one generous member—a lady—stood
the whole financial racket. Apart from the lunch
the Maorilanders found plenty of amusement—to the
great profit of this little Paris.
On Wednesday last we welcomed Lieut. Throssell,
whose V.C. was gazetted to the credit of Westralia,
because he was serving with the 10th Light Horse
from that State when he won it; but he’s South
Aus. this far —lie was educated at Prince Alfred
College. Port Adelaide shook him officially by the
hand when he got off the Indarra, and then his old
school took him in hand. Past and present head-
masters and Premier Vaughan (an old P.A.C. boy)
were responsible for the speeches, which were cut
to a sane length.
Lieut. Leslie Harvey has won the Military Cross.
He and his brother —sons of the late James Harvey—-
enlisted in the early days of the war. The Military
Cross one has just been married in London to the
third of the Edwin Thomas daughters. She’s a niece
of the late Robert Kyffin Thomas, of the Register.
The Lady Galway Gift Book, proceeds of which
are to go to the Belgian Relief Fund, will soon
be on the bookstalls. The work—literary, artistic
and publishing—has all been done locally and
gratuitously.
That fine old picnicking craft, the Governor Mus-
grave, has been sold to the Commonwealth at £2OO
less than the £6OOO which the State Government
originally asked for it. Officially its chief use has
been for lighthouse inspection—in which the State
has now no part—but it has proved useful in many
other ways; and that’s where the social interest
comes in.
Dr. T. G. Wilson has got back into khaki, with
the rank of Lieut.-Colonel, and gone on transport
duty. He was in Egypt in the early Gallipoli days
and had his share of illness.
Died last week, Henry Jacob, the surveyor who
prepared the plans for our National Park at Belair.
His uncle, by a coincidence, helped to prepare the
plans for Adelaide. Uncle was on Colonel Light’s
staff.
“Tommy” Ryan, the politician who demands con-
scription, has announced his intention of asking
Parliament to appoint a Racing Board of Control
and likewise authorise a half-crown totalisator. It’s
a curious mixture —sort of “Give me liberty or give
me death and half-a-crown on Staggerjuice for the
Hurdles 1”
Lieut. D. L. Davies, a one-time Renmark man,
has been awarded the Military Cross for “cutting
through about 80 yards of barbed wire and_ getting
right up to the enemy’s trenches in an important
reconnaissance.” He was holiday-making in England
when war broke out, and enlisted there.
A passable imitation of Noah’s deluge washed away
a good deal of the support that Red Cross Button
Day could otherwise have counted on; but even so
the takings were something over £l6OO.
Esma Inglis Clark, daughter of the late
Judge Clark, is now in San Francisco,
whence she sends news of her *.

Entrepreneur Nigel Brock and Mrs. Brans
combe went to the altar together the other
day. When she came here first with the
Westminster Glee Party Mrs. Brock was
Miss Marie Van Ilooten ; then she married
“Dandies” Branscombe, but last year induced
the law r to untie the knot.
Wilbur Struan Anderson, son of the late
Bob, of ’Change, has an appointment' at
Scots’ Kirk on June 17 with Evelyn
Macdonald —a very pretty girl from Perth
The wedding has no intention of
quiet. Evelyn is going to wear the loveliest
clothes, . pud there is to be a three-volume
wedding feast at . Menzies’. Bridegroom
Wilbur, who was associate to Justice
Hodges, is now in khaki.
Leonora Eddington, one of our real girl
sports, went off with her mother on the
Niagara to Canada and matrimony. Leonora
is to marry Dr. “Snowy” Carr, of 11.M.5.
Blank, and expects her naval man to turn
up at Halifax for the ceremony.
Captain Nigel Prichard, clever Kathie’s
young brother, is back from England. He
was one of the swarm of students who
answered the call of the British military
authorities last year for a hundred Austra-
lian medical fledglings to fill lieutenancies
in the R.A.M.C.; and his services lately
gained him an extra star and a trip home.
He is going to marry Yera Mathews, a Gee-
long girl. As for novelist Ivathie, she is
settled in Melbourne for the time being, lay-
ing the foundations for a few more little
things like her £250 prize-winner. She
may go back to England later on, war or no
war.
“Rosna.” gossiping from London, tells me
that one of the Yerco medical clan of Adel-
aide, Capt. Joseph Stanley to wit, who is
with the Ist Australian Stationary Hospi-
tal, has given notice that he will require a
parson's help shortly. The other sufferer is
Ellis Dorothy AYillis, daughter of A. 0.
Willis, once of Geelong and Perth.
Beryl Atkinson became Mrs. Blits at
Scots’ Church on Monday last. She chose
the dinner-hour for the ceremony; so only
those who skipped several courses were able
to see her wedding gown of satin and lace.
Beryl will go to .Java with her bridegroom.
Dr. Douglas Shields and his wife, who now
dwell in Park Lane, I.ondon, are mourning
the death of their only daughter Marie.
Frank Elliott, the Drury Tmne mummer,
who is such a beautifully-tailored District
Attorney in “On Trial,” is the husband of a
Melbourne girl—Florence, daughter of L. F.
Parsons, of the firm that has been mentioned
so often in the papers lately in connection
with the “black” groceries dispute.
Miss Fabian, the “On Trial” lady, gave
a tea party at the Oriental last week. She
wore an ultra-fashionable pink cloth dress
that almost crinolined, and the best dressers
of Melbourne devoured it with their eyes
while their mouths were full of straw-
berries. Though she had suffered a fall
from her horse- on the blocks of Flinders-
street that foggy morning the hostess
seemed gloriously unflurried as she floated
around. Evidently her horse exercise keeps
her very fit. The very English actress
has “adopted” South Africa for the last
two years, and she talks like a modern novel
heroine about her gallops on the veldt.
Rider Haggard and a particularly fascinat-

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Devil”: The Tasmanian devil (Sarco-
philus liarrisi) has a bad reputation as a ferocious
and untamable little beast, and he certainly doesn’t
look amiable; but, according to Mrs. H. L.
Roberts, who has kept him for years in her pri-
vate zoo at Beaumaris (Tas.), and succeeded, for
the first time on record, in getting a couple to
breed in captivity, he isn’t half as black as he is
painted. The first family, by the way, was not a
success, for soon after the young ones came out
of the pouch the old-man devil ate them. At
least they vanished without leaving a hair behind,
and he got all the credit for it. Next time he
was not left at home to walk the babies to sleep
o’ nights, and they grew and thrived all right.
Apart from this eccentricity on the part of Dad,
Mrs. Roberts found the devils lively, intelligent
and affectionate. She keeps, or has kept, pretty
well all the native animals going, from the kan-
garoo and the Tasmanian “tiger” down to the little
opossum mouse, but liked none of them better than
the devils.
*** * .
“Sassafras”: If “Corio” (B. 5/11/T6) is
not very careful he will fall into the hands of the
forest guard out Forbes (N.S.W.) way, or the bush-
ranger, as he is termed on the North Coast, for fell-
ing undersized trees. Meanwhile, if he comes across
to the Coast he will see real trees. This one is the
record as far as my actual working experience goes,
but I’ve seen far better specimens standing. Just on
10 years ago Bill and I worked up a messmate on
one of the southern tributaries of the Clarence.
We took 11 cuts off him, 7ft. long (mortise lengths),
and each cut averaged 45 posts 24in. x 9in. If
“Corio” applies arithmetic to the timber he will
tally 495 posts. The sapling was felled Bft. from the
ground and the tools used were two axes, a crosscut,
a maul and four wedges. There are thousands of
tallow-wood and blackbutt trees standing on the east
slopes of the Dorrigo plateau that will measure
100 ft. long with a 10ft. or 12ft. girth, and as solid
throughout as a pro-conscription argument. One
hundred and eighteen ordinary sleepers were hewn
from an ironbark tree for the North Coast railway line,
which is only a dozen behind the posts shredded from
“Corio’s” shrub.
* ♦ * *
“Amanda”: Re the pawpaw tree and its
fruit. At Geraldton (N.Q.) I met an old chap,
sugar-growing on the Johnstone River for years, who
told me of another quality of this strange plant.
According to him a poultice of pawpaw fruit is a
specific in tetanus. He had a Jap working for him
who carved his foot with a cane knife. A few days
later Nippon showed signs of tetanus. The nearest
medico was called in and presented the usual
chloral treatment. After the doctor left the Jap
grew worse, and the boss' looked round mentally
for a grave site. At this late stage an old gin
suggested the pawpaw poultice, and it was tried
as a forlorn hope. The Jap pulled through, and the
most surprised man in the district was the medico
when he met the patient of Black Mary, M.D.

“T. 8.” “Corio’s” (B. 11/5/TG) red gum
that split out 134 posts was a walking-stick com-
pared with a brigalow that I and my mate felled up
on the Murgon (Q.) scrub lands. When split up it
guve us 361 sin. posts and 121 palings; length of
posts 6ft.; palings 3in. wide. There are others still
standing on the ground which would deliver an
equal cargo.

“Simon”: He “J.G.” and his algaroba
(B. 25/5/’l6). This vegetable is also known as
carob, locust and St. John's bread-tree, and is,- when
it is at home, Ceratonia siliqua. The name algaroba
is also applied to some South American species of
prosopis. Fred Turner, of the Agricultural Depart-
ment (N.S.W.), in 1892 wrote a lengthy illustrated
article for the Agricultural Gazette (Vol. 111., p.
235). If “J.G.” reads this he will see that 24 years
ago a quantity of seeds were distributed throughout
the State, and I know that here and there they
grew into trees which bore beans. A legume that
grows to 10ft. in circumference is not the sort that
enriches the soil, though. Ask the pea-planting
orchardists of Richmond (N.S.W.) what they think!
The algaroba may have points as a good, all-round
performer, but you can knock it hollow in the Aus-
tralian Jmsh to-day. What can beat our own black
bean or maple for cabinet work, wattle and pine for
tannin, turpentine for piles, silky oak for “flower,”
spotted gum for waggon-building, and stringy-bark
for being the mainstay of Australia? In short, I
say “Coals to Newcastle!” until we learn to take
care of what bounteous nature has already done for
us in this respect.
-* * *
“Tropicus”: The man-eater of the high-
lands of Papua, in his leisure, devotes himself to agri-
culture. The spear is laid down for the yam-stick
and the firebrand, without which hi'own brother can
do nothing. Assisted by his numerous sons, daugh-
ters, sons-in-law, sisters, cousins and aunts, the
pi actised murderer marks out the boundaries of a ;
garden in the jungle and the axemen get busy as
inclined. Various rites are next performed by the
old men of the tribe, in order that the crop of food
may be a bountiful one. Various shrubs and vines
are planted alongside the yams, taro, sweet potatoes,
pumpkin, sugarcane, maize, arrowroot, tapioca and
other native foods, in the belief that they will act
as a guard and cause the vegetables to grow vigor-
ously. The jungle plants, being used to life in the
scrub, are supposed to tell the domestic plants how
to please the jungle devils. In some tribes a piece
of pottery filled with charms made by the local sor-
cerer is buried in the centre of the garden, and is
said to be a very reliable aid to the harvesting of a
big crop. The labor is well divided. The men fell
the trees and fence the land, the women plant the
seed after the men have broken the soil open with
digging-sticks, and the children help their mothers
to keep the weeds down. Once the garden is planted
the women and children have to keep it in order, but
any repairs to the fences are attended to by’ the
men. Most gardens are divided into sections accord-
ing to the number of people interested, the smallest
child who, perhaps, only carried an armful of sticks
to the burning-off fires, being given a section in
which all food grown is recognised as his personal
property. The sections are marked with logs and
stumps, and disputes are rare. Birds are prevented
from attacking bananas and pawpaw fruit by the use
of rattles made of bamboo and pebbles, which are
hung in the palms and trees. If these are not
sufficient, the fruit is wrapped in banana leaves, a
proceeding that baffles the attack of anything feathered
excepting the giant black cockatoo. As brown
brother will never do anything systematically, a large
amount of ground is always wasted, and it is only
that the grower is blessed with extremely rich soil
and a plentiful and reliable rainfall that he gets any
return for his labor. The planting period has its
special dances, and the harvesting is followed by
feasts. Every year a new plot of land is cleared, for
everybody has so much ground that he is able to use
only a small fraction of it. The old gardens fall
into grass and weeds, but nobody cares, for there
are millions of acres still untouched.

“Tibia” : In the risky old days spider-web
was thought a sure stopper of blood flowing from a
cut. That the superstition is still going was
shown at Euroa (Vic.) the other day when a
young man broke into himself while shaving. To
stop the bleeding some web was put on the cut.
It stopped the bleeding all right, and it also
did away with the inconvenience of further shaving
on the part of that- young man. He died of blood-
poisoning. Probably if he had taken “Cangai s
advice (B. 18/5/’l6) and used brown-paper ash the
result would have been the sanfl*.
IN GRATEFUL MOOD,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve summed it up, and argued con. and pro,
And here's my verdict specified below.
Australia’s child I’m satisfied to be —
It is enough if she’s content with me.
Her drought-smit plains, her jungle, tree and vine,
Her frost-touched heights, the air like draughts of
wine;
Her fern-fringed creeks for fairy hauntings meet;
The matchless dawns her matchless songsters greet;
Her grand old gum-trees from whose branches stout
The homely jackass flings his laughter out
(The message that he preaches from his tree:
“Laugh at your woes, whate’er your woes maybe!”) ;
The teeming life that, as a barefoot boy,
Was my familiar and enduring joy;
Her plains, rocks, caves, her beasts and birds and
trees —•
All these I love, and thank the gods for these!
For that brave time when brighter shines the sun,
When no day dawns in sadness grey and dun,
When on the Bush a softer beauty lies,
Because part-borrowed from a woman’s eyes,
And part from Love —for in the Bush I found
The dimpled god, and in his net was bound,
And still am bound, and feel his touch, and thrill
To the same beauty in the same eyes still!
For favors such as these bright blessings be,
Yet once again the gods have thanks from me.
I am not cursed with any narrow creed ;
No cramping Faith my charities impede—
Broad as our land and gen’rous as her soil
When she responds to well-directed toil!
All men’s beliefs my interest beguile,
Provoke to pity, or excite a smile.
For men themselves, and not their creeds, comprise
Whate’er is sacred, to my Pagan eyes.
The hostages to Fortune that I hold
Are two tall sons cast in their father’s mould.
To them descends of me th’ immortal part—
The brain to think with, and the feeling heart;
Forth to the future with my blessings sent
To learn new virtues and new “sins” invent!
No other life I look for after death—
I shall live doubly while my sons have breath,
And down the years while e’er my line shall last
My ego reach from out the long-dead Past.
For Land, Wife, Sons and sane philosophy
I thank the gods, if gods there chance to be !
N.S.W.

“Ding Bat”: Although the Maoriland kiwi
has come to be looked upon as almost extinct, here
where I live at Kopaki the bird is present
in abundance. I got 231 acres of bush
down the other day, and since the burn
have seen hundreds of the curious little chaps
about swampy gullies. I caught two different
species, and, of course, let them go again. What
interests me most is how these birds missed being
burnt in the fire, because their holes are not deep
nor so consti’ucted that the inhabitants would live
through such heat.
* * * *
“Six and Eight”: If in answer to
“L.W.G.’s” question (B. 27/4/’l6) it may be cor-
rectly affirmed that the Clarence and its tributaries
are the only Eastern waters in which cod are to be
found, then “Bad Australian” (B. 23/3/’l6) may be
sure that in those waters only can cod and eels be
found together, for there are no eels in the rivers of
the Western watershed. The swamps at the head of
the Cox are but a stone’s throw from the headwaters
of the Turon, a tributary of the Macquarie. The
sources of the Wollondilly and those of the Aber-
crombie, a tributary of the Lachlan, are closer still.
In those swamps and the Wollondilly I have caught
eels in plenty, but neither in the Turon nor the
Abercrombie nor in any other Western stream have
1 ever even heard of an eel being caught, and 1
have fished in all the waters particularised for more
years than I care to mention above a Sunday whisper.
* * * *
“ J.A.D.”: The skite about present-day
ox-chauffeurs makes me wild. The new-chums are
simply not in it with the old-timers of the Western
District of Vic. Some 30 years ago, in Camperdown,
or where Camperdown now stands, I saw Jack Powell
take an 18-team up a steep hill on a newly-made
road without a stop for a wager of £25. Two
of his best bullocks dropped dead when he stopped
them on the crest, although he never put a whip
on one from start to finish. But the daddy of the
craft was Long Joe Juice. Here is a couple of
tricks he used to perform, and I’d like to see some
of our present-day conductors try them. He’d place
six sixpences in a line, touching each other, on a
piece of smooth ground, stand off with the whip
(an old-timer, with a 9ft. handle) and flick each
coin away without touching the others. Again, he’d
bore a hole in a tree with an inch auger to a depth
of three inches and flick a traybit out of the aper-
ture With his lash. Although Long Joe could do
things with the whip that looked like black magic,
I never, in all the years I knew him, saw him strike
a bullock with the full weight of it but once, and
that was a horror not easily forgotten. The blood
simply spurted where the thong struck.
* * * *
“Wallaroo”: Pass the recipe to cure kan-
garoo skins so that they look like chamois. I don’t
mind swapping a pair of second-hand grandmothers
tor it.
* # * *
“O’Phimerty”: Let up a minute! If you
are planting an orchard, deep-sinking is lunacy,
because fruit trees spread their roots near to the
surface. The great thing is a stiff subsoil, not too
far down, that will keep the moisture near the top.
Other plants, such as the parsnip, like to delve
deep, and the best results are got by giving them
something to delve for. The solution of the problem
is that plants are like blokes —some like one thing,
some another.

“Casey”: Let’s get things shipshape
before we argue any further about “clam” chowder.
It’s not clam. It’s clamp. Any beachcomber from
Broome to Thursday Island will spell the shell that
way, even if you try to suborn him with a bottle of
Resurrection. For truth is mighty and will prevail,
unless, of course, the inducement to the contrary is
made extra special.

“Nanoon”: “Tiaki” asks (B. 18/5/T6)
has anyone seen a Chow albino. I have encountered
several around Shanghai and Tientsin and they are
even more numerous inland. I saw a Kanaka albino
in Townsville some years ago and a Filipino ditto in
Manila. Although I have knocked about Japan a
bit I never saw one of these deformities there.

“Quandong”: Let me have another chip
at the electric gear for shearing. I feel sure the
make of machine “Barcoo” used was identical with

gear.
* * # *
“McNorth”: In point of ferocity and
martial ardor the tribe of head-hunters known as
the Tugeris take the prize in Papua. Their villages
are supposed to be somewhere in Dutch New Guinea,
but all the- natives along the coast from the Dutch
border to near Daru, in Papua, live in con-
stant dread of the frequent raids of these black Huns.
A rumor that the Tugeris are coming will send the
inhabitants of whole villages into the interior in
search of safety. The raiders’ method of attack is
to sneak along in their war canoes and surprise
some unsuspecting settlement. No mercy is shown;
they kill all and sundry. The main object seems
to be the collection of skulls. These raids were so
frequent some years ago that the Queensland Gov-
ernment provided the natives of the island of
Saibai with firearms to protect themselves. Although
Saibai is in Bananaland waters it is only a mile
or two from the Papuan coast. Whites have gone
under to the Tugeris. A dreadful incident occurred
to a member of a punitive expedition which had
met and defeated a party of the head-hunters. The
nigs, made for their canoes, and one European, more
eager than the rest, pursued them across the treach-
erous mud flats, firing as he went. When near the
water his rifle jambed, and he stopped to fix it.
Before he realised what was happening he became
stuck in the mud—sinking. The Tugeris saw what
had happened, and, as there was nothing now to be
feared from the rifle, they went back. The unfor-
tunate white man was waist-deep and quite helpless,
and the Tugeris subdivided him, leaving the head-
less trunk upright in the mud before the rest of
the punitive party could interfere.
“0.5.”: During 1903, on Hughenden (0.1
station, owned by I. H. Frith, close upon 2000
eagles were poisoned, as many as 12 to 14 at a time
around a single lamb. On one occasion 18 were
picked up around a dingo which had been doped.
Hundreds of those birds were carefully measured,
and varied from 6ft. 4in. to 7ft. 9in. Not one
reached Bft. Through Central Victoria, North, South,
East and West Gippsland I have taken many
measurements and found results similar. Those
parties who talk of 10-footers ought to take a visit
to the Zoo or the Museum and study the birds a bit.
* * * *
“W.D.Y.”: “Hay’s” cocky, of Yorke
Peninsula (S.A.), entered for the bad cropping
stakes, must have been an isolated bird. I have
travelled farming districts in all the States, and
nowhere have I seen more up-to-date methods used
than on this same Peninsula. The whole district
could well be decorated for showing Australia the
best way to crop. Last year most of S.A. grew
nothing on account of little rain, yet Yorke Penin-
sula, with little if any more rain than other parts
of the State, produced three-quarters of the total
grain-harvest; and one is safe in saying that more than
three-quarters of the hay also came from the same
locality. Hardly an implement comes to market but
the Peninsula cocky bustles forward to test it, and
tw-o or three of the most successful of the men on
the land there draw fat royalties from implements
they have themselves patented.
♦ * * *
“Melb”: The Victorian aborigines are
now down to 260, and mostly half-caste. They
are spread over about half a dozen Government
stations, all large and comfortable . places. Since
Premier Berry’s time, the rule has been that they
are not compelled to work. Which is quite right.
Our black brother in the days of his prime lived
the life of the English country gentleman; and
having taken war and their country from them, we
at least owe them a life of leisure untroubled by
rate collectors.
“Bulloak”: “Corio’s” best tally of 143
fencing posts from one tree is in the also-rans. I
took 232 all 4in. x 6in. at the smallest end from
a red gum at Strathalbyn (S.A.) some years ago,
but I don’t want to have them on my conscience
again. I’m placing my roll on the red gum
as the toughest of all splitting trees ; but the posts
are dinkum once you have them. Next time I need
some I’ll stand them on the hobs o’ Hell and-see if
the heat ’ll split them. Ordinary persuasion with
wedges won’t.

“Pint Pot” : When “The Oldest Inhabitant
was mentioning the queer names of Sydney pubs (B.
25/5/T6), he forgot the Honest Irishman, which still
keeps the beer-pump going in Camperdown (N.S.W.).
Others worth mentioning are the Reviver, also in
Camperdown ; the Lily of St. Leonards (called after
a long-dead lubra) on Milson’s Point; the Yorkshire
Stingo, in Goulburn-street; the Cheshire Cheese, in
Elizabeth-street; and the Cauliflower, near the Chow
gardens out Waterloo way.

“D.F.” : Why is it that forest trees mostly
fall in still weather ? Those that fall in a gale of
wind are usually on the edge of the timber country.
The big old fellows inside choose a fairly-still day
to topple, and I have heard them come down in the
midst of a dead calm.
THE RUBAIYAT OF BILLJIM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Op out! Revalley through th’ chilly a ir
Wakes each bloke’s soul to grouch an’ bleak a
An’ hark! A blarsted N.C.O. now comes ’
To hagravate us more than we can bear!
Last night in dear old Aus. I seem ed to be-
Tarts fluttered round an’ all were good to see
A cobber said, “Jus’ break from ’em a while-.
An’ come an’ ’ave a drink along o’ me!"
An’ as I swanked up to the shrine of beer
A barmaid smiled an’ cooed, “Hullo, old dear!”
Then, as I stretched me arm a bottle burst
I woke, Gorstrewth! an’ found meself still » e re "l"
For each dawn-come revives th’ same old came
And o’er th’ slope the big guns’ greeting came-’
An’, lo! where last night our S.M. had slept
The crib had blewed up in a roar of flame! '
So gone is sleep with all its blissful dreams
An’ come for keeps this life of jumps, it seems
W’ith guards, fatigues an’ Gawd-knows-wot t’ do
Where shrapnel o’er th’ landscape hums an’ screams.
And look! Ten million blokes awake each day-
By night a few of ’em are booked for clay;
An’ this same dawn that sees us full of nark
May take me cobber Mick or me away.
An’ when I think of boys who now are dead,
Past ’appy days, an’ towns we painted red,’
Back in old Aus., an’ laughin’ girls they loved
It makes me think wot kind old Omar sed: ’
Alas, that Spring should vanish with th’ rose,
That youth's sweet manuscript should sudden close-
The lad who lars night in this dug-out sang—
Where drifts his soul this night—who knows?
Ah, w r ell—-it’s no use rousin’! W T ot must be
Just will be. And no prayers of You or Me
Or wimmen’s tears will alter things a bit;
We’re ’ere because we’re ’ere, t’ wait an’ see.
We take our charnce of what is in the pot
Until we are relieved—or bombed or shot;
Look forward to th’ day we’ll one day be
With those whose words an’ smiles we ain’t forgot!

“Copabella”- on the tricks of his trade: —
I can strike a light for “Burnley” (B. 27/4/T6)
on the match question. “Burnley” met a philan-
thropist when he bought local safeties at 3d. on
March 2. Local matches were then worth 3s. 9d. a
gross wholesale, and imported English 4s. to 4s. 3d.
The grocer was selling “below the market” —
probably “cutting” to draw trade. “Burnley” had a
good thing, and should have stocked up. As to the
sudden and general rise, local matches are a negligi-
ble quantity in governing the local market rate, the
daily 20 millions being absorbed quickly by a hand-
ful of distributors. Retailers do not buy first-hand
from the factory, but through the “houses,” which
charge “market price.” The latter is governed by
world-parity and the sweet will of the cornerers of
supplies. The factory price is, no doubt, based on
actual producing cost, but the distributor isn’t guided
by that —he buys to make money. About this time
last year there was a sudden inflation of the match
market. Owing to delayed shipments local holders
of the few English matches in bond forced prices
up to 6s. 3d., 6s. 6d. At the top of the boom one
house landed a large parcel of Jap. matches and
started to make a pot, distributing at 4s. 6d. or
so. That was a “scab” trick ; so another house that
had been “saving” against the inevitable fall decided
to “discipline the scab.” It “broke the market” by
offering its over-held stock of English stuff at
3s. 9d.! The investor in Japs, had to come into
line, and the match market once more became normal
or thereabouts. They call it Big Business.
* * * *
“Y.Z.”: I see there is an extraordinary
boom in fortune-telling in London ; and the
evidence of a remarkable trend towards the
palmist’s parlor and the den of the
“futurist” had smacked me hard in both
eyes here in Melbourne. In Sydney a youth
shoots himself to verify a fortune-teller’s
prediction; in Malvern (Vic.) a foolish
servant girl, told by a fortune-fakir that she
would lose her reason, worried herself into
an asylum within two months. It’s the war
fever in the blood of silly people. Portents
have become popular, all minds ate a little
awed and unsettled, and the wizards and
witches might provide a fat fund on their
own if compelled to pay tax on their war
profits. I know one woman who boasts of
an income of £lO a week from this source.
She also runs a servants’ registry, but in
another name. The facility with which
she picked up points from the tittle-tattle
of servant girls hanging about her rooms
first put it into her head to turn “futurist.”
It seemed a pity to waste so much valuable
information. Dunder-headed mistresses,
after crossing the she-seer’s hand with silver
and hearing so much of their own private
affairs, do not hesitate to credit the sly
dame with supernatural powers.
* * * *
“D.F.”: The Speck now produces over
2,000,000 bushels of apples a year, and
exports more than three-fourths of the crop.
Yet the first attempt at apple-growing was
not a success. In 1788, on his way
to Tahiti in the Bounty—it was on that
unlucky voyage after breadfruit which
landed him in a 3000-mile boat voyage—
Bligh put into Adventure Bay. While there he
had a patch of land cleared, and planted,
amongst other things, several apple trees.
When the French under D’Entrecasteaux
visited the bay in 1793 one tree was still
alive and had grown to a height of 54 feet.
But it was on its last legs and not expected
to live much longer. Even the local Abo-
liars, who have a whole forest of trees that
were marked by Captain Cook, don’t ven-
ture to point out Bligh’s apple-tree. It’s
a wonder someone hasn’t heaved it in as
proof that the Speck can’t grow apples.
* * * *
The oldest ship in commission is said to be
the Danish Tvende Brodre. She was built
129 years ago, hut, owing to the excessive
demand for anything that will float and
carry, she is still doing a brisk trade in the
North Sea. Put together at Merstal, she
is said to be sound and seaworthy still, ancf
her builders would he pleased to have the fine
advt. if they were not so very dead. Some-
one will go searching for Noah’s old ferry if
the war lasts much longer.

“Ses’s” hard-luck story :
We were talking about lucky finds, when a tall,
gaunt person with beer-buds in his whiskers chimed
in eomplainingly: “You talk about luck —lissener
me! I was ridin’ along on a cattle pad, when I
finds a heaper bones, with a swag alongside. Some
men keeper lookout for corpses, and overhaul any-
thing like that. But I was off it. I ledder constable
and a tracker out to the place. The tracker over-
hauled the swag, and in abouter minute he had me
like a corked-up volcano that wants to burst and
can’t. Strike me if that rotten old bundle didn’t
pan outer fortune! There was a bottler gold, a
vvadder sovereigns, and a roller banknotes —wrapped
in shammy and packed in a leather bag—altogether
about £7OO. ’Struth, seven hundred an’ me poor
as wood! That blasted swag’s haunted me ever
since. ’Tain’t decent snakes and gohannas what
does circus tricks about me; it’s swags. They come
rollin’ up, and I keep on tumbling over ’em. An’
the one I’m carryin’ gets bigger and bigger and
clings like a homeless blanky octopus!”
* * * *
Australian public offices are still as
devoted as ever to red tape, but as far as
Britain is concerned the actual and literal
crimson thread has vanished from the ser-
vice. In order to eke out the supply of
aniline dyes and effect a little war time
economy, white tape is being used instead
of colored. This saves about £l4OO per year
—enough to pay 31 members of the House
of Commons.
At a recent packed Town Hall meeting
in Melbourne the confident Larry Itentoul,
D.D., stood up before the congregation and
spoke: “As for the men of Anzac, I have
spoken of them in poetry and in prose.”
And of all that mighty concourse not one
man rose up to tell the holy man with the
shiny dome that he tarradiddled.
Lieut.-Commander G. 11. Dennistoun,
R.N., was handed the D.S.O. the other day
for settling accounts with a Gerhog gun-
boat on Lake Nyassa, Central Africa, in
May, 1915. A son of G. J. Dennistoun, one
of the wool monarchs of Canterbury (M.L.),
he joined the Navy 17 years ago, since when
he has spent most of his time in Australian
waters. When the present stew came to a
bubble he lent a hand to get the main M.L.
force to Egypt, after which he was passed
on to become senior naval officer on Lake
Nyassa.
Birdsnesting on an extensive scale is the
hobby of W. McLennan, who is now lying
ill at a station in the Northern Territory.
Sent out by IL 1.. White, of Belltrees,
Scone (N.S.W.), on a collecting tour, his
idea was to reach Groote Island, which is
chiefly inhabited by a Malay-Australian
cross of quarrelsome reputation. While he
was at King River the local Binghis stole
his food, and he had to obtain supplies at
a mission station. To the missionaries he
told his adventures at an egret rookery
among thousands of white-fronted and
white-necked herons and great-plumed and
lesser egrets, the fowls which yield osprey
aigrettes. Protected against crocodiles by
a blackboy with a rifle, McLennan’s custom
was to wade out of his boat in 4ft. of water,
and take what he wanted. How prolific
the birds are is shown by the fact that
three blackboys ate 100 eggs for tea, each
being an embryo egret with a potential
plume-value of two or three guineas. How’s
that for an epicure’s feast?
At 70, Josiah Symon, leader of the S. Aus.
Bar, has come back right to the “top of his
form,” especially in criminal cases. “Get
is still the motto for an accused
person, just as it has been at any time for
the past 30 years—and acquittal generally
follows, whatever the betting may have been
at starting. It is unfortunate for S.A. that
Symon was not made a Judge long ago. It
always is a pity when the Bar is not dom-
inated by the Bench. The last generation
of Judges Way, Boucaut and Bundey—
had (to put it mildly) a vast respect for
Symon s powers, and the present occupants
of the Bench are not likely to have less of it
—especially as the present Judges had very
little experience of the Criminal Court until
they began to preside over it.
Sydney’s old Parliamentarian, Bobby
Lowe who died Viscount Sherbrooke, has
had his memory revived in London by the
new war-tax on matches. As Gladstone’s
Chancellor of the Exchequer 40 years ago,
Bobby wanted to raise revenue in the same
way, but he reckoned without Bryant and
Mays match girls. They formed a noisv
procession and marched from the East End
to Whitehall, where they waited until
Bobby came out to attend Parliament, and
then mobbed him. Mrs. Casey, who headed
the procession and had the honor of knock-
ing Bobby’s hat off. is still alive and draw-
ing an old-age pension from Bryant and
May.
incent T.”: I’m glad to read that
Chummy” Fleming is still shaking his
knobby fist on Melbourne’s Yarra Bank
these Sunday afternoons. Twenty years
ago whenever I tired of looking' at his
pathetic, unsmiling face, his tragic mous-
tache and the fist that went up and
dowm I would turn and gaze at the dis-
giaceful pile of unearned increment that is
called Melbourne, sleeping placidly in its
smoke-haze, and waiting for Monday to
come and make it richer and more disgrace-
mi. I often wondered whether it would
another Sunday. Surely, if Heaven
had ears for Fleming, the old town would
be laid in ruins! But there it is' still! And
there is “Chummy” over the river going as
strong as ever! In a world that changes
a l too quickly it is good to hear that an
old-established entertainer is still doing the
thing he loves best.
“Viking” makes a suggestion ;
Why not State pawnshops? The municipal pawn-
shops of Paris are scattered all over that desirable
city, and the Parisian in need has no hesitation in
applying to Auntie for a loan, whereas we always
feel nervous about approaching Uncle. They lend
large or small sums at fair interest charges, and
the customer is not afraid of being squeezed. Court
proceedings in the various capitals, week after week,
show what bloodsuckers make this their business in
Australia; and it is most undesirable that the
widow and orphan of any killed or wounded soldier
should fall into their hands. In the days that are
coming there is bound to be serious distress, much
of it among people who have struck trouble through
doing their duty by their country; and we ought
to provide these people with some decent substitute
for the too-frequent three-ball harpy.

A new book, “My Secret Service,” by a
man just back from Vienna, gives a grim
detail of mountain warfare. The city hos-
pital, probably the biggest in the world
with 8000 beds, has 3000 soldiers who have
lost their sight. Within six months of the
day Italy came into the war, Austria had
80,000 men blinded. The reason given is
that in mountain fighting the chief shelter
to be got is behind rocks, and when these
are splintered by shell-fire the wounds are
mostly in the head.
A riian who -had been convicted on his
own evidence was handed a year’s imprison-
ment in Sydney the other day. He stopped
a detective in the street and suggested that
he should be given a drink and a packet of
cigarettes in exchange for information about
an attempted crime. Having been duly
refreshed and smoked, he took the sleuth to
a jeweller’s shop, re-enacted an attempt
that had been made to enter it, and then
announced himself as the guilty incompe-
tent. He told the Court later on that he
had no recollection of the distressing pro-
ceedings. When he comes out again he
should take up some system of memory-
training. Curates may be able to get along
with bad memories; criminals, never.
“Mulapsap”: As one who lived and
labored on the N.S.W. North Cowst for
over a decade, I ask permission to weep
with the crocodile over the woes of the
disappointed dairyman. Says the dubious
Telegraph :—•
A considerable number of district dairymen will
abandon business at the end of June, principally
owing to the scarcity of suitable labor and the
high price that is demanded by unsatisfactory farm
hands. Another reason given for this departure is
the heavy hand the Government has laid on the
industry by recent legislation, combined with the
wearying, monotonous continuity of the calling.
How sad, if true; but of course it is only
.25 true. Of the four reasons advanced,
the only true one is the last, viz., the
wearying, monotonous continuity of the
cow-calling. Other true reasons are care-
fully suppressed. Here they be: The
absurdly high price of dairy-farming land
charged by exploiters, who pose as the
farmer's friends; the extortionate rent
gathered by rackrenting landlords; the
iniquitous practice of selling cows and
heifer calves because of sky-high beef and
veal prices that tempt fools and needy
farmers. Suitable labor is only scarce be-
cause the grasping, squeezing, covetous
cow-cocky will not offer suitable wages in
connection with “the wearying, monotonous
continuity of the cow-calling.” The said
cow-cocky prefers to make cow-slaves of
his own and other folks’ children. Some
dairies are almost entirely run by the un-
fortunate State children; so the State
tolerates child slavery. It is a common
remark on the North Cowst, when farmers
leave dairying, that they leave because they
have made sufficient money to permit of
their taking life easy. Even the S. M.
Herald admits this. What a lovely and
luminous thing it would be if the Herald
and .Telegraph told the simple truth about
cow-cocky conditions of life and labor and
living! What a shock the butter-barons,
the paspalum princes and the cream counts
and their political parasites would get!
“J.F.H.” (London)-: The Australian
League. What’s that? Something, of
course, broad, comprehensive. Nothing of
the kind. It is bounded by the four walls
of the drawing-room of Lady Victoria
Buxton at Princes’ Gate, Hyde Park, Lon-
don. Its inaugural meeting has just been
held there. Lady Victoria is the widow of
Sir Thomas Fowell Buxton, a puritanical
British brewer who “governed” South Aus.
for a few years. Lady Victoria thinks it
is a duty she owes to her husband’s memory
to do something for the spiritual well-being
of the backblockers of the Commonwealth.
So she has founded the “Australian
League” to “provide” the money and the
machinery for the purpose. After Bishop
Thornton, late of Ballarat, had started the
proceedings with prayer, Sir T. F. Victor
Buxton took the chair and expressed
pleasure at representing his mother in so
good a cause. Then the Rev. E. A. Colvin,
“late of N. S. Wales,” made a fervent
appeal for men and money to relieve the
spiritual destitution of the backblockers
who were, he said, “far removed from
churches, clergy and the means of grace.
It was impossible, he added, for the Church
of England authorities in the cities and
older settled districts of Australia to make
adequate spiritual provision for their
brethren in the bush, so the only thing to
be done was to energetically circulate. the
hat in London ; and he thought the British
public should part up profusely. in token
of “gratitude for the enthusiasm 8D
devoted loyalty with which Australian
have stood by us in the war.” Others spo e
to the same effect. Isn’t it all humiliating-
First Ivalgoorlie, then Rockhampton, an
now Lady Victoria Buxton’s absurdly-
styled “Australian League”—all witlnn
few days. Is not that a shocking scamß
and a criminal libel on the Commonwealt •
The Church of England is the. richest
religious body in Australia; yet it ney
misses a chance to cadge. But Austral
might reasonably protest against .the nion y
being asked for as payment for its servic
at Gallipoli.
TAKING SHELTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“....and ran for shelter to Danger Bay.”
—Daily paper.
The little ships go up and down and potter to and
fro,
And everything is lovely till the winds begin to
blow;
The ocean’s like a kitten when it’s full of sleepy
grace,
But ugly, old and drunken when the greybeards
start to race.
There’s trade in little parcels to be peddled ’neath
the stars,
And smoky coasters take it to the towns behind the
liars ;
It’s easy in gnjod weather, but you’ve got to mind
your eye
When winter is the piper and the “stand-off” signals
fly.
It’s snug behind the headland, and it’s cosy in the
bay
When big-ship gales are raging and the bridge is
lashed with spray;
Let plunging liners face it if they've got to run to
time,
A wait for better weather in a coaster’s not a crime.
Prom Byron Bay to Jervis the little packets ride;
A message tells the owners: “Taking shelter; safe
inside.”
Oh, each obese director strokes a calm, reflective
chin,
And sailors’ wives sing softly when they know the
ships are in.
N.S.W.

POLITICAL
POINTS
In all this fulsome adulation of Kitchener
there is not a word about Itoberts, the
man who saw what was coming and had the
courage to incur displeasure in high places
by warning the nation to get ready. Never
a monument to Kitchener should be raised
but on it should be inscribed: “lie did a
great job that wouldn’t have been necessary
had we listened to the man of longer, clearer
vision who preceded him.”
Tasmania is exhibiting a fine example
of the insane results of having State and
Federal authorities at work on the same
job. The Builders’ Laborers’ Federation
recently got from the Arbitration Court
an award which fixed the wages of builders’
laborers in Hobart at Is. Gd. per hour for
a week of 44 hours. A State Wages Board
has fixed the wages for the same work at
Is. per hour for a4B hours’ week. The
half-dozen leading builders who were cited
as parties in the Arbitration Court thus
have to pay Is. Gd. per hour, while their
business rivals who were not cited pay Is.
per hour under the Wages Board. Relying
on the interpretation that the Arbitration
Court award applies only to members of
the claimant union, the firms that were
cited have now resolved to employ only non-
unionists and to pay them the Wages Board
rates; and as the bricklayers all work under
the State Wages Board and only get Is. Gd.
per hour, they give the cold eye when they
are asked to join in a sympathetic strike to
secure for their unskilled laborers the same
wage as they are getting for their own
skilled services. So, for the present, the
laborers have an award and no wages;
the trade is disorganised; and everybody
is giving three cheers for the chaos which
we call a system of government.
IN WOODROW WILSON’S YOUTH.
INTERESTED VISITOR: “And which, side of
the house do you think he favors?”
PROUD MAMMA: “Nobody. He’s neutral.”
Australia has just had another example
of the caste spirit that Britain’s bad old
voluntary system of military service breeds:
it finds that, not only are Australian pri-
vates barred by military pickets from the
two most respectable hotels at Cairo, but
ati order has been made directing that they
may not ride in first- or second-class rail-
way carriages—they must travel third with
the insanitary niggers. Under a democratic
system of universal service such an un-
democratic order would be unthinkable.
Justice Higgins told an advocate in the
Arbitration Court last week that he would
be dealt with if he gave any more impudence.
The desperado had said that if his union
didn't get what it wanted the men “would
simply knock off work one after the other
as your Honor suggested might be done.”
If his Honor has been misunderstood it is
his own fault. Beeby, who knows as much
about Arbitration jurisdiction as most of
his profession, puts it this way:—
The President of the Commonwealth Court has
ou more than one occasion definitely stated that after
conditions have been fixed by ins Court, so long as
men do not act in conceit and bring themselves
within the. strike provisions of the Act, they can
individually refuse to take work, and can bargain
with the employers for better conditions than the
Court granted.
Of course Justice Higgins knows the law;
but if this is the law, then the law is all
wrong. There is nothing worth while in
Arbitration if, having submitted the matter
to the Court,, either party can dodge an
adverse finding. And especially is no Court
in the least justified in giving preference
to unionists if the union will not hind itself
to obey the award of the Court. If it won’t
do that, what is preference officially sanc-
tioned for?
ANOTHER SCRAP OF RARER.
The central executive of the Political Labor
League of N.S. Wales has circularised candidates.
The idea is to refuse endorsement to the nomination
of any man who declares himself in favor of universal
compulsory military service. —Daily paper.
THE BOTTOM DOG: “ ’Ere, you get off! You
can't rule me! I refuse to endorse your
nomination.”
■V hundred years hence, when another sea
fight is about due, the man of 2U16 who
owned a careful great-grandfather, will look
at a yellowed newspaper of June 5, 1916, and
find alongside the roaring battle-news the
brief announcement that George V., on his
birthday, conferred the Order of Merit on
Arthur Janies Balfour, First Lord of the
Admiralty, in recognition of his services in
—what? Engineering? The invention of
some armored plate or explosive? The
development of a new idea in naval strategy?
Business organisation? No, nothing like
that. “In recognition of his services in
philosophy and literature.” And then the
hurried, overdriven man of 2016 will marvel
once more how it was that Britain muddled
through so long.
Last week's judgment of the High Court
in the priee-of-bread case establishes that
under the Mar Precautions .Vet the Com-
monwealth possesses a very large authority.
The essence of it is that the Execu-
tive and not the Court must be the judge of
whether the regulations issued are expedi-
ent. So the Court declined to determine
whether there was any connection between
the defence of Australia and bread at 41d.
which under the circumstances was ’a
very wise thing for the Court to do. The
judgment or judicial wink or whatever *t
may be disposes of a delicate question ; but
whether the Federal Government will
receive it with applause is another matter.
The Ma State six-o’clockers were up bright
and early last week for the great offensive
and flung themselves at the ballot-box with
such persistency that Bung reeled out of the
fray to decorate his hat with crepe and play
the Dead March on muffled hogsheads. Six
o clock won with an enormous majority. So
the purchase or sale of a bottle of beer in
N. S. Wales after 6 p.m. is going to be a
legal offence; and liquor will only be obtain-
able during the time the people are at work.
The decently-conducted pub will be shut
because the opening of the frowsy pothouse
was in the public mind a grave social danger.
All this is hopelessly irrational, but there
is nothing in last week’s vote to suggest that,
given a continuance of Parliamentary bung-
ling, this irrationality may not be afforded
opportunities to indulge in even greater
freakishness.
The “foul weed of militarism” seems less
rank in the cabbage gardens of compulsory
service than in the jungles of voluntary
enlistment. In Switzerland, two high offi-
cers of the General Stall' were removed from
their command recently for allegedly pro-
Gennan acts, but in exchange were given
responsible appointments bv the civil autho-
rities. In response to a popular outburst,
however, a regular military trial was
ordered, and among the seven judges of the
delinquent colonels sat a humble sergeant
and a lowly corporal. In allegedly demo-
cratic comrtries which still hang on to the
voluntary system, military service is a
matter largely for self-glorification; in truly
democratic countries like Switzerland it is a
plain civic duty like paying the income tax
and keeping the chimney swept.
Frank 11. Downer, of Adelaide, has
written to the local papers about conscrip-
tion of wealth: —
Mr. George Downer received last year, in respect
of rents from bis properties, in all £8192 13s.
Out of this he paid Federal land tax, £2572 Bs. 2d.,
and met other charges for taxes and insurance, which
left him only £2767 16s. 9d. Out of this he has
to pay State income tax £l7O and Federal income
tax £692.
However poor an opinion this paper has
of the “conscription of wealth” clatter it
wants to know a bit more before it feels
sorry for Mr. George Downer. The Federal
land tax is levied on unimproved capital
value; and if Mr. Downer got only £811)3
rent from landed property on which he paid
£2572 Federal tax, it looks as though Mr.
Downer was making ruinously poor use of
his landed property.
Three members of the Westralian Labor
Government are to have a run at the selec-
tion ballot for their goldfields boroughs.
Premier Scaddan, Mines Minister Collier
and Attorney-General Tommy Walker are
opposed by hungry Ljiborites who have
hitherto only enjoyed the crumbs of the
loaves and the scales of the fishes. It is
unlikely that either Scaddan or Collier will
be ousted, but Walker may have a stiff
run for his Ministerial screw. Tommy’s
anxiety to give the criminal, especially the
sexual offender, a chance to do it again, has
got on the nerves of the public. The Labor
plebiscite—in these goldfields electorates,
anyway—settles the hash of the candidates.
The following election is merely a matter of
form.
The new Westralian Act dealing with
venereal diseases is doing good work already.
A horrible case was lately dug up by the,
police. A woman took her 19-year-old
daughter to a notorious brothel and then
hawked the girl’s photograph among soldiers,
taking a percentage of the subsequent earn-
ings. The fiend got six months. Then the
police and the military between them traced
the infection of a number of soldiers in the
venereal “boob” at Blackboy Hill camp to
this house and to this girl. The Board of
Health thereupon took action and the

the profits made out of it. The mutual
insurance office does that. Three-fourths of
the “life” business of Australia is done
by offices .which have no shareholders and
l'ay no dividends, and nine-tenths of the
other one-fourth is done by one or two

In a field of four, .Tames Deany, the:
selected temperance Liberal, won the late
John Murray’s Warrnambool seat in the
Vic. Assembly by a majority of 1084 votes,
Laborite Alfred Pearce (secretary of the
Engine-drivers), lawyer Desmond Dunne
and engineer Percy Webb following in that
order. A peculiar feature of the polling
was that on the first count
Deany was only 35 votes
ahead of the bottom candi-
date, in a poll of GOG 4. On
the next count, Deany got
three-quarters of the second
preferences on the outed
Webb’s papers and Dunne
went down. Deany then
took most of the preferences
in the final counj and can-
tered in. It is claimed as a
victory for the local Tem-
perance Council, since the
new member is in favor of
an early-closing referendum.
Still, there is something to
chew over in the fact that
Webb would have won, leav-
ing Temperance at the bot-
tom of the poll, if he had
managed to rake up only
three dozen more No. 1
votes. Deany has a music-
warehouse at Warrnambool,
where the public may buy
anything from a copy of the well-known
hymn. “Why Can’t I Kiss You, Cora?” to
a genuine Guarnerius at 17s. Gd.
BATTLE-NEWS FROM THE SEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I cannot calm my restless soul to-night;
I cannot bear the sight of mirth and ease,
Hearing those shrill young voices that affright
W ith half-told tales of battle ’mid black seas —
Steeled shadows rushing through the whistling dark,
Blown smoke and flame and shatt’ring thunder-
blast
Which tell High Heaven that nations armed and stark
Have murder done and Hell is loose at last.
] cannot face the staring fact to-night
That 1 am roving free, a flaccid tiling
No warrior would ever ask to fight;
And for me cannon flash and good men fling
Their flesh and bone into the flame and smoke,
Yet ask me for no help ! —there is the shame
No word can soothe, no coward logic cloak !
They fight for me —and die in England’s name!
x.s.v.
UNTRAINED CIVILIAN.
JEAMES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Winston Churchill states that in the British army
there are 200,000 officers with 200,000 servants and
50,000 grooms.
The Empire in its need, it seems,
lias found a final use for Jeames;
No longer on the Turkish rug
He pedicures my lady’s pug,
Or, clad in plush and stockings, states
(With one eye on the mansion gates)
‘'My Lord, my Lord, the carriage waits.”
But still amid the dirt of war
He proves that grace surpasses gore.
At ho else could clean the Flanders mud
From master’s boots, or find his stud,
Or brush his khaki tunic, so
That outland foreigners may know
How men of birth should meet the foe?
VICTORIA.
RANN DALY.

WAR HAS LOWERED
the Price of Pearls by One-half.
Our latest receipts from the fisheries
show a drop in prices of at least one-half,
and this is enabling us to offer Pearl
Hopes and Earrings and Brooches at less
than half the old prices.
Breast-pins, too, for Men’s wear; Sets
of Dress Studs, etc., all are being offered
at the latest low prices.
It is no use shipping the Pearls to
Europe—no buyers. America buys but
few pearls, and so we arc offering them
to our own buyers.
It is good business to buy now, as they
must rise enormously after the war is
over. Why ? Simply because the world's
supply is not equal to the demand. We
have secured tens of thousands of them.
They are worth seeing.
