SPORTING
NOTIONS
“Trot”: What a chance has been missed
by Bung anrl the Waterwaggoners to help win the
war, give sport a lift, get away with a hatful from
Ikev Moses, and swell the patriotic fund! The
opportunity was/ sticking out a foot all over the Ma
State while the Six v. Nine elimination tourney was
on. Suppose Snowy Baker had announced a couple
of six-round prelims, between barmen and elders, and
a struggle over the Marathon course between a
licensed seller of the clirsed D-rink and a licensed
manipulator of the marriage Bonds. And also sup-
pose the old familiar dope about training had been
handed out, and all parties had worked hard with
their chins, and the proceeds had been advertised to
go to the Anzacs! Why, the Stadium wouldn’t have
been able to hold the crowd! Also, teams of Bung
and Wowser weight-lifters, wrestlers, tug#>f-warriors,
quick bun-eaters and hash artists could have been
sent on tour all over the State, worked up excite-
ment to boiling point, and amassed great wealth.
There seems mighty little enterprise in the Ma
State these times. But there will be a chance directly
to do all this in Victoria. Therefore will someone
kindly arise and awake? There is boodle in this,
Do you hear — -boodle —hatsful of it!
* * * *
Cetigne’s brother in blood and poor rela-
tion in the matter of stake winnings, College Green,
came to light in the Farm Stakes at Warwick Farm
(Sydney) on Saturday, when he beat 12 others of
the same mediocre class as himself. College Green
is a season older than Cetigne and had previously
Won in Victoria. Peculiarly, College Green is in the
stable which shelters his slick young brother, and his
trainer’s name is Green. As Mrs. M. Moran owns
the gelding there was a hint of Home Rule about
the victory. Cetigne’s owner, “Jumbo” Barnett,
went close to winning a double on the same day
with Lady Antonio. The lady arrived third in the
Flying, only about a neck behind the winner, and in
the Welter later on pushed Huthwaite into second
place, i Lady Antonio’s trainer is not Green. But
he can shout “Hurroo” just as well as if it was. His
name is O’Brien.
* * * *
The Sydney owner who disguises himself
as “J. Birks” was not getting any out-of-his-turn
smiles from Luck when St. iicdoc landed the War-
wick Farm Hurdles on Saturday. St. Medoc was* a
strong tip for several jumping events recently, but
failed to materialise. “Mr. Birks” gave a hatful of
money for the nag, anl was rewarded with a few
small prizes. Value, the imported mare which won
the Warwick Handicap, was also once his property.
He brought her to Australia, but she didn’t show pace
enough to head a jumbuck. Booth, the successful
Rosehill trainer, now lias Value under his eye, and
she has apparently decided to get a move on for a
change. Anyhow, she staggered past the judge with
Foil’s head at her throat strap.
» * * »
Though they probably loathe the name,
Banana land bookmakers should set up a monument
in memory of Demaranthus when the unexpected
animal passes out. Demaranthus won the 1000 sovs.
attached to the Albion Cup on Saturday by four
lengths, starting at 12 to 1. On May 3i the same
nag (14 to 1) covered two miles quicker than any-
thing else in the Brisbane Cup. That time Demar-
anthus obliged Ikey by carrying Bst. 71b. On Satur-
day he humped 9st. 121 b., which included a penalty
of 101 b., the distance being a little over a mile anil
a quarter. The perplexed punter is now waiting to
find out how the prad shapes when it crosses the
border.
**** - t -
At Adelaide Birthday Cup meeting the
totalisator takings were £46,891, as against £54,700
for the three days of the Adelaide Cup meeting on the
same ground. Last year, both were under £50,000, but
before the war both were up in the sixties. From
the machine the Adelaide Racing Club got £3500,
just half of the stake-money, and the Government
kept £1172. Cyklon again won the only weight-
for-age race, and this time made no mistake about
the Cup, whereby Mrs. Hawker became the biggest
winner ( £1500) of the week. Hiekenbotham was a
bad second with £6BO, mostly won by the too-
ligfitly-treated Polybius; then came “Rawdon”
Syme, J. V. Smith, and the local A. E. Hamilton.
R. M. Hawker, husband of Cyklon’s owner, had a
collection of mere second and third places.
The successful running of the Adelaide
Cup meeting and the Birthday Cup meeting in quick
succession on the one course has set Adelaide sports-
men wondering whether there is any real reason for
keeping up the rival establishments of the S.A.J.C.
and the A.R.C. Each has a. fully-equipped course
with all accessories, and, the military having
annexed Morphettville for a temporary camp, the
Adelaide Gup moved to Victoria Park without any
inconvenience to speak of. For the past 30 years
the clubs have had the good sense not to compete
against each other in any way; and it simply means
that each expensive establishment has been used in
turn while the other lay idle. In fact, each Club
races on only seven davs in the year, six of'the 14
being crowded into the two Cup weeks—and there
are a few odd days allowed to Tattersall’s, and a
solitary one to the Hunt Club. Meanwhile, a third
course, suburban, like Morphettville, is being worked
up towards perfection by the Port Adelaide 1 Club;
but, even so, all Adelaide’s , racing days put together
wouldn’t make up one a fortnight; and trotting and
the ponies are entirely unknown there. The objection
to amalgamating the two big courses—which means
abandoning one of them—would be (bar sentiment)
mainly one of uncertainty about the future of rac-
ing. Morphettville is five miles out; but the
S. owns the freehold. Victoria Park is the
People’s course, being on the city’s park-lands, with
a free Flat; but it is only leased from the Adelaide
Corporation, which might some day take a wowsing
fit and decline to renew. So, allowing also for
sentiment, which would make either club unwilling
to give up existence, it is long odds that the present
system, wasteful as it is, will continue.
* * * *
Alan McFarlane’s lownit (Grafton —My
Own) retired to the stud after the Birthday Cup
meeting in Adelaide. He has been an unlucky horse
winning nothing big except the Newmarket, and that
as a four-year-old ; the last two seasons he has been
second and then third in the same race. With form
fully exposed he has in Adelaide also run second and
third in the Goodwood, and second in the City
Handicap both years. On the last occasion he was
giving over 3st. to Hickenbotham’s Polybius, the
winner. The following week he gave Polybius - 2st.
and a beating—and then retired to domestic duties'

“Musketoon”; Regarding Waddy Boy’s
feat at Gladstone, on the Macleay, of losing a walk-
over through his lead-bag slipping (B. 11/5/’l6), old
sports at Forbes (N.S.W.) tell of a similar happen-
ing. Ted Clifford had a hot-stuff horse named
A.L.G. The prad was in such good form that owners
scratched their nags, leaving A.L.G. to canter round
alone and gather half the prize. But when jockey Col-
litts came to weigh in he was 151 b. under weight.
The lead-bag had slipped from under the saddle near
the half-mile. There was a large-sized row, and court
proceedings followed, but Costs declared in favor of
the club in disqualifying the horse for the race. Yet
it didn’t matter tuppence, and was a pretty shabby
thing for the club to do.
* * * *
At a recent Durban race meeting A.
Wheatley, an English jockey, rode six winners on the
one day. It was the first time any jockey had
brought this off in S’Africa since J. E. Boardman
.did it in 1903.

The latest body of sports to give McSnuffle
a push is the Maoriland Metropolitan Trotting Club.
At a meeting the other day, the profits from the
November, February and April meetings Were handed
over to the fund for sick and wounded soldiers.
Divvy, £3915, whereupon MacStiggins and Chadband
handed in 2d. and remarked that this horrible hnsi-
ness of going to races should be stopped in order to
give the Empire a chance.
T. 11. Lowry, owner of Desert Gold, lias
so far this season won slightly over £lB,OOO in
stake-money on the race-tracks of Maoriland. This
is easily the biggest amount ever bagged by an
owner thereabouts in one season. The previous best
was Stead’s £12,325 in 1901-2. Yet apart from
racing Lowry has more cash than he knows what to
do with. He inherited cash, married cash, made
cash, and then bought cash with this filly. If he
hadn’t been worth a bean Desert Gold would have
fallen down dead in her first race. That’s the luck
of the game.

“Pot Jostler. junior,” from Rockhampton:
The day after Howard English, one-time owner of
racing horses, had resigned the job of chairman of
the Central Bananaland executive, after 20
years’ battling for straight racing, the remaining
members gave a decision in an appeal which has led
to about 2457 people being tangled up in the mess
of their lives. At the Easter meeting of the
Rockhampton Jockey Club a nag named Port Ste-
phens, once owned by William Christian, of Sydney,
won the Opening Handicap, beating Glenkite, owned
by a local bookmaker. After the race the bookie
protested against Port Stephens being given the prize-
money on the ground that the winner’s nomination
fees had 'not been paid before eight o’clock on the
night nominations closed. The stewards dismissed
the protest on the grounds that the practice prevail-
. ing for many years in the Central .District was to
accept nominations without the fees being paid at
the time. Thereupon the owner of Glenkite appealed
to the C.Q.H. executive, which decided in favor of
the appellant, gave Glenkite the race, declared all
bets on the -disqualified prad off, and fined Ikey £25
for not reporting the matter before the race was
run. Then the other Bar-Vons took a hand and re-
fused to pay Glenkite, alleging that the horse had
started at a false price, because Port Stephens had
no chance of winning according to the decision of the
executive, and they have asked the Rockhampton
Club for redress. Also the Club has received a pro-
test against Glenkite being paid the prize money, as
his nomination fees had not been. lodged in time
nor the horse’s ownership stated. Further, a writ
has been thrown at the club by the owner of Port
Stephens claiming the boodle and an injunction to
restrain the Club from paying out the money to
Glenkite. In addition, another owner has written to
'the Club stating that he lost £llO backing Lazy
Mollie in the same race, and as her nomination fees
were not paid in time his bets about her should be
declared off by the same token. On inquiries being
made it was found that only one horse was properly
entered, and as he was not weighed in the tangle
became more beautiful than ever. Meanwhile fren-
zied stewards of the Jockey Club are yelling to the
executive to reconsider its decision, punters are howl-
ing for gore, and Costs is contentedly chewing his
oats. The prize is worth £35, but, up to now,
one King s Counsel, two barristers and four solicitors
have butted in with opinions and bills.

Carfax” : Talkijig about cheap horses, I
know a man who bought for £4 a nag with a hind
leg swollen like a balloon. He fired it, turned the
animal into swampy country for two years, and the
next thing that happened was that he won five
hurdle races in succession, and after two or three
byes won a couple more. Then the horse broke down
beyond hope of redemption. The owner was a pluckv
bettor and was over £3OOO ahead of it. Then he
started making an ass of himself and gave £5OO
apiece for a couple of yearlings. They went to a
big trainer. Sheol knows where the rest of the cash
and a lot more went to. The lad who trained and
rode the lumper for hbn prevailed on the local
publican to acquire an animal which had
been handed to him to break in for a liverv stable
but wouldn’t stay in its paddock. Bung ‘won the
best end of a fortune, and the trainer a big reputa-
bon and much patronage. The luck of the game’
phfin luck! 8 Per Cent judgment and 99 Per cent.

“Flat”: At Bingara (N.S.W.) the other
night Premier Holman was asked at a meeting why
horse-i acing on Sydney courses had not been cur-
-3S T ™ ngara the F hadn’t had any for two
years, most of the men and horses in the district
having gone to the war. Holman did a slide for life
b.v blaming the Federal Government. For himself
he-said, he thought racing should be lessened, but
lhi W xrdW “f e 111 whlch he couldn’t interfere with
the National Government (or words to that effect)
In his hurry to change the subject he forgot to
mention that it is the Chief Secretary of N S Wales
who issues licenses for horse and pony racin'- die
tates the number of days each club shall be allotted
and decides all matters in that connection. After that
the Treasurer collects the State’s share of the swag
and the bookmakers do the rest.

Sydney bookmakers who dabble at start-
ing-price odds on country meetings got a smack in
the Bag last week. While someone unostentatiously
led Wongabindi to the Tuggerah meeting, someone
else drifted up against McNoses and put the cash on
at starting-price. The course?neglected animal started
at 10 to 1, and the coup was something to rave over.
When last heard of, Ike was croaking
and making even ruder remarks.
* * * *
The embryonic footballer of future years
is getting his hand in nicely in Melbourne. T’other
Saturday two juvenile teams belonging to a church
association got into holts at Studley Park, and one
crowd chased the other to its travelling carriage—a
furniture van —with blue metal. The driver speeded
up, but his mokes weren’t built for speed, and pre-
sently four khaki-clad barrackers climbed aboard
and dealt out stoush enthusiastically. The enemy
dealt it out likewise; and the road was strewn with
ejected footballers. The driver was making the best
time possible on his appointed course when the
trickle of blood at his feet diverted him to the
nearest police station. Somebody had enforced an
argument with a penknife, and the Law is now
taking a mild interest in the matter.
•* *• * *
No, Maoriland Rugby Union is not having
any trouble with its banker, thank you. The Union
began the season with a surplus of £9007, built up
out of the “All-Black” tours to England and Aus-
tralia. Among its outstanding accounts is £362 due
by N.S.W. and Bananaland Unions; and considering
the blueness of the Rugby game in Australia, the
item looks good enough to remain outstanding.

“S.T.S.”: The Bulletin might fairly re-
print, in honor of parson Mullineux, just come from
the U.S.A. to M.L. to enlist (B. l/6/’l6), the jolly
verses it gave when he made a surprising first
appearance in Australia, nearly 20 years ago, as
captain of an English Rugby team. They began
(quoting from memory) :
I should put his weight about 9 stone 8,
And his height about 5 foot 2 ;
Face like the face of an eight-day clock,
Comes of a good old fighting stock—
Reverend Mullineux.
Jf the rest I .only remember the end, an ecstasy of
appreciation of the little nugget: “ Look at him
chucking big blokes about! Reverend Mullineux.”
* *■ * *
Mrs. Virginia Emily Heckenberg, mother
of champions, died in her native town, Liverpool
(N.S.W.), last week, at 72. She had 13 children,
nine of whom are alive. There are eight sons. Jim,
the eldest, held the wood-chopping championship for
15 years; then stiff joints compelled him to hand it
over to his youngest brother. All the sons are big
men, averaging 6ft. lin., and none are under 16st.
Two are in the police and three in the Garrison
Military Police, being beyond the age for active
service. Her husband, who survives her, was born
in Sydney in 1843. He is 6ft. 2in., and son of
Captain Heckenberg, a Yorkshire man, who arrived
in his vessel in Sydney in 1834 and settled here.
* * * *
‘‘Trooper Redgum”: Somewhere about
the 10th round in our scrap with Abdul we were
sent to relieve on the extreme right, and from the
hillsidc*“behind Chatham’s Post we could often see
schools of mullet playing about in the clear blue
waters of the Aegean immediately below us. Visions
of a Comino breakfast making us ignore any rules
and regulations of Abdul’s fishery board, we would
sneak down to the beach with a few bombs from the
firing-line. A few minutes later the Turk snipers in
the Balkan gunpits, 750 yards away, would grab
their rifles at the sight of a naked figure which
rushed across the narrow beach to the water’s-edge
and there stood just long enough to light a bomb
with a cigarette bumper and hurl it in amongst the
fish. Mr. Turk would just about vtake up in time to
let fly a few shots as the vision dasHied back" to the
safety of the abruptly-rising hillside. About 10
seconds later, after the bomb had exploded, two more
Chidley converts would do the goose-step across the
danger zone and make a flying dive into the water.
Then the fun would start and the Turks would have
the time of their lives pinking at whatever portion
of your anatomy was visible at the moment of diving
after the fish at the bottom. Plop! plop! plop!
w'ould land the bullets alongside you whenever you
showed your periscope to locate the nearest sartif on
which to throw your two handfuls of fish. Then more
submarine groping on the bottom until you had
recovered all the breakfasts in the locality. After
that would come the ticklish part. Dog-paddling
in as far as the water would cover you, you’d wait
until you thought Abdul’s magazine was about
empty, and then you’d grab as many fish as you
could and bolt for shelter 15 yards across the beach.
When we got amongst a school, with luck we’d get
about 30 beauties, averaging a pound apiece. When
the mullet had appointments elsewhere we’d hurl
the bomb, with a rather longer fuse, into one of the
many patches of seaweed, and we’d always score
about a dozen fair-sized black and silver bream
Always our greatest trouble was to keep a crowd of
other loafers from swooping down on our mined
area, to help us recover the booty. When the party
was select, dividends were larger and there’d be less
chance of a shrapnel coming along to wish us good-
morning. a

As there was a scrap with Darcy hing-
ing on the result of an unpleasantness between Dave
Smith and Colin Bell at Sydnej' Stadium on Satur-
day, a fair-sized wad depended on the referee’s deci-
sion. But it didn’t come to that. Smith outed the
big fellow m the fourteenth round. They made a
strenuous go of it, and Smith won because he is the
better boxer and because Bell omitted to go in and
finish him when he had the chance. Allegedlv they
Mere fighting for the heavyweight championship, and
perhaps the claim to that effect was legitimate
enough; but anyone who could get Axcited
about the big fellows with a wallop in Australia
nowadays would become exhilarated on a refrigerated
squash. °

“Buck” Crouse was given a chance at
Melbourne on Saturday to put his wallop on Mick
King. It was a weary business. Mick had the most
science, but the weight of the Yank’s broadsides
gave him a rough passage. Up till the tenth Mick
scored well by slithering out of range when Crouse
led, and then jabbing left and right to the other
cove s head. Crouse frequently found Mick’s plim-
soll with vicious right drives, and once bashed him
the 6ar > In the 10tli round his J' aws snapped
together as Crouse uppercut him twice, and thence
Mick was in Queer-street. By attaching himself to
Crouse at every opportunity he managed to evade
an absolute woodener; but he also evaded the
referee s blessing. The crowd’s language was purple.

>** * _ *
Bill Williams, who died at Neutral Bav
(Sydney) recently, was a sculler of note 40 years aeo
An Anniversary regatta introduced him to the
public in a race for men who had never won a
prize. Subsequently he became Ned Trickett’s
trainer, and was a great success in at least one
respect—he kept Ned away from his enemy.
The Californian tennis comet, McLough-
lin, is reported to have “chucked” the courts. Mac
got so tired of being walked over bv Great
Unknowns last season that he reckoned the time was
ripe to try another game. So he has taken to golf,
and already experts, who probably don’t know any-
thing, are beginning to allege that he has the mak-
ings of a great exponent of it.'
* * * •*
International sport these days seems to be
carried on only by lonely little bands of Yank
pugilists and their chattering managers. Grofcr
Upton's marine racing-machine Shamrock IV.,
which crossed the Atlantic with hopes of the
America Cup, is now coffined in a specially-built
galvanised iron shed in a Brooklyn shipyard, it
will probably sleep there till some fat 'munition-
making Yank decides to buy it and help Lipton
pay his English war taxes.
Port Adelaide no sooner got rid of the
train running along its main street to
the Outer Harbor than it asked to have it
back again. The Railways Commissioner
replied that he could not upset the new
policy ; but, on “very special occasions,” as
the rails are still there, lie might send a
train that way if the military wished it.
So the town councillors asked the Army,
and the Army asked the Commissioner, and
a departing contingent last week was fare-
welled all down the street as of old, instead
of being run to the harbor by the new and
safe back way.
The Early Pioneers’ Association, meeting
in Melbourne the other day, decided to start
a hunt for the oldest living Australian
native. Septuagenarians, octagenarians
and centenarians throughout Australia may
be expected to appear with the light of
battle in their eyes; but whether they will
all have their birth certificates in their
hands is doubtful. Without these it will
be a difficult job to pick the winnef ; and
whatever happens, the association can count
with confidence upon being called a fraud
by at least six furious centenarians,

Major Albert Holdsworth, a Melbourne
man whose name is among the latest
D.S.O.’s, has received the reward of a
patient soldier. He went early to Egypt with
the A.S.C., but only got an offshore view, of
Gallipoli. After nine dusty, dry months,
back in Egypt at his dry, dusty job, Ho Ida-
worth asked for a whack at something. 'They
sent him up to Tripoli, and he/emerged with
qualifications for hospital and the D.S.O. He
was in the Intelligence Corps before war
broke out and had a S. African medal with
a bunch of clasps. Arthur, an elder bro-
ther, has just left for parts unmentionable
as a despatch-rider attached to the latest
motor-transport division ; and two other bro-
thers have been worrying the doctors to let
them through.

W.A.
THE COMPUISORY DISTENER.
Ormond College (Melbourne Uni.) lias a
fine record. Eight military crosses have so
far been awarded to the A.1.F., and three
of these have gone to Ormond. In addition,
Mac Neil, the Rhodes scholar of 1914 and
now with the Highland Light Infantry,
placed another to the credit of his old col-
lege at Loos.
The same old muddling misuse of men
Out of 82 engineers in the* Muoriland Public
Works 32 have enlisted; but, with the exception of
nine of them who went as officers in the Tunnelling
Corps, they have all had to go as privates, while
youngsters with Naice parents and no knowledge of
handling men have been placed over them. Many
civil and electrical engineers have had to go in the
ranks simply because the Defence authorities abso-
lutely disregarded their special knowledge. A M.L.
Public Works engineer, who afterwards won fame
by building the Anzac pier under fire and using a
Turk shell as a monkey to do it, had to scramble
for his commission from the ranks. British engineer-
ing officers were sent to learn the Maorilander’s
original tricks; nevertheless this brainy soldier will
probably find himself junior to some penpusher
trained at Trentham at the next turn in his career.
When war broke out Arthur Guest, of
Parkes (N.S.W.), was working at Broken
Hill. Not having his fare for the long
journey round by Adelaide, yet badly want-
ing to enlist in his native town, young
Guest decided to tramp across the 250 miles
of mostly-sand to Cobar and take train
from there. Grit like that deserved better
luck. Guest was missed very early in the
fighting in Gallipoli, and now, no trace
being found of him, he is included with the
killed.
It is mentioned in a just-published report
by the Inter-State Commissioners that —
The importation of cork, bungs, rings, mats and
other manufactures in 1913 was valued at £98,000.
That the duty paid amounted to £29,938.
That there is a waste of imported material of 60
per cent, for which there is no profitable outlet.
That cork waste is utilised in other countries in
the manufacture of linoleum.
Nearly half a century ago Dr. von Mueller,
the German refugee who probably saw what
was coming and wanted to make Australia
self-contained, urged the cultivation of
the cork tree in this country. Now
the Inter-State Commissioners repeat what
the old-time botanist had to say, and
hope it will be given “serious con-
sideration.” But, on present appear-
ances, it will he given no consideration; the
Australian will go on corking up his bottle
of imported pill and covering his kitchen
with goods supplied by foreigners. Why
plant cork trees, anyway, when there are
plenty of other trees to burn down?
“Cleveland” records a fight to a finish,
Red Tape v. the Spiny Curse: —
The caretaker reported that prickly pear had
appeared on his reserve. Nothing was done. Seven-
teen months afterwards, when the curse had swamped
the fences, parade ground and kitchen garden, a very
important person happened along and decided that
the time was ripe. Nothing more was heard of him.
Later on the caretaker flung out a warning that the
pear was settling on the line of communications, and
that he had only seven months’ provisions on hand.
After a lapse of 27 days his letter was returned with
a remark that the date was written on the wrong
line —would he please amend it? lie wrathfully com-
plied with this, and went back to his job of keeping
the pear out of the house. By and bye a cheerful
person surveyed the curse from afar, and put in a
careful report on (1) the necessity for clearing the
pear, and (2) the approximate cost of the work. But
the Minister for Prickly Pear having gone election-
eering the matter slithered until a muffled cry came
through from the caretaker. The Minister at once
got busy, demanded another report, and finally
notified that the work might be done; whereupon the
cheerful person wrote back that (1) he would re-
quire more money, as the pear had grabbed more
territory during the argument, (2) that half the
pear was of the variety that the cochineal insect 'feeds
on, and (3) he could get some of the insect locally
for 2d. A cataract of correspondence ensued, and a
further lapse of many months gave the pear breath-
ing time, When the extra money was voted the
cheerful person reported that he would not require
this amount, as there was less pear to deal with, some
fool having taken it upon himself to liberate cochin,
eal insects in the early part'of the argument. He
also mentioned casually that the caretaker had re-
tired. An awful silence followed, during which the
pear, advancing from ail sides, took complete control
of the reserve.
“T. 0.1.”: Apropos church bells (B.
11/5/’Ri). About the year 1807 the King
presented a peal of bells to old St. Philip's
Church. Sydney. There were eight of them,
and they hung in the round tower. One by one
they were removed by persons in authority
allegedly for various public purposes.
John Campbell, who gave a peal to the new
St. Philip’s, on condition that they were
chimed on the anniversary of his birth, gave
one of the old bells to St. Jude’s at Rand-
wick. where it now hangs. A second was on
board the old lightship Bramble at the Sow
and Pigs reef, and is now in the Harbor
Trust Office. Where are the other six? The
bell which hung in the belfry at The Rangers,
the old Mosman home of Oswald Bloxsome
and Henry Stuart Russell, now hangs at St.
Peter’s. Neutral Bay. The ancient bell
which used to “clear” old George-street
Markets from 1842 til! their demolition, is
at Woolloomooloo Fish Markets. W hen the
material of the old markets was sold the
bell was the onlv item reserved. Another
Australian peal of note is in St. Patrick’s.
Melbourne. These were founded in 1852 by
Murpliy. of “the Liberty,” Dublin. I went
with mv father, then on the Commercial
Journal, to inspect and report upon tin* gift.
Each had in raised letters the name of the
church and “Melbourne, South Australia.
The peal arrived in 1853 and-lay in the
porch of St. Francis’s, Melbourne, until hung
in St. Patrick’s in 1868,

A note from Buenos Aires:—
Buenos Aires is full of derelicts and dead-beats
from every land under the sun. The other morning,
fresh off the boat that was carrying me from Maori-
land, I connected with one. “Good-mornin’, sorr,”
he said. “Do yell spake English? But av coorse
yeh do! No, no, sorr; hould off a minute an’ let
me shpake w No! Oi don’t want anny job, an’ Oi’m
not goin’ to tell yeh that Oi haven’t aiten annything
for the lasht tin days. Mahn, dear, Oi haven’t
enough appetoite to dale wid a divilled shnoipe this
blessid minute. Oi’m afther spindin’ the noight an’
a few nickels wid a frind below at the docks, an’, be
all the goats in Oireland (an’ 'that same is a loively
oath), I have a,tongue on me loike .the lid av Hell
this day, an’ it’s a dhrink I want- -n dhrink wid a
boite to id loike ky-ann pepper or the tongue av a
British Consul (Hell to the sow] of thim!). For
the love av Moike, sorr, wan dhrink, jusht wan
shmall noggin to save the life av- me.” The appeal
hit me where I lived, and I weakened and handed
him 50 cents, and told him to go into the buffet
and buy a good stiff whisky. “A whisky at 50
cints a . toime is id! Ah, thin, sorr, divil a thing
would I like betther; but thim days is pasht for the
loike av me. But there’s an Oytalian doive round
the corner where I can get foive or six canas for
the same money, an’—savin' yer prisince, sorr—Oi’ll
be the dhrunkest an’ happiest mahn in Boness Arees
befoie tin o clock, plaze God.” I never saw him
again, but I’m sure he did his best.
Melbourne’s current mystery surrounds a
party on a train returning to town from a
race-meeting. To pass the time a little game
ot hazards was suggested, and the Coroner
was subsequently told by some of the
players that one man had, at Sydenham,
unaccountably left the carriage and stepped
along the footboard towards the engine
That was the last they saw of him. But
this doesn’t explain the fact that when his
body was found on the line, there was money
in his severed hand and loaded dice in his
pocket, while coins bestrewed the track
further back. The police are to inquire
further, with probably no useful result,
except to hint to persons inclined that way
that even in select circles it isn’t healthy to
play with loaded bones.
They told me you had good manners
A helping hand to beginners
Some people tell awful crammers
It is the way of all sinners.
I sent you a piece on Anzac land
Did it ever come to your hand
Or was it past out of command
And looked on as dirty black sand.
Your sarcastic old Bulletin
Once a week only circulatin’
Is run bv a crowd of old women
Too knock-kneed for any fightin’.
If you see a suit of khaki
And a kind and loving face
It’s a fighting soldier
Of Australia’s daring race.
To the fields of storm and rage
Amid the falling snow
They leave their happy homes
Because they have to go.
To fight for their freedom
And Australia’s fame
They haves fought and fell
And made, Australia’s name.
In battles they have fought
And showed their spirit to
In the lives of Gallipoli
Like them of soldiers true.

Put all your eggs in one basket
and then watch that basket.
(Carnegie.)
Many people can do several things fairly well, but nobody can do many
things superlatively well.
This is what Michelin recognises, and it is why WC concentrate
the whole cf our attention upon the manufacture of pneumatic
tyres only.
Michelin
are superlatively good tyres,
because we have put all our eggs in one basket, and do not spread our
efforts over the manufacture of various articles like g If balls, rubber
shoes, rubber hose pipes, hot-water bags, etc. etc.
All the brains of our chemists and engineers are concentrated upon
one task —the task of making the Michelin Tyre the best tyre in the world.
If you are not a user of Michelin Tyres, try them!
Wc don’t fear comparisons ; wc solicit them.
Michelin Tyres and Accessories can be
obtained from all the Leading Garages.

“Nerves”

like nerves rad ate in all di- |
rections throughout the body.
So long as the nerve centres
are capable of continually
supplying nourishment to the
nerves, the nerves will remain
strong and healthy. But
directly the nerve centres be-
c me weakened by overwork,
I worry or anxiety, they are un-
I able to transmit the necessary
| nourishment, and the nerves
b ;come worn out and on
edge.” Then it is that a sud-

IN BANANA LAND
E. J. Stevens, who retires from the chair-
manship of directors of Brisbane Courier
and associated papers, took the helm in the
troublous times following ’93. whqp every-
thing in Bananaland was in the melting-pot
and even the future of the Courier was un-
certain. He not only pulled the paper
through, but leaves it one among the most
profitable dozen in Australia. Although
he quits the chair of the Courier, he is still
trustee for the most important shareholders.
“Itan”: Weather-propheting has quite a
number of apostles in Bananaland. The
usual madness is to issue predictions for
the whole season ; then, when the weather
during a certain number of weeks happens
to fit in with the forecast, the fulfilment
of the prophecy is boosted in the
papers. Of course, not a word is
said about the other 999 tips that
don’t come off. The impostors are certain to
hit it sometime and somewhere in the State.
For instance, this writer reckoned this was
going to be a month of prosperity and
drought. Here you are: the sugar “districts
are about to gather a record crop, while no
finer maize areas have been seen at Atherton ;
but one big spot in the South-West and
another in the Central-West are as dry as a
Methodist conference.
From a travelling correspondent:-
Tropical Australia should take off its simhat to
Mrs. John Hill, of Glen Boughton, near Cairns. At
the Cairns and district show this lady produced a
single-handed exhibit running into hundreds of items,
ranging from soaps and candles to crystallised fruits.
Seven varieties of marmadale, conserves of grena-
<l ;lla, pawpaw and vegetable-marrow,- candied peel
of four varieties, IS kinds of confectionery, pre-
served’ ginger, fruit butter, orange spice, ground
coffee, extract of coffee, nine sorts of pickles, vinegar,
six different sauces and chutnevs, dried fruits, banana
flour, prepared arrowroot, bottled fruit, preserved
vegetables, and an extensive selection of home-made
wines, ciders, effervescent drinks and liqueurs were
included in the production of this 011 c woman from
one North Queensland farm. The spare time left on
her hands she dedicates to housework and a con-
siderable share in the management of a large dairy
farm. It would pay tropical Australia to engage
such a woman as travelling demonstrator in the
art of living comfortably at small cost from one's
own resources. The Northern housewife has
become so accustomed to getting everything
eatable and drinkable out of a tin or a bottle
that she has come to regard these as the natural
envelopes of all food materials. Mrs. Hill at a
thousand a year would be an infinitely better invest-
ment-towards the permanent settlement of the semi-
Tropics than a roomful of agricultural lecturers,
tobacco experts or horse-doctors.
.Tames Carden Collins, who answered the
bell-bird's call *1 Yeppoon a few days ago,
was one of. Bananaland’s oldest cattle men.
llis dad was a navy captain, who brought
the infant Jimmy to Australia in 1829.
Young Collins hit out for himself in Bauaua-
land in 1842, and has been going strong
there ever since. lie gradually got together
Tambourine and Maroon stations, and later
on added properties at Baffle Creek and
Torilla. For a spell he was cattle selector
for the Central Queensland Meat Export
Co. Incidentally Jimmy Collins was a top-
notch horseman, skilful with his lists, and
had a memory that wouldn’t let go.
In its hurry to become a fishmonger, the
Ryan Government has given itself a rotten
advertisement for loyalty to the land that
pays its screw. This is how Acting-Premier
Theodore put the matter to a metal-trades
deputation which stirred him up for propos-
ing to go outside Australia for a trawler: —
The Government wants to put a trawler into com-
mission as early as possible, and has been endeavor-
ina' to purchase one overseas, thus far without
success.
If Theodore and other bad Australians con-
tinue to rake up excuses for going outside
the Commonwealth whenever they have work
to do, how long do they think it will he before
Australia will be able to do anything in a
hurry? Theodore has a lot of luck that he
didn’t have a mother who acted towards him
when ho was an infant as Theodore is acting
towards Australian industry in its infancy.

John Hop, of Queensland, is a very
cheerful person these days. Unionism has
been good to Robert in his brief experience
of it. It gave him the eight-hour day to
begin with; now it has persuaded the Home
See. to _ insure that the eight shall be
worked in a lump instead of in two shifts
of four. As a further concession the Large
1' eet of the North will have alternate Sun-
days free.
Inspector Kelly, one of the best-known of
Bananaland’s police officers, has signed his
last charge-sheet. He was on sLx months’
leave at the time of passing out, and was
putting it in at Stanthorpe. Kelly knew
the State better than most men, having pur-
sued the law-breaker from the boulevards of
Cloncurry to the wilds of Brisbane, via
Mackay, Mount Morgan, Charters Towers,
Childers and Townsville.
“Viking” is torn with doubts: —
After reading of the many methods of converting
prickly-pear into gold—-from paint to potash, from
paper to printers’ ink—l’m wondering why
the blessing is still referred to as a curse.
At odd times I have also seen prickly-pear
advocated as good horse and cattle feed, and heard
that pigs fattened on pear fruit are equal to
corn-fed. If it is such a valuable asset why the
feverish desire to wipe the blessing out? I also
want to know why Bananaland’s rural population
still heaves curses at it, when they have hut to take
up a 500-acre pear selection and retire on the
wealth. Is it that the papers conspire to pull my
leg?
“Viking”: Naturally the agitation to
bottle up Bung has spread to Bananaland,
and extensive escapes of gas are threatened.
If the Ryan Government wants to prove that
it is more serious in its job than the Labor
Ministries of the other States, it might con-
sult that portion of its platform which
reads: “Nationalisation of the liquor
traffic,” etc. That wasn’t secretly shoved on
the platform a fortnight ago, either; it has
been there for upwards of a quarter of a
century. Isn’t there one Labor Govern-
ment in Australia with a good enough
opinion of the Labor policy to even ask the
people whether they want it?
For 18 years Rockhampton has had a
permanent branch of the Supreme Court;
but business has been so poor that most of
the time the resident Judge has had to look
to Brisbane Courts for a job. The Govern-
ment now proposes to put the shutters up,
and Central Bauanalanders take it very
quietly. Yet once the mere mention of such
a thing would have set all the Separationists
threatening Brisbane with every curse right
up to civil war.
Wafted to Beinga recently, Mrs. Tareha,
sen., of the Ngati-Kahungurus, one of the
best-known chieftainesses in Maoriland, and
an old amazon who saw war from the firing
line. She fought against the Haushaus at
Omaranui and was trying to get a bullet into
Te Ivooti when Karauria was killed.
Whereupon she personally carried that
stricken chief a mile over broken country to
a place of safety—a real Gallipoli perform-
ance.

My Disinfectant Dear.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
I remember, well remember, the cold corner near the
pail;
Where 1 used to wait and listen for your footsteps in
the dark,
For the quick, caressing greeting and the kiss on
firm lips set ;
And the smell of disinfectant I can never quite
forget.
From the house of pain and healing, when your long
day's work was done,
You would laughing come to meet me, when there
was no Other One ;
And 1 loved you—loved you —loved you ! — s to dis-
traction —to despair,
While the smell of disinfectant lingered subtly in
your hair.
You were, full of “my profession,’’ and your patients
were my foes ;
When you petted them and pitied, all the savage in
me rose;
When, involved in our embraces, we "took chances in
the park,
Something moral (disinfected!) hung about 11s in the
dark.
Dear, your two eyes danced with mischief, and for
months I couldn’t rest
If you were not there beside me with your head upon
my breast.
How 1 loaded you with flowers as the seasons came
and went!
lint the good old disinfectant beat in turn each
tender scent.
We escaped each other, Helen! I was far too poor
to wed,
And you shelved me, like a darling, for some other
beau instead.
1 m afraid I quite forget you (time is moving on
so fast!),
Till I smell some disinfectant -sacred fragrance o
the Past!

George Barriskill, of the C.S.R. Co., has
been killed in action in France. “Bam”
was a popular Grammar School boy, and a
good sport. He was of the Bundaber?
Barriskills.

Toowoomba Chronicle, and a nephew of

Mrs. Bob Bell was the lady soloist at the
Brisbane Apollo Club concert last Tuesday,
ilrs. Bell sang from a gold brocaded crepe
de chine frock, and collected enough vege-
tables to stock a shop. Cecil Houghton
should also have sung, but he was at home
nursing a sore throat; so veteran Joe
Bottger and the khakied Magness Fisher
were appointed to the vacancy.

du Tambour Major,” Joe Bottger, Robert
Wish art, Lance-Corporal Magness Fisher

aged Billjims.
011 Friday the Church of England Sol-
diers’ Help people raised over £360 for
their Soldiers’ Club. They called it Laven-
der Day, and all the helpers wore some
of the old-fashioned color, and sold the frag-
rance fresh plucked or shrouded in muslin
sachets.
Pretty Vera Atkinson was married to
W. P. Watts at the Thompson Estate
Church last Wednesday. The moon is shin-
ing in the North.
Gone to burrow under the Hun, James
Loekie W ilson, of Calliope and Galliungal
stations, and until recently owner of Raglan
station, Dungog (N.S.W.). He contributed
heavily to the patriotic funds, but when his
junior partner and 11 employees at Calliope
and dozens of others in the district com-
menced to clink the sixpenny-pieces in their
spurs, he handed his 25-h.p. car to the Red
Cross and fell in with the Mining Corps. lie
carried three bars on his arm when he left.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the well-known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the* less ’‘treatment” you give
the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
Photo: Wrather
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any-
thing inferior to wash the hair with. Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it will look bright and
glossy.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little —what do you call
it? —stimulant, and for this I
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick. Give your
hair a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.
Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. rais
simple treatment is then repeated a re"
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.

Will Dyson lias opened another exhibition
of war cartoons in London, showing the
originals of his Land and W ater and
Daily Sketch cartoons. The Kaemaekers
boom has not affected the Australian in
the least, the Daily Sketch having brought
him a new public. Mrs. Will (Ruby Lind-
say) is now chief cartoonist on T.P.’S
Weekly.
Geraldine Farrar, the Yankee soprano
whose favorite press gag for some time was
that she had come to an unalterable deter-
mination never to marry, has succumbed to
a Dutch mummer, Lou Tellegen. The lady
used to say: “One can be a splendid woman
and a great artist, but not a great artist and
a mother.” Hers is evidently a case in which
hope has triumphed over inexperience.
The Era, London’s oldest theatrical
weekly, has changed hands, brewer Sir
William Bass having got tired of his jour-
nalistic toy. When “Tipperary” had
moulted its head and tail, what was left
was the Era. Feldman, the publisher of
the tremendous early wartime boom, made
enough money out of it and a few other
things to finance himself and the Era’s old
editor into the paper.
The first lot of limestone to be used as
flux for the Broken Hill Prop.’s Newcastle
steelworks was shipped from Tasmania the
other day, when 2000 tons were shot into
the steamer Odlancf in three hours. That
was at Devenport. The stone is tipped into
an 8000-ton bin, with 50 shoots about 6ft.
apart, which feed it into the ship’s holds.
Two inventive Maorilanders have solved
another problem—the soldering of alu-
minium. They got a company behind them
and w T ere sent to London, where they obtained
an almost indisputable patent, as well as
getting the glad hand from scientific and
commercial men. The War Office asked
for a demonstration at the \\ oolwich
arsenal, and was so satisfied that it grabbed
the patent and started to make tests on its
own account.
“Qwertyuiop”: The late W. Shakspeare
must have been rather a busy man. He
was in the play writing game for about
20 years, and wrote 40 plays. That means
a “Hamlet” or a “Richard III.” every six
months. The late Sophocles was also
fully employed. He kept the theatrical
managers going for 62 years, and is believed
to have composed 119 plays—again one
masterpiece every six months. That also
was the rate of the comparatively-late
Moliere’s production. Ibsen, on the other
hand, took two years apiece for his various
glooms.
“Exile” from somewhere in the Pacific: —
There is a hyphenated German-American-Jew on
board returning home with a bag full of Australian
orders from wholesale warehousemen for softgoods.
His lists tot up 600,000 dollars (say, £120,000),
and a good deal of it is for slop clothing to be
made in Chicago sweat shops. If one commercial
traveller on one trip can sell so much Amurrikun
stuff, what will Australia look like when the glad
news becomes known to all the other makers of
ready-made clothing, boots and hats in the U.S.A. ?
Nothing but a minimum tariff on softgoods of 75
p.c. will prevent that one hundred millions of
Amurrikuns working three shifts a day to send us
everything we use, from feeding bottles to brass-
bound caskets for the final act. The Union S.S. Co.
has 12 cargo boats busy at present bringing goods
from San Francisco to Australia; and they’d be
better engaged carrying Australian wheat to
Europe. We can't get freight for our exports with
which to pay our debts, but there are ships in
plenty fetching wearing apparel we ought to be
making ourselves.
“J.F.H.” i A curiosity of the Inky Way
is a t little paper, Free Belgium, which is
published right under the nose of Yon Biss-
ing. the German Governor of the Desolated
Kingdom. Von Bissing has been moving
Heaven, earth and the other place in frantic
efforts to discover the writer, printer and
circulator of the accursed thing, but all in
vain. It has no appointed dates of appear-
ance, but Von Biss, and all the principal
officials of the invading Huns promptly re-
ceive copies when it does appear. Each
number —37 have been issued to date—opens
with a defiant letter to Biss.; then follow
letters from the Front, selections from British
and French journals, cutting gibes, galling
witticisms, and funny stories at the expense
of the Bodies in general and the Governor in
particular.
“F.H.R.”; For 20 years I followed min-
ing in N. S. Wales and was not aware until
five years back that a holder of a miner’s
right could take up two acres of land on
any mining reserve or temporary common
for residential purposes. In many in-
stances these holdings can be converted into
special leases or residential areas of from
20 to 40 acres. I have at present a 10-acre
block held on a 15-year lease at an annual
rental of half a crown an acre. Half of the
area is good cultivation, with an unfailing
water supply. On this patch I grow pota-
toes, turnips, cabbages, cucumbers, toma-
toes, melons, squash, marrow, peas, beans,
beet, rhubarb, lettuce, cherries, quinces,
peaches, nectarines, apples, plums, pears,
grapes, cows, babies, a pig or two, and fowls,
and honey is in the bush for the taking.
The only time a woman cannot look after
a small place like this is at ploughing and
sowing time —say a fortnight a year. Yet
people are herded in towns and growling
about the cost of the steak and loaf. Let
the bush toiler, or a lot of him that can get
a pocket-handkerchief farm, shake himself
to see if he is awake and grasp the oppor-
tunities that lie to hand.
According to a review of the world’s
wheat-production, compiled bv the Victorian
Department of Agriculture, Australia i£
1910 came seventh on the world’s list, with
lio million bushels, and garnered more than
the estimated harvest of Germany and Aus-
tria combined. The world’s crop was a
record, and so was that of Australia; and
the Department of Agriculture reckons that
next season the position “will be in favor of
the producer.” Which means that the Aus-
tralian farmer, if he works as hard at seed-
ing this year as he did last, will get a
bigger reward than the one that is verv
gradually coming to him through the Gov-
ernment.

Is the best medicine known lor the treatment of croup.

her than to listen to the choking cough and watch the little one gasping for breath.
But there is no need for alarm when she uses Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.
The great danger of croup is the formation of a false membrane in
the throat. Until this is expelled the spasmodic choking continues.
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy given in frequent doses loosens this
membrane and causes the child to expel it by vomiting.
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is an absolutely safe and certain
remedy for croup and will save the unnecessary expense of a doctor’s
visit. If given as soon as the child becomes hoarse or even after the
croupv cough appears it will prevent the attack.
Experienced mothers know that for the tieatment of croup in* an\

The silly lawsuit threatened between the
Premier of S. Aus. and 111 s brother Jim, the
Attorney-General, on one side, and Opposi-
tion-leader Peake on the other, lias fizzled
out. It got as far as the lawyers, but not
into court. Peake assured the Vaughans that
he had never made or intended to make any
charge of disloyalty, or even of sympathy
with Germans, and they accepted the assur-
ance. It was a storm-in-a-thimble; still it
took lust a year to blow over.
Dead in Maoriland, at 79, Kimble Bent,
renegade soldier, rebel, outlaw, whose life
was a romance of extraordinary adventure.
’Way back at the beginning of things Bent,
a private in the 57th Regiment, having been
flogged for insubordination, deserted to the
enemy and became a Maori in everything
except actual breed. He was held as a kind
of prisoner for several years, for he knew
how to make powder and the Maori didn’t
want to lose him. But he wasn’t hard to
keep, because the pakeha was offering a
good solid lump of cash for his head. So
Bent lived a savage life, mostly in the wild
country around the head waters of the Wan-
ganui River and in the hinterland of
Taranaki. After many years he was par-
doned and emerged from cover, a hardy old
warrior whom exposure and long association
with another race had so changed that only
the practised eye could identify him. He
talked little and few people knew him, but
at various times he told most of his story
to James Cowan, and it forms one of the
most trustworthy records extant of life
among the Maoris during the time they were
fighting against the paleface. He never
tried to be a pakeha any more. Of late
years he had been living at a hapu in Marl-
borough. forgotten, outcast.

The successful

are skilled in turn-

Just set Big Ben and turn in

on the dot Big Ben’s bell sounds
a lively call, that gets you out
of bed, feeling fit, fully rested
by a night of refreshing, un-
worried sleep.
Big Ben is seven inches tall,
well built, handsome, punctual.
He has a big, easily read dial—a
cheerful, deep toned voice—large com-
fortable winding keys—a light running
movement with watch-like escapement

minutes or intermittently for ten.

The Inky Way.
“Soho”: Apropos the boasting of Mel-
bourne Herald and Weekly Times about
their long-distance employees, the Age can
show a decent record. Old “Geordie” Mit-
chell will be in the Syme service “60 years
come Ist January next, please Gawd” —40
years on night work in the publishing room.
He hag refused to take a pension, and often
challenges the office youngsters to a 100
yards sprint. He still refers to the present
head of the firm as “Master Herbert,” and
recalls the times in the ’6o’s when he
(Geordie) and his brother could carry the
whole daily issue on their shoulders. In
the Collins-street printery there are 12
hands with service ranging from 49 to 40
years; 47 from 30 to 40 years, and of mere
youngsters with over 25 years’ yakker
behind them there are 36.
This is what the Mullumbimby Star
(N.S.W.) thinks of the matter: —
The soul, mind and conscience of the editor of this
paper is held at small value by at least one in the
district. One subscriber values it at half a crown
a quarter. Because we refuse to be dictated to as to
who should be employed on the staff he has stopped
his subscription. Let it be understood, we only sell
the paper and not the staff for the half a crown; the
sale of human flesh and blood was stopped years ago
(or supposed to be), we may explain to this archaic
and paltry-minded soul.
It recalls a long-defunct Richmond River
weekly which every two months or so made
vague references to a change of ownership
in order to keep alive the subs. of cocky
readers whose susceptibilities might have
been wounded by some par. or other.
C. J. Joyce, who has driven a pen through
the Hobart Daily Post for some and
has worried along with the thankless job of
secretary of the Tasmanian branch of the
A.J.A., is moving to a warmer climate. He
lias been the Post’s sporting expert, and is
going to a similar job at Broken Hill.
A SAUSAGE FANTASY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was lying tired and fretful, with that feeling most
regretful
Which beclouds the poorest pressman when he's far
away from home—•
Home, of course, it is the singing and the clatter
and the ringing
Of the presses; ’tis his lodestone wheresoever he may
roam.
Be he married, be he single, yet his veins most
quickly tingle
To his inky vampire lover, who will never let him
go.
In disdain, or rage, or sorrow, he may leave her —
till to-morrow ;
For her kiss is on his fingers and he thrills within
its glow.
Well, upon these few reflections came to me swift
recollections
Of some work I felt was urgent and necessitous and
due;
But the subjects they were dimful, though my brain
was teeming, brimful
Of politics and gossipings and scoops—well, not a
few.
Now, I don’t know how you fellows when you start
to ply the bellows
Figure out the scheme of structure of your current
litany ;
But, in my own concerning, when I feel the topic
yearning,
I must work on from the star-line till I reach
finality.
Whether ’twas that I was ailing, or my well of
thought was failing,
Is of little mark or moment since remains the con-
crete fact
That there came to me the rotten, the infernal, ill-
begotten
Line that could not be evaded by nor forcefulnes.;
nor tact.
Like the raven damned and dreary that made Edgar
sad and weary,
It alighted on my thought-rail, and it shunted out of
me
Every other phrase and heading, and I raved upon
the bedding:
“Life is like a sunlit sausage on a cayenne pepper
tree!”
Now, I ask you, on the level, did the thing come
from the Devil ?
For it seems to me it never could have birth in god
or man.
And no matter how I fretted I was absolutely netted,
I was sweltering in trouble Worse than Broken Hill
gives Cann.
“Damn!” I said, “I'll write on Trading, showing up
the German’s raiding £
Of the markets of Australia and I’ll let Frank Tudor
seo” —
Then again there came the humming as of imps of
Sheol thrumming:
“Life is like a sunlit sausage on a cayenne pepper
tree 1”
Quick I hobbled to the bottle and I raised it to my
throttle,
Gulping down a half a gallon of raw glycerine and
gin;
Then I banged upon the table and I roared out,
“Now I'm able
To throw down this flaming sentence ; now on work
I will begin 1”
So I started: “It is rumored that electors will be
humored
With another Royal Commission on the Northern
Territ’ry.
This recalls the words we uttered” —here I dropped
the pen and muttered:
“Life is like a sunlit sausage on a cayenne pepper
tree 1”
By this time the phrase unravelled had me fair and
squarely gravelled,
There was nothing but to chuck it and roll out to
Callan Park,
And remain there for a season till I gathered up my
reason
And induced the loonies’ doctor to relight its vital
spark.
But before I pass from vision, in a spirit of derision
I am risen from my sick bed and I’m off upon the
spree
To obliterate and sever, to forget for now and ever,
Life is like a sunlit sausage on a cayenne pepper
tree.
N.S.W.

Song of the Singing Brothers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FOOLS as we ever have been and fools
as we must remain,
We hearten ourselves with visions
and off on the track again ;
The pleasures we build in fancy are false
as the pleasures we knew,
And we who are Singing Brothers are kin
to the Wandering Jew.
The Further for ever lures us; yet, in
spite of the dearth in store,
We have known what is left behind us, and
the Known is a paid-off score;
The swag of our life is heavy, but we
hump it for ill or well,
With each haven we make for. Heaven;
each haven w T e gain to, Ilell.
There are happy folk by the wayside and
they welcome us to their mirth,
But we join them as watchers only, making
lonelier still our dearth;
Though we lilt them a dancing measure and
slug them a careless strain,
Within is a wistful sorrow —we must off on
the track again !
Not with a God-speed spoken and a lift of
our swag on the road,
For ours is a lonely tramping and no one
can share our load;
Unseen, as the revel waxes, with Joy at its
greatest height.
Unseen, and ourselves observing, we take
to the track at night.
By night and alone in silence, with only
the stars* above,
We deny us the things we long for, Beauty
and Joy and Love.
Not with a weak self-pity, not with a cheap
self-pride,
But because of an impulse in us wiiose
bidding won’t be denied.
At the end. maybe, is a Quiet, or maybe a
newer Quest;
Take it, my brothers, take it—whatever
there be is best!
But I. for the tangle beats me, I would
sue for one favor of God,
Before I would make Him obeisance or bow
to His awful nod.
I would say to the Great All-Father: “I
am here and a rebel soul;
But what is the game Thou art playing,
and what is the final goal?”
It may be that He’d dismiss me with a
wave of His mighty hand.
But somehow I’ve got a feeling that He’d
tell me and... .understand !
JIAORILAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
A Day in a Troopship.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
dismal sound of “Reveille” drones
I drearily out upon the still, morning
air. Its listless, erratic notes bespeak
a newly-aroused bugler. He is no more
than a disturber of the starry peace. _ Over-
head and round about a vast indigo-
tinted concave displays its myriads .of
scintillating specks; a brilliant setting
planet casts a forked reflection across the
dark, undulating waters. To the east t e
creamy twilight signals the birth of anothei
tropical day. The balmy stillness is en-
hanced by the engine’s subdued rhythm the
propeller’s throb, and the soothing, rippling
wash from the vessel’s bows.
The distressing bugle wail rouses one sue-
denly from the mystic depths of a heavenly
reverie to the work-a-day life of a troopship,
whose bleary-eyed passengers—-going forth
to do battle for the honor of their eounti ,\
and various private reasons stretch them-
selves moodily and growl curses at then
rude awakener. But the wash-deck fat-
igues” are on the warpath, and theie is
no time for idle reflection. Already gurg-
ling streams of water are cascading down
the bridge and boat-deck ladders, and along
flooded waterways the brass-grated scupper -
holes choke and guzzle as though m a tip-
pling ecstasy. The deck sleepers, in scan J
night attire—an airy singlet or less VT se V
below from the on-coming deluge blankets
and palliasses trailing in their wake, me
pulsing hoses pursue them, vomiting the se-
and sweeping away the signs of last nig -
revelry and idle hours. Dead marines, p 1
knockings, countless matches, oiange P pe
ings, ancient periodicals, cake crumbs,
tracts, a sock or two —all float swiftly past
on their way to the sea that swallows
everything. . , ,
Then comes—impromptu, with no bugie
overture —the great undress parade. -
dormitories belch out files of nude v a •
of various shapes who march gamely an
gaily beneath the douching hose, me sun
is now coyly peeping out, but the nursing-
sisters are still abed. So the amazing
riors wear their identification discs with an
On tliev come, all tan and muscle the
squatter, 'the chemist, the piano-tuner the
plumber, and hundreds more, from every
calling on the list. All are brothers now m
the common cause. The ex-stoker g < •
hose from the dentist and pays off an old
score on the minister of religion, ‘
panied by shrieks of mirth from the g
and the tram-conductor, who shortly B et it
in the neck themselves and are reduced to
helpless gasps and splutters. _ , ,
As soon as the scanty working-rig
tropics has been donned, companies fall in
on their respective parade grounds and g
through physical drill. “Arm stretclnn
hexercise!” shouts out the seige.
the South African ribbon. Arms hupwau.
stretch. Two! ’Ands on the , lp ® ~' a ? ®,
on. “Commence! One— two! tine-two.
lias yer were.” The men put much bee
into their work. They possess stiong m .
cular arms, fine expansive cheats . and
sturdy, well-developed legs—a goodly crowd,
tanned and convict-shorn, volunteers in the
pink of condition. Imoking at then
forget the printed rubbish about t
dent Australians.
The crowned and starred elect . ~
parading on the boat deck. A itli the aid
of a blackboard the officer eommandjng
hereinafter known as “Q.C.”— imparts to
his officers the elementary rules of survey-
ing and the intricacies of the prismatic
compass, by which rough and ready instruc-
tion they may intelligently lead their men
in a given direction or pick up an ordered
position. It is practical and interesting
stuff.
Suddenly the full-throated bugles blare
out the familiar and well-loved invitation to
the cookhouse door, and a noisy fuss
begins. The hungry soldiers pass the word
fore and aft, and the sea-birds wheel at the
sudden clamor for “Mess-orderlies! Mess-
orderlies !”
These legionaries, who have put an edge
on first-class appetites with undress parade
and physical contortions, are impatient cus-
tomers. Breakfast is eaten in relays, after
which, stripped to their waists, the “fatigues”
proceed to clear up.
The buglers, now amply fortified and in
good wind, rasp out an inspiriting call,
Companies with their respective officer's
double along the decks and fall in for in-
spection. “Two bells” strikes. The adju-
tant steps forward and in his deep official
voice orders: “Companies.... off hats!”
Hundreds of close-cropped heads suddenly
gleam pinkly in the sunlight. The ranks
sway gently together to the easy roll of the
ship. The padre takes up his post. The
adjutant salutes and reports all correct to
the 0.C., and the “carry-on” nod having
been received the men are ordered to stand at
ease. Before the ranks of bowed heads the
padre then lifts up his voice in thanks to
the Almighty for bringing them safely to
the beginning of another day, and prays for
comfort and strength in this their time of
trial: he prays also for the King, for
comrades-in-arms in both the Army and the
Navy, and finally he commends to “Thy
Fatherly keeping those dear ones from whom
we are now separated.” A touching little
service—impressive because of its simplicity
and environment.
At “dismiss,” unit commanders take
charge of their companies and put in a
strenuous forenoon. A squad here and there
at bayonet exercise holds the eye. “Guard !
—Lunge ! —Best!” The cold steel glints
dazzlingly in the sun. Some day soon those
splendid men will try it on living enemies.
A company at physical drill has a corner.
Further along is a lecture on scouting, and
close by a first-aid class is learning about
the compression of arteries. An epidemic
which broke out at the outset of the voyage
occupies the “off duties” attention of
everyone. It is a kind of St. Vitus’s dance
known by the simple appellation “Morse,”
and is played with arms, flags, semaphores,
lamps—anything and everything.
In a guilty file the defaulters breast up to
“orderly room” and have justice done to
them. Others, on sick parade, gather round
a neighboring bar. where they are served
with depressing drinks. Now comes an
event of considerable moment. An orderly,
coming smartly to attention, salutes and
informs the ship’s commander that it is
“10.30 a.m., sir.” The commander must
know this already, seeing that he has two
chronometers, one clock, and a watch in his
cabin. But one must not quit the firmly-
drawn line of routine. Both men firoceed to
the precincts of the orderly room, where a
number of “brass hats” are assembled. All
instantly start saluting at top speed. lhe
official courtesy being concluded, the ship s
sergeant-major salutes on his own account :
“For’ard, sir?” to the commander. “Yes,
replies the latter, “carry on.” (Note—
When in doubt try “Carry on” ; it’s a win-
ner every time!) The inspection of the ship
has now commenced. The sergeant-majoi
leads the way through a maze of soldiery,
veiling like fury as he marches along—
“Party—’shun—shun there —don t yer ear.
—ripes out!—party —orficers’ inspection—
Get up, there —’shun —’ere! you on the sky-
light, there—’SllUN!” A fine clerk of
the course is the sergeant-major. He forms
the head of the processional snake, which
comprises the ship’s commander, 0.C., ad-
jutant, r.M.0., ship’s chief-officer, military
quartermaster, orderly-officer of the nay,
orderly-sergeant, and last, but not least, the
tail of this imposing wriggling thing ter-
minates in an ex-Venetian blind-maker,
officially rated as sanitary-sergeant. Com-
panies on parade stand fast as the
“brass hats” proceed, those off duty—lolling
about reading or playing draughts—scramble
to their feet as the M.C. approaches. 1 os-
sibly this man was in the throes of writing
home an exaggeration of his “private life y
possibly the sultry day had produced a cer-
tain lassitude ; possibly he was a ‘ conscien-
tious objector.” Whoever he was the O.C.
overheard the unfortunate remark: S pose
they’ll be wanting us to stand up to our
blankv food next.” The cortege halts in-
stantly. “Crime that man, sergeant-major,
shouts the O.C. Then there is a warning
mutter, “Orderly room ter-morrer morning
All right. Carry on, sir.” The pro-
cession then dives down ladders to the dor-
mitories away along the decks, twisting
and turning itself around amongst bunks-
in and out of recesses; down more ladders,
along more decks, poking into every nook
and corner, the electric torches revealing
the furtive fluff to the chagrin of the dormi-
tory-sergeant. .
\ trooper, belli ted in bis work, is tevei-
ishly scrubbing a ladder at the far end
of the deck, beads of sweat on his brow,
singlet saturated. He stands to attention
as the officers pass. There ■is congestion
round the sculleries and wash-houses. The
orderly-officer’s face presents a broad grin.
“What’s the joke, Meldrum? asks the
adjutant. Orderly-officer sniggers and points
down the dormitory ladder. “See old Thomp-
son scrubbing the steps there? Veil, just
as we had passed up he brought out one ot
bis typical growls. *No, siree,’ be sohlo-
qtiised. ‘Never again! No ! not on your
]if e n o! Ao# even if my blanky backyard
was full of white feathers!’ ”
“He’s that squatter chap, isn t he !
ouerics the adjutant.
“Yes, worth over twenty thousand.

If any illustration appearing in T-£
Bulletin takes your fancy, write to. the
Manager, who will quote you price for it. :
of the day reluctantly exercises his great
privilege of shouting canteen bubbly-water
to the “brass hats.”
An interval and the bugles are yelling
again about the cookhouse door —which is
promptly besieged. A scene of heated
encounters is “Galley’s Post.” The files of
orderlies soon emerge one by one from the
not, and struggle along the deck under
weighty, steaming mess-tins and overflowing
chcksies. The concoctions of hours vanish
m a few clamorous minutes.
This being Saturday, the afternoon is
devoted to sport. The first item happens to
be a demonstration by a school of porpoises
which suddenly appears frolicing in the bow
waves. The ship lists as the men make an
eager rush to the side to view the display.
Irooper Sheepdip, from Back o’ Sunset,
yells above the din of voices: “Strike me
pink! See them flyin’ fishes?” Whereupon
Corporal Counter jumper sneers: “Cut it
out, mate, them’s porcupines!” By that
time the sea steeplechasers are gone. Other
items include spar pillow fighting, lazy stick
and the usual potato races and grab-apple.
Most of the events—not forgetting “Are
you there, Murphy?”—bring more happi-
ness to the spectators than to the competi-
tors. Boxing is naturally the most popular
with all hands. Puttee the Plugger and the
Werribee Wobbler, stripped to their waists
(bar identification discs), do their best to
look like Les Darcy and Dave Smith. The
second round ends dramatically enough
when, through some mischance or other, the
Wobbler gets one beauty on the “boko.” The
packed house applauds generously while he
staggers with a gory nose back to his corner
soap-box. The seconds work over him glee-
fully with towel and sponge. “Time !” Poor
old doomed Werribee staggers gamely to his
feet, his receding chin quivering strangely.
He fumbles stupidly with his disc in an
endeavor to get it off over his head. He
must be entirely unhampered for his forlorn
hope.
"No, no!” shouts the referee-sergeant,
suddenly rushing up and staying his hand.
“Leave it on, matey,” he urges; and with a
grim wink to the crowd lie adds: “Might be
required yet—yer never know !” One more
clout on the nose and Werribee is being
sympathetically assisted to his box.
The programme closes with a series of
terrific triceps-swelling tugs-o’-war, and the
men eventually tumble down to their last
meal worked up to a feverish state of sport-
ing enthusiasm, and the mess rings uproari-
ously.
Once more the cheerful bugles sound :
“Officers’ wives eat puddings and pies.”
Officers’ mess this time, and there is a wild
rush to be in time for grace, for dinner con-
stitutes a parade. A joyful atmosphere per-
vades the officers’ mess. The O.C. frivo-
lously chaffs a nursing-sister about her “boy”
in the Isolation Hospital, and the P.M.0.,
who has just defeated the quoits champion
adjutant, rubs his hands in kittenish delight.
The rivals denounce each other’s flukes in
crushing repartee. Two junior surgeons—
contrary to all regulations—have smuggled
io a bottle of wine for “Sweethearts - and
Wives.” The pop of the cork, however, gives
the show away and their cunning fails igno-
miniously, the “pretty-haired boy” being
unanimously condemned for the fatal attrac-
tion he thinks he has for the flimsy sex.
He seems glad when everyone’s attention is
attracted by shouting at the far table, where
the short-sighted Q.M. has narrowly escaped
swallowing a cockroach !
Suddenly—a blare from bugles outside.
In a flash the happy mess springs to atten-
tion. Gaiety is transformed to gravity—
and there is not a smile, not a sound save
the haunting strains of the “Retreat.”
Throughout the ship every man stands
rigidly. It is the solemn passing of day to
night, the memorial to the fallen —
the prayer for victory. Simultaneously
the ensign slowly descends from the peak.
So dinner draws to a close and glasses are
charged. The “president” calls upon “Mr.
Vice.” The mess rises once more. “Ladies
and Gentlemen. ‘The King.’ ” The toast
m none the less sincere for being drunk in
limejuice.
Meanwhile Private Dauber, leaning over
tht rail, has been feasting his eyes on the
exquisite ever-changing coloring of a glori-
ous sunset. In a voice tinged with emotion
he remarks: “What superb grandeur, lads!
I reckon that's the loveliest sight we’ve
seen since we left home.”
“Get out!” sneers Sapper Goodfeed.
“Ever had a squint at the orficers’ mess?”
The Saturday night sing-song has its
fleeting fling. The orchestral selections
range from the “William Tell” overture to
“Hold Your Hand Out, Naughty Boy!”
and there are some good singerb as well as
a few indifferent reciters.. But the head-
liner is the artist who can render a soul-
reaching domestic ballad of “When yer
gits back ’ome at night, after a good old
boose. About the middle of a raucous-
\ciced patriotic ailment: “Oh, we don't want
ter lose but we think yer oughter gaw”
—the watchful buglers blow in “The Last
Post” as a sort of obligato. The artists
and the patient audience then rise and
hoarsely bawl the National Anthem, and
after a few minutes’ breather, the dreary
bugles mournfully blow their copper-blasted
day to a merciful release—“ Lights Out!”
CATHEAD.
Venus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE quaintest feud I have met with
was between Ambrose Bandy and
McKewan’s cow Venus. Venus was
a lady of pedigree ; she had won medals for
it, but presumed not at all. To other people
she was just a plain, amiable blonde who
show r ed her taste only in the superfine quality
of her set of fur, and betrayed her breeding
to the close observer merely in her reserved
manner and the carriage of her head and the
elegance of her legs and tail; but to Ambrose
Bandy she was splenetic and rash, an ill-
tempered and contumacious brute.
Ambrose Bandy, it must be admitted, de-
served all he got. He was a hatter, with
a selection of 210 acres adjoining
McKewan’s farm at Twin Hills. What
had persuaded Bandy to go on the land—
and such land —nobody knew, but everybody
gave a guess. A few favored the idea that
he had been ruthlessly crossed in love, with
so sudden a jerk that his faculties had
slipped off the hinge. Others said he had
murdered a sergeant of police, and was in
hiding from the people who wished ro present
him with a testimonial. The majority fav-
ored the idea (hat he was avoiding the
society of two or more wives.
Whatever else he was he was the greatest
waster on Twin Hills. Yet everybody
called him “Sir,” and he was the only man
the tradespeople honored with the tag “Esq.”
in the course of their essays in polite letter-
writing begging an early settlement of the
enclosed. He made no effort to induce his
section to blossom like the rose or flourish
as the green bay tree, but put in his time
for the greater part being gratuitously inso-
lent to the wise, temperate inhabitants, and
bumptiously overbearing to the otherwise,
who assembled in Chaffey’s bar to pass the
long evenings experimenting in hydraulics
and studying specific gravity.
But more especially Mr. Ambrose Bandy
employed himself in antagonising McKewan’s
cow. He would stand for a full hour talk-
ing to her in a low, hissing, irritat-
ing tone, and his remarks were in-
variably most insulting. He called that cow
everything. He was never loud or aggressive,
but he was tireless ; his pale-blue watery eye
fairly sizzled with scorn, and there was a
biting edge to every word he spoke.
At first Venus treated him with the un-
concern peculiar to cows, but after he had
been going 40 minutes or so she would shake
her head as if worried by gnats, and turn to
avoid the incessant drone. But Bandy was
not to be shaken off; he had set himself to
break down that cow’s reserve, and to betray
her into an unseemly display. And eventu-
ally he succeeded.
One afternoon, when he had been going
for a little over an hour, Venus threw off all
restraint and went at Ambrose with a
bellow of rage. Ambrose started seeking a
fence, and fell over it, two feet ahead of the
avenger. He seemed highly delighted, and
continued his tactics. McKewan told him
he was souring the cow, and threatened to
beat him unmercifully if he persisted in
being an annoyance to the pride of the
dairy. Despite all that, followed by
McKewan’s assaults and two court cases,
Ambrose continued his vindictive whisper-
mgs,.and the time came when Venus grew
positively ropable at the mere sight of the
fellow.
To those who know the domestic bovine as
a creature wholly unsusceptible to verbal in-
sult, this may sound ridiculous. But Venus
was a cow of lineage, more emotional, more
highly strung than most cows. And the
man’s persistence is not to be forgotten.
One night something hit Bandy’s bark hut
like an old Roman catapult, and went
straight through it. The hut was gored to
ruins, and in the morning Bandy was found
i. "'l arc ‘ s nearer creek, shockingly mis-
He died at noon, but before passing he
whispered to McKewan : “It was that infer-
, c °w- Then he chuckled with bitter
i ee ‘ •, I „y, oke her U P at last. I woke her!”
ae said. I d made up my mind to move her.
tone was the living image of my wife !”
Then Ambrose died happy.

rpHE off-shore wind was cool and sweet
§ A full moon lit the sea;
